The Necessary Universal Proposition: Definition and Refutation of a Misconception
Author refutes the claim that all universals are necessary and defines the necessary universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the necessary universal proposition and distinguish it from non-necessary universals using the condition 'as long as the essence exists'.

Core Idea
A necessary universal holds as long as the subject's essence exists, not just when it is called.

In this passage, the author corrects a common error: thinking that all universal propositions are necessary. He then provides a precise definition of the necessary universal, explaining that it means every actual individual of the subject possesses the predicate as long as the subject's essence exists. This definition is crucial for understanding modal logic in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Some people claim that a proposition is not universal unless it is necessary. Find this claim in the Arabic.
The author calls this a mistake.
2
Find the author's refutation
The author argues that there are universal propositions where the predication is not always true, showing a misunderstanding of necessity.
Look for 'وذلك غلط' and the subsequent explanation.
3
Extract the definition of the necessary universal
The author defines 'Every A is B necessarily' as: every actual individual of A is B, not just when it is called A, but as long as the essence of A exists.
Pay attention to 'ما دام ذات ما يطلق عليه أنه أ موجود'.
4
Understand the condition 'as long as the essence exists'
This condition distinguishes necessary universals from conditional ones. The predicate holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence, whether always or at some time.
Contrast with conditional necessity where predication holds only at the time of description.
5
Note the author's comment on different expressions
The author mentions that different scholars (like the Sheikh) have phrased the definition similarly, emphasizing the actual description under conditions.
This shows consensus on the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد زعم قوم أن القضية لا تكون كلية إلا وأن تكون ضرورية: وذلك غلط فإنا نجد كليات تكون للحمل في كل واحد من الموضوع لا دائمًا كما مرَّ فلا نلتفت إلى ذلك. فإن ذلك من سُوء فهمهم المراد بالضرورة. والآن يلزمنا أن نذكر الكلية الضرورية فنقول : إن قولنا كل «أ» «ب» بالضرورة معناه أن كل واحد واحد مما هو «أ بالفعل سواء كان في الحال أو دائما أو وقتاً ما فهو «ب» لا عندما يطلق عليه أنه «أ» ولا وقتا ما كذلك بل ما دام ذات ما يطلق عليه أنه «أ» موجود إن كان دائمًا فدائمًا وإلا فلا كما مر في بيان الضرورة المطلقة من قبل. والحق في هذا البحث أنه يفتقر إلى تحرير المبحث وهو الكلي الضروري بأنه ما هو وكيف هو، فإنه إذا كان عبارة عما هو المعتقد عند الخصم كان كما اعتقده لا محالة لكن العقل شاهد بأنه لا يكون عبارة عن ذلك بل يكون عبارة عما ذكرناه، وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. ثم الألفاظ مختلفة في بيان معنى قولنا كل «أ» «ب» بالضرورة ، فالشيخ قال معناه: «إن كل واحد واحد مما يوصف بالفعل على الشرائط المذكورة إنه «أ» فذلك موصوف بأنه
And some people have claimed that a proposition is not universal unless it is necessary: and that is a mistake, for we find universal propositions where the predication in every one of the subject is not always, as has passed, so we do not pay attention to that. For that comes from their poor understanding of what is meant by necessity. And now it is incumbent upon us to mention the necessary universal, so we say: Our saying 'Every A is B necessarily' means that every single thing that is actually A, whether it be in the present or always or at some time, is B; not when it is called A, nor at some time likewise, but as long as the essence of that which is called A exists; if it is always then always, otherwise not, as has passed in the explanation of absolute necessity before. And the truth in this investigation is that it needs a precise treatment of the topic, namely the necessary universal, what it is and how it is, for if it were an expression of what the opponent believes, it would be as he believes necessarily, but reason witnesses that it is not an expression of that, but rather an expression of what we have mentioned, and that is known by reflection. Then the expressions differ in explaining the meaning of our saying 'Every A is B necessarily'; the Sheikh said its meaning: 'Every single thing that is actually described under the mentioned conditions as being A, that is described as being
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage defines the necessary universal proposition. The author first refutes a mistaken belief that every universal proposition must be necessary, pointing out that there are universals where the predication is not always true, showing a misunderstanding of necessity. He then defines 'Every A is B necessarily' as meaning that every actual individual of A is B as long as the essence of A exists, whether always or only at some time. This is contrasted with conditional necessity. The author emphasizes that precise investigation is needed to determine what the necessary universal really is, noting that it is not mere belief but what reason dictates. Finally, he mentions that different expressions exist for this definition, citing the Sheikh (likely Ibn Sina or Aristotle) who says that every actual individual fulfilling the conditions is described as B.

Key Terms
الكلي الضروري (al-kullī al-ḍarūrī): The universal necessary proposition, meaning that every actual individual of the subject is B as long as the essence of the subject exists. [Note: No significant shift.]
الضرورة المطلقة (al-ḍarūra al-muṭlaqa): Absolute necessity, as opposed to conditional necessity, referring to the necessity that holds as long as the essence exists. [Note: This term is consistent with other modal logic texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'كل أ ب بالضرورة' mean?
A) Every A is B when it is called A.
B) Every actual A is B as long as the essence of A exists.
C) Most A are B necessarily.
D) A is necessarily B at some time.

Key Takeaway
The necessary universal is defined by the condition 'as long as the essence exists', not mere universal quantification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Every believer is righteous.' Is it a necessary universal? Explain using the author's definition. (Hint: Does the predicate hold as long as the essence of 'believer' exists?)
?
Find an example of a universal proposition that is not necessary, such as 'Every student is studying.' How does it fail the condition of the necessary universal?

The Scope of the General Absolute Proposition
Author clarifies that 'absolute' does not include possible propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general absolute proposition and identify which types of propositions it includes.

Core Idea
Absolute propositions only cover actual judgments, not merely possible ones.

In this passage, the author refines our understanding of the absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma). The phrase 'absolutely such' might suggest that all modes, including possibility, are included equally. However, the author warns that this is a misinterpretation: the absolute proposition is restricted to judgments that are actualized, not merely potential. This distinction is crucial when analyzing modal propositions in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Read the first sentence: 'The general absolute proposition gives the impression that it includes the possible as it includes others.' The author then denies this. Your task is to extract the exact claim being rejected and the correction.
2
Understand the distinction between actual and potential
The author says the absolute proposition concerns 'what contains a judgment actually occurring' (بالفعل) not 'what contains a judgment that only occurs potentially' (بالقوة). This is the core differentia. Note that a possible proposition might be considered under the absolute only if it contains an actual judgment at some time.
3
Parse the additional remarks about rulings
The author then states that propositions have general rulings (as propositions) and more specific rulings when predication is permanent or not. This connects the absolute to other modal types. See how this supports the earlier clarification.
The 'First Teaching' refers to Aristotle's Organon, indicating that this interpretation is traditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المطلقة العامة كقولنا إن كذا كذا بالإطلاق العام يوهم أنها تعم الممكنة كما تعم غيرها من القضايا المذكورة : وليس كذلك فإنها من حيث هي هي متناولة لما يكون مشتملا على حكم يحصل بالفعل لا ما يكون مشتملا على حكم لم يحصل إلا بالقوة لكن يعرض لها أعني الممكنة ولغيرها لما أنها تقابل المطلقة بحسب ما فيها من الزوائد وإن لم يدخل الكل تحتها. ولتعلم أن للقضية من حيث هي القضية أحكامًا، ومن حيث هي قضية يوجد فيها الحمل دائمًا أو لا دائما أحكام أخص. والأمثلة في التعليم الأول تدل على أن المطلقة بحسب الاصطلاح الأول هي العامية.
Then the general absolute proposition, like our saying 'such and such is absolutely such,' gives the impression that it includes the possible just as it includes other mentioned propositions. But it is not so, because it in itself concerns what contains a judgment actually occurring, not what contains a judgment that only occurs potentially. However, it may apply to the possible and others because they correspond to the absolute in terms of the additions in them, even if not all are included under it. Know that a proposition has rulings insofar as it is a proposition, and more specific rulings insofar as it is a proposition in which predication occurs always or not always. The examples in the First Teaching show that the absolute according to the first convention is the general one.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scope of absolute (mutlaqa) propositions, particularly the general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-‘amma). The author warns that the phrase 'absolutely such' might be misinterpreted as including possible propositions (al-mumkina) in the same way it includes other types like necessary and eternal. That is not correct: the absolute proposition concerns a judgment that is actual, not potential. However, a possible proposition can still be considered under the absolute if it contains an actual judgment at a particular time. The author then notes that a proposition has general rulings as a proposition (e.g., truth conditions) and more specific rulings when considering whether predication is permanent or not. The reference to 'the First Teaching' (al-ta‘lim al-awwal) likely indicates Aristotle's Organon, where the absolute is understood as the general sense.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
الممكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Possible judgments
B) Actual judgments
C) Necessary judgments
D) Eternal judgments

Key Takeaway
The general absolute proposition is limited to actual judgments; possible propositions are only included if they involve an actual judgment.

?

Refutation of Necessity Requirement for Universals
Refutes the necessity condition and defines necessary universal with comment on Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that all universal propositions are necessary and define a necessary universal proposition.

Core Idea
Not all universals are necessary; necessity requires the predicate to hold as long as the essence exists.

Some logicians incorrectly equate universality with necessity. The author provides counterexamples and clarifies the true meaning of necessary universal. This lesson dissects that error and the precise definition, including a note on Ibn Sina's formulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false claim
Read the first sentence: 'Some people claim that a proposition is universal only if it is necessary.' This is the claim being refuted.
The author calls it a mistake (غلط).
2
Recognize the counterexample
The author mentions universals where predication holds for each individual but not always. Think of 'Every human is walking' – true for each person at some times, but not always.
This shows universality does not entail necessity.
3
Understand the diagnosis
The error stems from 'poor understanding of the meaning of necessity.' Necessity is not mere universal application; it involves duration of essence.
The author will define necessary universal next.
4
Define necessary universal
'Every A is B necessarily' means: whatever is actually A (now, always, or at some time) is B as long as the essence of A exists. If the essence is permanent, B holds always; otherwise only for that duration.
This definition contrasts with the mistaken view.
5
Analyze Ibn Sina's wording
Ibn Sina said: 'Every single thing that is actually described as A is B.' This might imply the subject is other than A (the described vs. the description). The author clarifies that they can be the same entity (e.g., a human is both subject and described as human).
This is a subtle point about identity in predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد زعم قوم أن القضية لا تكون كلية إلا وأن تكون ضرورية: وذلك غلط فإنا نجد كليات تكون للحمل في كل واحد من الموضوع لا دائمًا كما مرَّ فلا نلتفت إلى ذلك. فإن ذلك من سُوء فهمهم المراد بالضرورة. والآن يلزمنا أن نذكر الكلية الضرورية فنقول : إن قولنا كل «أ» «ب» بالضرورة معناه أن كل واحد واحد مما هو «أ بالفعل سواء كان في الحال أو دائما أو وقتاً ما فهو «ب» لا عندما يطلق عليه أنه «أ» ولا وقتا ما كذلك بل ما دام ذات ما يطلق عليه أنه «أ» موجود إن كان دائمًا فدائمًا وإلا فلا كما مر في بيان الضرورة المطلقة من قبل. والحق في هذا البحث أنه يفتقر إلى تحرير المبحث وهو الكلي الضروري بأنه ما هو وكيف هو، فإنه إذا كان عبارة عما هو المعتقد عند الخصم كان كما اعتقده لا محالة لكن العقل شاهد بأنه لا يكون عبارة عن ذلك بل يكون عبارة عن ما ذكرناه، وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. ثم الألفاظ مختلفة في بيان معنى قولنا كل «أ» «ب» بالضرورة ، فالشيخ قال معناه: «إن كل واحد واحد مما يوصف بالفعل على الشرائط المذكورة إنه «أ» فذلك موصوف بأنه «ب»»، وإنه من جملة ما يوهم أن الموضوع في هذه القضية غير الألف. فإن ما يوصف بالفعل إنه «أ» فهو غيره لما أن الموصوف غير الصفة لكنه لا يلزم أن يكون غير الصفة بل يمكن أن يكون عن الصفة نحو الموصوف بأنه إنسان مثلا : فاعتبر بما عرفت.
Some people claim that a proposition is universal only if it is necessary. That is a mistake, for we find universals where the predication holds for each individual subject but not always, as has passed. So we pay no attention to that. That is due to their poor understanding of the meaning of necessity. Now we must mention the necessary universal, and we say: Our saying 'Every A is B necessarily' means that every single thing that is actually A, whether now or always or at some time, is B — not when it is called A nor at some time likewise, but as long as the essence that is called A exists; if always then always, otherwise not, as has passed in the explanation of absolute necessity. The truth in this investigation is that it requires a refinement of the topic: what the necessary universal is and how it is. For if it were an expression of what the opponent believes, it would be as he believes inevitably, but reason witnesses that it is not an expression of that but rather an expression of what we mentioned, and that is known by reflection. Then the expressions differ in explaining the meaning of our saying 'Every A is B necessarily.' The Shaykh [Ibn Sina] said its meaning: 'Every single thing that is actually described, under the mentioned conditions, that it is A, is described as B.' This is among the things that give the impression that the subject in this proposition is other than the A. For what is actually described as A is other than it because the described is other than the attribute, but it does not necessarily follow that it is other than the attribute; rather it can be from the attribute, like the described as human, for example. So take what you have learned.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a critical response to a false claim and a definition of the necessary universal proposition. The author first refutes the view that every universal proposition must be necessary, giving the counterexample of non-necessary universals (like 'every human is walking' — true sometimes but not always). Then he defines the necessary universal: 'Every A is B necessarily' means that whatever is actually A (whether always, temporarily, or in a moment) is B as long as the essence of A exists, not merely when the term 'A' is applied. This definition is contrasted with the mistaken understanding. The author then discusses Ibn Sina's (the Shaykh) formulation from al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat, which states 'every single thing that is actually described as A is B.' He points out that this wording might wrongly suggest that the subject of the proposition is something other than A itself, but he clarifies that the description (e.g., being human) is not necessarily distinct from the thing described — the same entity can be both the subject and the attribute. This is a nuanced point about identity in predication.

Key Terms
الكلية الضرورية
الشيخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It uses the wrong variable
B) It may imply the subject is different from A
C) It says 'actually' which is unnecessary
D) It requires the subject to exist always

Key Takeaway
A universal proposition is not necessarily necessary; necessity requires the predicate to hold for the subject as long as its essence exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Provide an example of a non-necessary universal proposition from Islamic law (e.g., 'Every traveler may shorten prayer') and explain why it is not necessary according to the definition (the predicate holds only under the condition of travel, not as long as the essence of the traveler exists).
?
Task 2: Rewrite Ibn Sina's definition ('every single thing that is actually described as A is B') in your own words to avoid implying that the subject is other than A. Use an Islamic example (e.g., 'Every actually existing human is mortal').

The Three Grades of Imkan: General, Special, Most Special
Author defines three grades of possibility and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-imkan al-‘amm, al-imkan al-khass, and al-imkan al-akhass and explain their definitions as given by the author.

Core Idea
The three grades of possibility form a hierarchy based on how much necessity they exclude.

Modal logic distinguishes degrees of possibility. In this lesson, we unpack the author's precise definitions of the three types of imkan: general (al-‘amm), special (al-khass), and most special (al-akhass). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly interpreting modal propositions in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Locate the definitions of al-imkan al-‘amm, al-imkan al-khass, and al-imkan al-akhass in the Arabic text. Note the key phrases that define each.
2
Understand the relationship
The author says al-imkan al-‘amm is not simply the negation of necessity of non-existence, but what accompanies that negation and includes necessity and special possibility. What does that mean for the scope of each type?
3
Examine the objection
The author addresses an objection that this definition would make the necessary composite. How does the author refute it?
4
Distinguish al-imkan al-akhass
This type is defined as the negation of necessity from every aspect. Why is it considered the most deserving of the name 'possibility'?
5
Apply the definitions
Given examples like 'Every fire is hot' and 'Every gold is meltable', classify them according to the three grades.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيء يلازم نفسه وإنه هو سلب الضرورة لا غير؛ غير أنه لا يكون كذلك فإن الإمكان العام لا يكون عبارة عن سلب ضرورة العدم من حيث هو السلب بل هو عبارة عما يلازم هذا السلب وغيره أعني الوجوب والإمكان الخاص. ولا يقال لو كان كما ذكرتم لكان الواجب متركبًا منه ومن غيره فإنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء لازما للشيء أن يكون وجوديًّا فضلا عن أن يكون داخلا في ماهيته. وقد يقال ممكن ويفهم منه معنى ثالث وهو سلب الضرورة من كل وجه وهذا هو الإمكان الأخص. وإنه أحق باسم الإمكان لخلوه عما ينافيه وهو الضرورة فتكون مقابلتها بحسب الذات والوصف والوقت وغير ذلك. ثم لقائل أن يقول إذا كان هذا الإمكان أخص بالنسبة إلى الأول والثاني كانت الاعتبارات أربعة: واجب، وممتنع وممكن وخاص؛ وهو الذي يمكن أن يكون ضروريا بوجه ما، وممكن أخص؛ وهو الذي لا يمكن أن يكون ضروريًا بوجه ما البتة. لكنا نقول يمكن أن تكون الاعتبارات خمسة كذلك لأن ما له ضرورة ما في جانب الوجود غير ما له ضرورة ما في جانب العدم؛ غير أنه جعلهما قسمًا واحدًا لعدم المنافاة بينهما على خلاف الواجب والممتنع. ثم الضرورة كما أنها متعددة بحسب الذات والوصف والوقت فكذلك رفع الضرورة فإن رفع الضرورة بحسب الذات غير رفع الضرورة بحسب الوصف؛ فالممكنة قد تكون مطلقة حينئذ، وقد تكون مشروطة، وقد تكون عرفية، وقد تكون وقتية، وقد تكون منتشرة على ما عرف فلو قلت ممكن بالإمكان العام فمعناه أنه لا يمتنع وجوده كما في قولنا «كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام، فإنه لا يمتنع كونها حارة والإمكان عدمه بالضرورة؛ ولا ضرورة فيه، وما لا يمتنع وجوده فلا يخلو من أن يمتنع عدمه وهو الواجب، أو لا يمتنع وهو الممكن الخاص. فالممكنة العامة هي التي حكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة عن جانبها المخالف؛ وإنها هي الأعم بالنسبة إلى ما عداها من القضايا كلا وجملة. والخاصة هي التي حكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة عن جانبي الموافق والمخالف كما في قولنا كل ذهب ذائب بالإمكان الخاص وإنها هي الأعم بالنسبة إلى المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة والوقتية والمنتشرة والوجودية اللا دائمة والممكنة بالإمكان الأخص أيضًا. فإنه يمكن أن يكون فيها الضرورة بوجه ما على خلاف الأخص فإنه لا يمكن فيها البتة كما في قولنا «كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الأخص»، فالخاص بالنسبة إلى العام هو الأخص، وبالنسبة إلى الأخص هو الأعم.
The thing accompanies itself, and it is the negation of necessity only; but it is not like that, for the general possibility is not an expression of the negation of the necessity of non-existence insofar as it is negation, but rather it is an expression of what accompanies this negation and other than it, namely necessity and special possibility. And it is not said: if it were as you mentioned, then the necessary would be composed of it and other than it; for it does not follow from a thing being concomitant with a thing that it is existent, let alone that it enters into its quiddity. And it is said 'possible' and a third meaning is understood from it, which is the negation of necessity from every aspect, and this is the most special possibility. And it is more deserving of the name 'possibility' because it is free from what contradicts it, namely necessity, so its contrast is according to essence, description, time, and other than that. Then a speaker may say: if this possibility is more special relative to the first and second, then the considerations are four: necessary, impossible, special possible (which can be necessary in some respect), and more special possible (which cannot be necessary in any respect at all). But we say: the considerations can be five as well, because that which has some necessity in the side of existence is different from that which has some necessity in the side of non-existence; except that he made them one division due to the absence of incompatibility between them, unlike the necessary and the impossible. Then just as necessity is multiple according to essence, description, and time, so likewise the removal of necessity: for the removal of necessity according to essence is different from the removal of necessity according to description. So the possible [proposition] may be absolute then, or conditional, or conventional, or temporal, or distributed as known. So if you say 'possible with general possibility', its meaning is that its existence is not impossible, as in our saying 'Every fire is hot with general possibility', for it is not impossible for it to be hot, and its non-existence is not necessary; and there is no necessity in it, and that whose existence is not impossible does not fall short of either its non-existence being impossible—that is the necessary—or not impossible—that is the special possible. So the general possible [proposition] is that in which it is judged that necessity is removed from its opposite side; and it is the most general relative to all other propositions collectively. And the special [possible] is that in which it is judged that necessity is removed from both the agreeing and opposing sides, as in our saying 'Every gold is meltable with special possibility', and it is the most general relative to the special conditional, the special conventional, the temporal, the distributed, the non-permanent existential, and also the possible with the most special possibility. For it is possible that there is necessity in some respect, unlike the most special, for which it is not possible at all, as in our saying 'Every human is a writer with most special possibility'. So the special relative to the general is the more special, and relative to the most special is the more general.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional exposition on the different types of possibility (imkan) in modal logic. It begins by correcting a misconception: the 'general possibility' (al-imkan al-‘amm) is not simply the negation of the necessity of non-existence; rather, it is something that accompanies that negation and includes both necessity (wujub) and special possibility (al-imkan al-khass). The author then addresses an objection that this would entail the necessary being composite, but dismisses it on the grounds that a concomitant need not be existent or part of the quiddity. A third meaning is introduced: 'most special possibility' (al-imkan al-akhass), which is the negation of necessity from every aspect and is the most deserving of the name 'possibility' because it is free from any necessity. This leads to a debate about the number of modal categories: an interlocutor suggests that with this third meaning, there are four categories (necessary, impossible, special possible, most special possible), but the author argues for five by distinguishing between those with necessity in the side of existence versus those with necessity in the side of non-existence, though the author concedes they can be grouped together. The passage then explains that just as necessity can be multiple (by essence, description, time), so too the removal of necessity, generating different types of possible propositions: absolute, conditional, conventional, temporal, distributed. It defines general possible as the judgment that necessity is removed from the opposite side (e.g., 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' means its non-existence is not necessary), and special possible as the judgment that necessity is removed from both sides (e.g., 'Every gold is meltable with special possibility'). The relations between these are spelled out: the special is more general than the most special but more special than the general.

The three grades of possibility—general, special, and most special—differ by how much necessity they exclude: the general includes even necessity, the special excludes necessity from both sides, and the most special excludes necessity entirely.

Types of Possible Propositions and the Multiplicity of Necessity
Author explains the multiplicity of necessity and types of possible propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different types of possible propositions (absolute, conditional, conventional, temporal, distributed) and explain how the removal of necessity varies according to essence, description, and time.

Core Idea
Possible propositions vary based on the kind of necessity that is negated.

The passage goes on to explain that just as necessity can be multiple, so too the negation of necessity, giving rise to different types of possible propositions. This lesson unpacks the distinctions among absolute, conditional, conventional, temporal, and distributed possible propositions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the multiplicity of necessity
According to the author, necessity can be by essence, description, or time. Give examples of each from the text.
2
Understand the corresponding removal
The removal of necessity also varies: removal by essence, by description, by time. How does that generate different types of possible propositions?
3
List the types of possible propositions
Identify the five types mentioned: absolute, conditional, conventional, temporal, distributed. Use the examples from the text.
4
Distinguish general possible from special possible propositions
General possible removes necessity only from the opposite side; special possible removes from both sides. How does this relate to the types?
5
Apply the classification
Given a proposition like 'Every fire is hot with general possibility', determine what type of necessity is removed and why it is general.

The thing accompanies itself, and it is the negation of necessity only; but it is not like that, for the general possibility is not an expression of the negation of the necessity of non-existence insofar as it is negation, but rather it is an expression of what accompanies this negation and other than it, namely necessity and special possibility. And it is not said: if it were as you mentioned, then the necessary would be composed of it and other than it; for it does not follow from a thing being concomitant with a thing that it is existent, let alone that it enters into its quiddity. And it is said 'possible' and a third meaning is understood from it, which is the negation of necessity from every aspect, and this is the most special possibility. And it is more deserving of the name 'possibility' because it is free from what contradicts it, namely necessity, so its contrast is according to essence, description, time, and other than that. Then a speaker may say: if this possibility is more special relative to the first and second, then the considerations are four: necessary, impossible, special possible (which can be necessary in some respect), and more special possible (which cannot be necessary in any respect at all). But we say: the considerations can be five as well, because that which has some necessity in the side of existence is different from that which has some necessity in the side of non-existence; except that he made them one division due to the absence of incompatibility between them, unlike the necessary and the impossible. Then just as necessity is multiple according to essence, description, and time, so likewise the removal of necessity: for the removal of necessity according to essence is different from the removal of necessity according to description. So the possible [proposition] may be absolute then, or conditional, or conventional, or temporal, or distributed as known. So if you say 'possible with general possibility', its meaning is that its existence is not impossible, as in our saying 'Every fire is hot with general possibility', for it is not impossible for it to be hot, and its non-existence is not necessary; and there is no necessity in it, and that whose existence is not impossible does not fall short of either its non-existence being impossible—that is the necessary—or not impossible—that is the special possible. Fourth Meaning of Imkan: Future Possibility
Author introduces a fourth sense of 'possible' based on future time without present existential implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the fourth meaning of 'possible' (based on future time) from the other meanings, and explain why present existence does not affect future possibility.

Core Idea
Future possibility is independent of present existence.

In this passage, the author introduces a fourth sense of 'imkan' that is purely temporal. Unlike the earlier senses that considered necessity in relation to essence or description, this sense looks only at future times. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when discussing modal claims about future events in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term is 'imkan' in this fourth sense. Note that it is a meaning of the word 'possible' (mumkin). The author is giving a semantic definition.
2
Extract the defining condition
The condition is that the thing is not necessary at any future time. It is not about present existence or non-existence. The passage says: 'if it is not necessary in every time of the assumed future times, then it is possible.'
3
Contrast with other meanings
How does this differ from essential possibility? Essential possibility is neither necessary nor impossible in itself. This one is about temporal contingency. The fourth meaning is conditional on future times, not on the essence.
4
Analyze the author's refutation
The author refutes the potential objection that present existence would make it necessary. He uses a reductio: if present existence implied necessity, then present non-existence would imply impossibility, which is rejected by consensus. So present existence does not affect future possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال ممكن ويفهم منه معنى آخر وهو أن لا يكون الالتفات في الاعتبار بحسب حال من أحوال الوجود من إيجاب أو سلب بل بحسب الاستقبال. فإذا لم يكن ضروريًا في كل وقت من الأوقات المفروضة في المستقبل فهو ممكن وإنه لا يكون مشروطًا بشرط أن لا يكون موجودًا في الحال كما هو عند البعض؛ إذ الوجود في الحال لا يخرجه إلى ضرورة الوجود وإلا لكان العدم في الحال يخرجه إلى ضرورة العدم كذلك؛ وذلك لا يخرجه بالاتفاق.
And it is said 'possible' and another meaning is understood from it: that the consideration is not based on any state of existence, affirmative or negative, but rather based on the future. So if it is not necessary in every time of the assumed future times, then it is possible, and it is not conditional upon the condition that it does not exist in the present, as it is with some; for existence in the present does not make it a necessary existence, otherwise non-existence in the present would make it a necessary non-existence likewise; and that does not make it necessary by consensus.

Commentary
This passage introduces a fourth semantic sense of the term 'possible' (mumkin). Unlike the previous definitions that consider necessity in relation to the thing's essence or description, this sense considers time: a thing is possible if its existence is not necessary at any future time. Importantly, this possibility does not require that the thing not exist at present. The author refutes an objection that present existence would entail necessity of existence, arguing that if that were so, present non-existence would entail necessity of non-existence, which is not accepted. Thus, this fourth meaning is independent of present states and purely future-oriented.

Modalities of Predication vs. Quantifier
Author distinguishes modalities of predication vs. quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between modalities of predication (jihaat al-haml) and modalities of quantifier (jihaat al-sawr) in a modal proposition.

Core Idea
Modalities can qualify either the predication itself or the scope of the quantifier—they are not the same.

You already know the basic elements of a proposition: subject, predicate, quantifier. Now the author introduces a critical nuance: when we attach a modality like 'necessarily' or 'possibly', we must ask—does it modify the predication (the 'is' relation) or does it modify the quantifier (the 'all' or 'some')? This distinction is foundational for understanding modal logic in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the modality in the Arabic example
In the phrase 'يجب أن يكون كل ج ب', the modality is 'يجب' (it is necessary). Identify where it appears relative to the quantifier 'كل' and the predicate.
Modalities are often expressed through verbs like يجب (necessary), يمكن (possible), يمتنع (impossible).
2
Determine whether the modality qualifies the quantifier or the predication
If the modality directly attaches to the quantifier, it modifies the scope: 'كل ج يجب أن يكون ب' (every J is necessarily B). If it attaches to the predication, it modifies the copula: 'يجب أن يكون كل ج ب' (it is necessary that every J is B).
In Arabic word order, the position of the modal verb often indicates its scope.
3
Explain the difference in meaning
Jihaat al-sawr means necessity applies to each individual subject's possession of the predicate (e.g., every J is necessarily B). Jihaat al-haml means the proposition as a whole is necessary (e.g., it is a necessary truth that every J is B).
These can have different truth conditions: e.g., if B is accidental to some J, the first is false while the second may still be true.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example from Kalam
Consider 'It is necessary that the world is contingent.' As jihaat al-haml, this means the proposition 'the world is contingent' is necessarily true. As jihaat al-sawr, it means the world is necessarily contingent, which might imply a different modal status.
This distinction affects how we interpret modal arguments for the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: فيما يجب أن تراعيه في الإيجاب والسلب على حسب ما يتعلق بهما من الجهات وأحوالها. ولما عرفت حقيقة الموضوع والمحمول وعرفت أقسام الجهات أمكنك أن تحكم بشيء على الشيء كيف شئت لكن العلم بما يتعلق به من الأمور اللازمة من اللوازم؛ وذلك من وجوه الأول أن هذه الجهات قد تكون جهات للحمل وقد تكون جهات للسور. فإنك إذا قلت يجب أن يكون كل «ج» «ب»
Section: On what you must consider in affirmation and negation according to what pertains to them of modalities and their states. For if you say: 'It is necessary that every J is B'...
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage opens a new section titled 'On what you must consider in affirmation and negation according to what pertains to them of modalities and their states.' The author begins by reminding that having understood the nature of subject and predicate and the divisions of modalities, one can form propositions freely, but there are necessary consequences to consider. The first aspect introduced is that modalities (like necessity, possibility, etc.) can be attached either to the predication (jihaat al-haml) or to the quantifier (jihaat al-sawr). An example is given: 'It is necessary that every J is B' — presumably this can be interpreted with the modality on the quantifier (all J are necessarily B) or on the predication (necessity of the judgment itself). The passage ends here on the page, but it sets up a crucial distinction in modal propositional logic.

Modalities as Attached to Copula or Quantifier
Author explains modalities attached to copula or quantifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between modality attached to the copula and modality attached to the quantifier, and determine the truth value of a modal proposition in each case.

Core Idea
A modal operator can qualify the copula or the quantifier — confusing them leads to false judgments.

In the previous lesson you learned the divisions of modalities. Now the author warns: when you say 'It is necessary that every J is B', you must ask: does the necessity apply to each individual J being B, or to the whole set of Js being B collectively? The answer changes the truth of the proposition. This lesson teaches you to spot this ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two possible attachments
The modality (e.g., necessity, impossibility, possibility) can attach either to the copula (the relation between subject and predicate) or to the quantifier (the scope term like 'every'). Look at the proposition 'It is necessary that every human is an animal' and consider what the necessity qualifies.
The author calls these jiha al-haml and jiha al-sawr.
2
Analyze the truth conditions for each attachment
For jiha al-haml (copula-modality), the proposition is true if each individual human is necessarily an animal. For jiha al-sawr (quantifier-modality), the proposition is true only if the whole set of humans is necessarily animal (collectively). The author says the latter is false because the set of humans does not have the attribute of animalness necessarily as a whole.
The example shows that the same sentence can have two readings with different truth values.
3
Apply the distinction to other modalities
Test with impossibility: 'It is impossible that every human is a horse' – true for both readings. Test with possibility: 'It is possible that every human is a writer' – here the author suggests truth may differ. The author discusses a tricky example: 'It is possible that every human is eating (at a specific time)'. For each individual it is possible, but for the whole set collectively, it is not known if it is possible (since it requires simultaneous eating by all humans).
The distinction is crucial for evaluating modal propositions in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: فيما يجب أن تراعيه في الإيجاب والسلب على حسب ما يتعلق بهما من الجهات وأحوالها ولما عرفت حقيقة الموضوع والمحمول وعرفت أقسام الجهات أمكنك أن تحكم بشيء على الشيء كيف شئت لكن العلم بما يتعلق به من الأمور اللازمة من اللوازم؛ وذلك من وجوه الأول أن هذه الجهات قد تكون جهات للحمل وقد تكون جهات للسور. فإنك إذا قلت يجب أن يكون كل «ج» «ب» فإنه يحتمل أن تكون الجهة فيه جهة للكيفية ثبوت المحمول لكل واحد من آحاد ذلك الموضوع، ويحتمل أن تكون جهته لتلك الآحاد من حيث المجموع كما إذا قلت يجب أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانًا. فإن كان المراد أن كل واحد واحد من الإنسان بهذه الصفة فالجهة جهة الحمل والقضية صادقة. وإن كان المراد أن الكل من حيث هو الكل بهذه الصفة فالجهة جهة السور؛ والقضية كاذبة. وعلى هذا إذا قلت يمتنع أن يكون كل (ج)) ((ب) أو يمكن أن يكون كل (ج)» «ب»: والفرق بين الكل وكل واحد فذلك من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه. وأما صدق الحكم في أحدهما فإنه قد يكون من لوازم الصدق في الآخر كما إذا قلت يمتنع أن يكون كل إنسان فرسًا، وقد لا يكون كما علمت في قولك يجب أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانا. وكذلك في قولك يمكن أن يكون كل إنسان كاتبًا. وأما إذا قلت يمكن أن يكون كل إنسان آكلاً ففيه نظر؛ إذ العلم حاصل بأنه لا يمتنع أن يكون كل واحد واحد من الناس أن يأكلوا عند الطلوع طعاما معينًا أو شربة معينة. فأما أن الكل من حيث هو الكل فالعلم لا يكون حاصلا بأنه هل يمتنع أم لا؛ والمعلوم بالضرورة غير ما هو غير معلوم بالضرورة.
Section: What you must consider in affirmation and negation according to what pertains to them of modalities and their states. Since you have known the reality of subject and predicate and have known the divisions of modalities, you can judge something about something as you wish, but knowledge of what pertains to it from necessary matters is from the necessary consequences. And that is from aspects: First, these modalities may be modalities for the copula and may be modalities for the quantifier. For if you say 'It is necessary that every J is B', then it is possible that the modality is a modality for the quality of the predicate's affirmation for each individual of that subject, and it is possible that the modality is a modality for those individuals collectively, as when you say 'It is necessary that every human is an animal'. If the intention is that every single human (one by one) is with this attribute, then the modality is a modality of the copula and the proposition is true. If the intention is that the whole as a whole is with this attribute, then the modality is a modality of the quantifier and the proposition is false. Likewise if you say 'It is impossible that every J is B' or 'It is possible that every J is B'. The difference between 'all' and 'every one' is among what has been discussed. As for the truth of the judgment in one of them, it may be among the consequences of the truth in the other, as when you say 'It is impossible that every human is a horse', and it may not be as you learned in your saying 'It is necessary that every human is an animal'. Similarly in your saying 'It is possible that every human is a writer'. As for if you say 'It is possible that every human is eating', then there is consideration; since it is known that it is not impossible that every single human (one by one) eats at sunrise a specific food or a specific drink. As for the whole as a whole, it is not known whether it is impossible or not; and what is known by necessity is not what is not known by necessity.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a section (fasl) on correctly applying modalities to quantifiers and copulas in propositions. The author begins by stating that once you understand the subject, predicate, and divisions of modalities, you can form judgments. However, one must consider necessary consequences. The first aspect (al-awwal) is that modalities (jihaat) can attach either to the copula (haml) or to the quantifier (sawr). For example, the proposition 'It is necessary that every J is B' can mean either: (1) the necessity applies to the copula (i.e., for each individual J, it is necessary that it is B), or (2) the necessity applies to the quantifier (i.e., it is necessary that the whole set of Js is B). The author illustrates with the example 'It is necessary that every human is an animal'. If taken as copula-modality (each human necessarily is an animal), it is true. If taken as quantifier-modality (the whole of humanity necessarily is an animal), it would be false because the entire set of humans is not necessarily an animal in a collective sense (the set could cease to exist?). The same distinction applies to impossibility and possibility. The author notes that sometimes truth in one sense follows from the other, e.g., 'It is impossible that every human is a horse' is true in both senses because it's impossible for each individual and for the whole. But other cases differ, like the animal example. The author then considers a tricky case: 'It is possible that every human is eating (at a specific time)'. It is known that it is not impossible for each individual to eat at sunrise, but it is not known whether it is impossible for the whole set to eat collectively (simultaneously) because that would require all humans to eat at once, which may be impossible. The author concludes that what is known by necessity (the possibility for each individual) is different from what is not known by necessity (the possibility for the whole).

Modalities Can Be Predicates
Author explains that modal terms can themselves serve as predicates, changing the proposition's type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a modal term functions as a modality versus when it functions as the predicate, and determine the resulting proposition type.

Core Idea
A modal term is not always a modality; it can itself be the predicate, changing the proposition's logical form.

In this passage, the author reveals a subtle but crucial distinction: modal words like 'must' can play two different roles in a proposition. You might intuitively treat them as attaching to the copula, but they can also be the very thing being predicated. This dramatically changes the type of proposition you are dealing with—from a necessary proposition to an absolute general one. Let's unpack this with a careful analysis of the structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles of modal terms
Read the opening sentence: 'these modalities may sometimes be modalities and other times be predicates.' The author sets up a dichotomy. Modal terms can either qualify the copula (as jiha) or be the predicate themselves (as mahmul).
This is the key distinction to internalize.
2
Analyze the proposition 'Every J must be B'
The author gives a schematic proposition. Identify the subject (J), the modal term (must), and the term B. Then consider two interpretations: (1) B alone is the predicate, and 'must' is a modality on the copula. (2) The entire phrase 'must be B' is the predicate, with 'must' itself as the core predicate.
The Arabic phrase 'يجب أن يكون ب' is treated as a unit in the second interpretation.
3
Determine the proposition type for each interpretation
When 'must' is a modality (case 1), the proposition is necessary (daruriyya). When 'must' is the predicate (case 2), the proposition is absolute general (mutlaqa 'amma). The author explains that obligation is a relative concept, so it is defined by mentioning its relative (B), and the proposition loses its necessity.
The type changes because the modality is no longer on the copula but is itself the predicate.
4
Apply the distinction to the example
The author gives: 'Every human must be an animal.' In case 1, 'animal' is the predicate and 'must' is a modality, so the proposition is necessary: 'Human necessarily is animal.' In case 2, the predicate is 'must be animal', so the proposition means 'Human is (must-be-animal)' – a different logical form, which is absolute general. The author notes that 'animal' may or may not be the predicate depending on interpretation.
This example helps solidify the abstract analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أن هذه الجهات قد تكون جهات تارة وقد تكون محمولات أخرى، فإنك إذا قلت كل (ج) يجب أن يكون ((ب)) فالجيم هو الموضوع فلو كان المحمول هو الباء كان الوجوب جهة لذلك الحمل، والقضية ضرورية وإن كان المحمول هو قولنا يجب أن يكون «ب» كان الوجوب نفس المحمول، والباء مما تعرفه؛ إذ الوجوب أمر نسبي. والأمور النسبية ليس لها أسماء معينة بل إنما تمكن الإشارة إليها بذكر منسوباتها، فالباء تذكر لتكون معرفة الوجوب الذي هو المحمول، والقضية مطلقة عامة؛ فلو قلت كل إنسان يجب أن يكون حيوانا فالحيوان يحتمل أن يكون محمولاً ويحتمل أن لا يكون. وعلى هذا إذا
Second, these modalities may sometimes be modalities and other times be predicates. For if you say 'Every J must be B', then J is the subject. If the predicate is B, then the obligation (wujub) is a modality for that copula, and the proposition is necessary. But if the predicate is our saying 'must be B', then the obligation itself is the predicate, and B is what you know; for obligation is a relative matter. Relative matters do not have definite names but can only be indicated by mentioning their relatives. So B is mentioned to be a definer of the obligation which is the predicate, and the proposition is absolute general. So if you say 'Every human must be an animal', then 'animal' may be the predicate or may not be. And upon this...
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect (al-thani) about modalities: they can sometimes function as modalities (jihaat) and other times as predicates (mahmulat). The author analyzes the proposition 'Every J must be B'. The subject is J. If the predicate is B alone, then the modal term 'must' (wujub) is a modality attached to the copula, making the proposition a necessary (daruri) proposition. However, if the predicate is the entire phrase 'must be B', then 'must' (obligation) itself becomes the predicate, and B serves to identify or define that obligation because obligation is a relative concept. Relative concepts do not have fixed names; they are indicated by their relatives. In this case, B is mentioned to specify the obligation. The resulting proposition is 'absolute general' (mutlaqa 'amma), not necessary. The author gives the example 'Every human must be an animal'. Here, 'animal' could be the predicate (if the modality is on the copula), or it could be part of the predicate (if the modality itself is the predicate). The text ends mid-sentence, indicating further explanation follows.

Key Takeaway
A modal term can be either a modality on the copula or the predicate itself, and this determines whether the proposition is necessary or absolute general.

Distinguishing Collective and Distributive Modality in Universal Propositions
Author clarifies truth conditions of modal propositions with universal quantifier vs distributed individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the mode applying to each individual (جهة الحمل) and the mode applying to the whole (جهة السور) in a modal universal proposition, and determine the truth value based on the intended interpretation.

Core Idea
Modal universal propositions are ambiguous: the mode can apply distributively or collectively, changing truth.

When we say 'Every human must be an animal,' does necessity attach to each human individually or to the set itself? The author of this passage clarifies that the answer determines whether the statement is true or false. This distinction is crucial for evaluating modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes
Look for phrases like 'جهة الحمل' (mode of predication, distributive) and 'جهة السور' (mode of quantifier, collective). These correspond to readings where the modal applies to each subject individually or to the subject-term as a whole.
2
Extract truth conditions
For distributive mode, check if the modal property holds for every single individual. For collective mode, check if the modal property applies to the set considered as a unified whole.
The truth value may differ between the two readings.
3
Test with the example
Take 'كل إنسان يجب أن يكون حيوانًا'. Under distributive reading, it is true because each human is necessarily an animal. Under collective reading, it is false because the whole set is not an animal as a single entity.
The author explicitly says: if mode is for each individual, proposition is true; if for whole, false.
4
Apply to other modals
Extend to impossibility and possibility: e.g., 'يمتنع أن يكون كل إنسان فرسًا' is impossible under both readings. But 'يمكن أن يكون كل إنسان كاتبًا' may be true distributively but not necessarily collectively.
Note when truth of one reading follows from the other (e.g., impossibility pairs) and when it does not (necessity and possibility).
5
Analyze the nuanced example
Consider 'يمكن أن يكون كل إنسان آكلاً' (It is possible that every human is an eater). Under distributive reading, it is known possible. Under collective reading, it is not known, showing modal knowledge differs.
This highlights the epistemic dimension: what is known by necessity vs what is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه يحتمل أن تكون الجهة فيه جهة للكيفية ثبوت المحمول لكل واحد من آحاد ذلك الموضوع، ويحتمل أن تكون جهته لتلك الآحاد من حيث المجموع كما إذا قلت يجب أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانًا. فإن كان المراد أن كل واحد واحد من الإنسان بهذه الصفة فالجهة جهة الحمل والقضية صادقة. وإن كان المراد أن الكل من حيث هو الكل بهذه الصفة فالجهة جهة السور؛ والقضية كاذبة. وعلى هذا إذا قلت يمتنع أن يكون كل (ج) ((ب) أو يمكن أن يكون كل (ج)» «ب»: والفرق بين الكل وكل واحد فذلك من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه. وأما صدق الحكم في أحدهما فإنه قد يكون من لوازم الصدق في الآخر كما إذا قلت يمتنع أن يكون كل إنسان فرسًا، وقد لا يكون كما علمت في قولك يجب أن يكون كل إنسان حيوانا. وكذلك في قولك يمكن أن يكون كل إنسان كاتبًا. وأما إذا قلت يمكن أن يكون كل إنسان آكلاً ففيه نظر؛ إذ العلم حاصل بأنه لا يمتنع أن يكون كل واحد واحد من الناس أن يأكلوا عند الطلوع طعاما معينًا أو شربة معينة. فأما أن الكل من حيث هو الكل فالعلم لا يكون حاصلا بأنه هل يمتنع أم لا؛ والمعلوم بالضرورة غير ما هو غير معلوم بالضرورة.
For it is possible that the mode in it is a mode of the quality of the affirmation of the predicate to each one of the individuals of that subject, and it is possible that its mode is for those individuals in terms of the totality, as when you say: 'Every human must be an animal.' If what is meant is that each single human has this attribute, then the mode is the mode of predication and the proposition is true. And if what is meant is that the whole in terms of being the whole has this attribute, then the mode is the mode of the quantifier; and the proposition is false. And similarly, when you say: 'It is impossible for every J to be B' or 'It is possible for every J to be B': and the difference between 'the whole' and 'each one' is among what has already been discussed. As for the truth of the judgment in one of them, it may be entailed by truth in the other, as when you say: 'It is impossible for every human to be a horse', and it may not be, as you have learned in your saying: 'Every human must be an animal.' Likewise in your saying: 'It is possible for every human to be a writer.' And if you say: 'It is possible for every human to be an eater,' there is consideration in it; for it is known that it is not impossible for each single person to eat a specific food or a specific drink at sunrise. As for whether the whole in terms of being the whole is impossible or not, it is not known; and what is known by necessity is other than what is not known by necessity.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two interpretations of a modal universal proposition: one where the mode applies to each individual separately (جهة الحمل), and one where the mode applies to the collective whole (جهة السور). The author illustrates with the example 'Every human must be an animal' – if we take 'must' as applying to each human individually, the proposition is true; but if we take it as applying to the whole set collectively (i.e., the set of all humans must necessarily be an animal), it is false because the totality is abstract. The same distinction applies to impossibility and possibility propositions. The author then notes that sometimes the truth of one interpretation follows from the other (e.g., impossibility for each individual implies impossibility for the whole, as in 'Every human is a horse' is impossible per se), but sometimes it does not (e.g., necessity for each individual does not make the whole necessary). The final point about 'every human is an eater' is nuanced: we know it is possible for each individual to eat a specific meal at sunrise, but whether the whole set collectively can eat is not known by necessity. This guards against conflating distributive and collective readings of modalities in quantified propositions.

Modal Terms as Mode or Predicate
Author explains modal terms can be modes or predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a modal term in a proposition functions as the mode of predication or as the predicate itself.

Core Idea
The same modal word can change a proposition's type from necessary to absolute general.

In this passage, the author explains a crucial distinction: modal terms like 'must' and 'possibly' are not always just qualifiers of the predication; they can themselves be the predicate. This affects how we classify the proposition's modality. Let's examine the examples and learn how to distinguish the two roles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal term
Locate the word that indicates necessity, impossibility, or possibility in the proposition. In the example 'Every J must be B', the modal term is 'must'.
2
Determine if it qualifies the predication
Ask: Does the modal term modify the relation between subject and predicate? If 'must' attaches to the predication ('must be B'), then it is the mode, and the proposition is necessary.
In 'Every human must be an animal', if 'must' is the mode, the proposition asserts necessary predication.
3
Check if the modal term could be the predicate
Reinterpret the sentence: If the modal term is itself the predicate (e.g., 'Every human is (must-be-animal)'), then it is the predicate, and the proposition becomes absolute general.
The author notes that relative terms like 'necessity' have no fixed name, so they are indicated by their objects.
4
Apply the test to other modalities
Repeat the same analysis for impossibility (e.g., 'Every J is impossible to be B') and possibility. The same ambiguity applies: the modal word can be the mode or the predicate.
When the modal term is the predicate, the proposition is absolute general, regardless of the modality's inherent necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أن هذه الجهات قد تكون جهات تارة وقد تكون محمولات أخرى، فإنك إذا قلت كل (ج) يجب أن يكون ((ب)) فالجيم هو الموضوع فلو كان المحمول هو الباء كان الوجوب جهة لذلك الحمل، والقضية ضرورية وإن كان المحمول هو قولنا يجب أن يكون «ب» كان الوجوب نفس المحمول، والباء مما تعرفه؛ إذ الوجوب أمر نسبي. والأمور النسبية ليس لها أسماء معينة بل إنما تمكن الإشارة إليها بذكر منسوباتها، فالباء تذكر لتكون معرفة الوجوب الذي هو المحمول، والقضية مطلقة عامة؛ فلو قلت كل إنسان يجب أن يكون حيوانا فالحيوان يحتمل أن يكون محمولاً ويحتمل أن لا يكون. وعلى هذا إذا قلت كل «ج» يمتنع أن يكون «ب» أو يمكن أن يكون «ب»: فإنه يمكن أن يكون الامتناع جهة ويمكن أن يكون محمولاً . وكذلك الإمكان فلو كان محمولاً هذا أو ذلك كانت / القضية مطلقة عامة فإنه هو المحمول ولا بيان للحمل بأنه [١٠٣ و] كيف هو . ولا يقال كيف هو والامتناع للممتنع ضروري؛ والإمكان للممكن كذلك؛ إذ العبرة في كون القضية موجبة هي أن تكون مذكورة في اللفظ.
The second: that these modes may sometimes be modes and sometimes be predicates. For if you say 'Every J must be B', the J is the subject. If the predicate is B, then 'must' is the mode of that predication, and the proposition is necessary. But if the predicate is our saying 'must be B', then 'must' is itself the predicate, and B is what you know; for necessity is a relative matter. Relative matters do not have specific names but can only be indicated by mentioning their relativized terms. So B is mentioned to identify the necessity which is the predicate, and the proposition is absolute general. So if you say 'Every human must be an animal', 'animal' may be the predicate or may not. And similarly, if you say 'Every J is impossible to be B' or 'Every J is possibly B', it is possible that impossibility is a mode and possible that it is a predicate. Similarly possibility. So if it is a predicate, the proposition is absolute general, for it is the predicate and there is no explanation of the predication as to how it is. And it is not said 'how is it' while impossibility for the impossible is necessary, and possibility for the possible is similar; for the criterion for a proposition being affirmative is that it is mentioned in the wording.

Commentary
This passage discusses the ambiguity of modal terms in propositions: they can function as the mode (جهة) qualifying the predication, or as the predicate itself (محمول). The author uses the example 'Every J must be B'. If 'must' is the mode, then the proposition is necessary (ضرورية) because it asserts that J is necessarily B. If 'must' is the predicate, then the proposition is absolute general (مطلقة عامة) because the predication is simply 'J is (must-be-B)', and 'must' is a relative notion that needs specification. The same applies to impossibility and possibility. The key point: when the modal term is the predicate, the proposition is not modalized in its content; it is just an absolute affirmative. The passage also notes that relative matters like necessity are not named directly but through their relata. This is important for understanding how to read modal propositions in syllogistic contexts.

Classifying Modal Propositions by Subject/Predicate Attachment
Author explains how modalities can apply to both subject and predicate terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a modal proposition as having modality on the subject, predicate, both, or neither, based on the position of the modal qualifier.

Core Idea
Modalities can attach to subject, predicate, or both — each produces a distinct proposition type.

In this passage, the author explains how modal qualifiers like 'necessity', 'impossibility', and 'possibility' can apply to the subject term, the predicate term, or both. This classification is crucial for understanding the logical behavior of propositions in syllogisms, especially when converting or combining them. By learning this, you will be able to precisely determine the modal status of any proposition and avoid confusion when reasoning about necessary, impossible, or possible things.

Guided Path
1
Locate the modal qualifier
Read the proposition and find words like بالضرورة (by necessity), بالامتناع (by impossibility), or بالإمكان (by possibility). Note whether they appear immediately after the subject, immediately before the predicate, or both.
In Arabic, the modal qualifier can be placed after the subject (e.g., كل ج بالضرورة هو ب) or after the copula 'هو' before the predicate (e.g., كل ج هو بالضرورة ب).
2
Determine attachment side
If the qualifier is attached to the subject (ج بالضرورة), it is 'necessary in subject' (ضرورية الموضوع). If attached to the predicate (بالضرورة ب), it is 'necessary in predicate' (ضرورية المحمول). If both, it is 'necessary in both extremes' (ضرورية الطرفين). If no qualifier, it is absolute (مطلقة).
The author also notes that for impossibility and possibility, the same patterns apply.
3
Classify the proposition
Using the author's categories, assign the proposition to one of: مطلقة عامة (absolute general), ضرورية الموضوع (necessary in subject), ضرورية المحمول (necessary in predicate), or ضرورية الطرفين (necessary in both).
Example: 'كل ج بالضرورة هو ب' is ضرورية الموضوع; 'كل ج هو بالضرورة ب' is ضرورية المحمول.
4
Identify special predicate-only modalities
Note that some modal types, like necessity according to description and perpetuity (الضرورة بحسب الوصف والدوام), can only be considered in the predicate side, as seen in conditional propositions like 'كل كاتب متحرك بالضرورة ما دام كاتبًا'.
These cannot attach to the subject because they require a descriptive condition that belongs to the predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث أن من الجهات ما يمكن اعتباره في الطرفين كما إذا قلت كل «ج ب وعنيتَ به أن كل ما له الجيمية كيف كانت فله البائية كذلك: فإن القضية مطلقة عامة بحسب الموضوع والمحمول ولو قلت كل «ج» بالوجوب فهو «ب» أو قلت بالضرورة فإنها ضرورية الموضوع مطلقة المحمول، وبالعكس عند العكس كما إذا قلت كل (ج) فهو بالضرورة (ب) فإنها مطلقة الموضوع ضرورية المحمول. ولو قلت كل «ج» بالضرورة فهو «ب» بالضرورة فإنها ضرورية الموضوع والمحمول فهي إذن ضرورية الطرفين. وعلى هذا إذا قلت كل «ج» بالامتناع فهو «ب» أو بالإمكان فهو (ب) ومنها ما لا يمكن اعتباره إلا في جانب المحمول كالضرورة بحسب الوصف والدوام كذلك كما مرّ في المشروطتين والعرفيتين . فإنه لا بد من ذكر وصف يكون شرطًا لضرورة المحمول نحو الكاتب في قولنا بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك ما دام كاتبًا وليس مع وصف الموضوع وصف آخر يكون شرطًا للضرورة أو الدوام، فلا يمكن اعتبار هذه الأربع إذن إلا في جانب المحمول.
The third: that among the modes are those that can be considered in both extremes, as when you say 'Every J is B' and you mean that everything that has J-ness in any way has B-ness accordingly: then the proposition is absolute general with respect to subject and predicate. And if you say 'Every J is necessarily B' or 'by necessity', then it is necessary in subject, absolute in predicate. And the opposite when inverted, as when you say 'Every J is by necessity B', then it is absolute in subject, necessary in predicate. And if you say 'Every J is by necessity B by necessity', then it is necessary in subject and predicate, so it is necessary in both extremes. And similarly if you say 'Every J is by impossibility B' or 'by possibility B'. And among them are what can only be considered in the side of the predicate, like necessity with respect to description and perpetuity as mentioned in the conditional and convential propositions. For it is necessary to mention a description that is a condition for the necessity of the predicate, like 'writer' in our saying: 'By necessity every writer is moving as long as he is a writer.' And there is no other description with the subject that is a condition for necessity or perpetuity, so these four cannot be considered except in the side of the predicate.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage categorizes modal propositions based on whether the modality attaches to the subject term, the predicate term, or both. The author distinguishes: (1) propositions that are absolute in both subject and predicate (مطلقة عامة بحسب الموضوع والمحمول) when no modal qualifier is attached to either; (2) 'necessary in subject, absolute in predicate' when the modal qualifier is attached to the subject (e.g., 'Every J necessarily is B'); (3) 'absolute in subject, necessary in predicate' when the modal qualifier is attached to the predicate (e.g., 'Every J is by necessity B'); (4) 'necessary in both extremes' when both subject and predicate have modal qualifiers. The same applies for impossibility and possibility. However, some modal types (specifically 'ضرورة بحسب الوصف والدوام' – necessity according to description and perpetuity) can only be considered in the predicate side, as seen in conditional propositions like 'Every writer is necessarily moving as long as he is a writer', where the necessitating condition (writing) is attached to the predicate. This is a refinement of modal logic relevant to syllogistic figures.

Modalized Negation vs. Negation of Modality
Author contrasts negating the mode from modalized negation in propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'بالوجوب ليس' (necessarily not) and 'ليس بالوجوب' (not necessary) and correctly identify the scope of negation in modal propositions.

Core Idea
Negating the mode is not the same as modalizing the negation.

In modal logic, the position of negation relative to the modal operator changes the meaning. The author distinguishes two Arabic constructions: one where necessity applies to the negation (بالوجوب ليس) and one where the negation denies necessity (ليس بالوجوب). Understanding this difference is crucial for interpreting modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with necessary, impossible, and possible statements in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two constructions
Locate 'بالوجوب ليس' and 'ليس بالوجوب' in the Arabic text. Note that they are distinct phrases with different logical meanings.
The first is a modalized negation, the second a negation of the modality.
2
Parse the scope
In 'بالوجوب ليس', the negation falls under the scope of necessity: 'it is necessary that not'. In 'ليس بالوجوب', the negation scopes over necessity: 'it is not necessary'.
Think of scope as a box: in the first, necessity contains negation; in the second, negation contains necessity.
3
Test logical relations
Determine if one implies the other. 'بالوجوب ليس' entails 'ليس بالوجوب' (if necessarily not, then it is not necessary), but the converse does not hold.
This is a one-way entailment.
4
Apply to impossibility and possibility
The author states the same distinction applies with 'بالامتناع' (impossibility) and 'بالإمكان' (possibility). Consider how 'بالامتناع ليس' (impossible that not) relates to 'ليس بالامتناع' (not impossible).
The pattern mirrors necessity: modalized negation vs. negation of modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع هو ما يتعلق بالسلب عند الإيجاب وبالإيجاب عند السلب، كما إذا قلت بالوجوب ليس مثلا فإنه مما يفارق قولنا ليس بالوجوب؛ إذ الأول مما يتحقق السلب عند تحقق الإيجاب، والثاني مما ينافي الإيجاب عند تحقق السلب والفرق بينهما بين وعلى هذا في الامتناع والإمكان فإن قولنا بالامتناع
The fourth: what pertains to negation in the case of affirmation and affirmation in the case of negation, as when you say 'by necessity not' for example, for it differs from our saying 'not by necessity'. Because the first is where negation obtains when affirmation obtains, and the second is what opposes affirmation when negation obtains. And the difference between them is clear. And similarly in impossibility and possibility, for our saying 'by impossibility...'
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage addresses the interaction of modal terms with negation. The author distinguishes two constructions: 'بالوجوب ليس' (by necessity not, i.e., necessarily not) and 'ليس بالوجوب' (not by necessity). The first expresses that the negation is necessary: it is the case that the predicate is necessarily negated of the subject. The second denies necessity: it is not the case that the predicate is necessarily affirmed. These are logically distinct: 'necessarily not' (بالوجوب ليس) implies 'not by necessity' (ليس بالوجوب), but the converse does not hold. The author notes that the same distinction applies to impossibility and possibility, but the text cuts off at 'بالامتناع' (by impossibility). The clear difference guards against confusing the scope of negation and modality, a common error in modal logic.

How Modalities Attach to Subject and Predicate
Author explains how modalities apply to both subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a modality is attached to the subject, predicate, both, or neither in a given categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Modalities are not just for the whole proposition—they can attach to individual terms.

In this passage, the author clarifies that modalities such as necessity can be attached to the subject term, the predicate term, or both, and some modalities only apply to the predicate. This parsing skill is essential for accurately interpreting modal propositions in Islamic logic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality word
Look for words like بالضرورة (necessarily), بالإمكان (possibly), بالامتناع (impossibly) in the Arabic proposition.
2
Determine its position relative to the subject and predicate
See if the modality comes immediately after the subject (e.g., كل ج بالضرورة ب) or after the predicate (e.g., كل ج ب بالضرورة) or both.
3
Classify the proposition type
Based on position, classify as: absolute general (no modality), necessary of subject only, necessary of predicate only, necessary of both, etc.
4
Recognize the special case of descriptive necessity
Understand that some modalities like الضرورة بحسب الوصف only apply to the predicate because they require a condition in the predicate and not in the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث أن من الجهات ما يمكن اعتباره في الطرفين كما إذا قلت كل «ج ب وعنيتَ به أن كل ما له الجيمية كيف كانت فله البائية كذلك: فإن القضية مطلقة عامة بحسب الموضوع والمحمول ولو قلت كل «ج» بالوجوب فهو «ب» أو قلت بالضرورة فإنها ضرورية الموضوع مطلقة المحمول، وبالعكس عند العكس كما إذا قلت كل (ج) فهو بالضرورة (ب) فإنها مطلقة الموضوع ضرورية المحمول. ولو قلت كل «ج» بالضرورة فهو «ب» بالضرورة فإنها ضرورية الموضوع والمحمول فهي إذن ضرورية الطرفين. وعلى هذا إذا قلت كل «ج» بالامتناع فهو «ب» أو بالإمكان فهو (ب) ومنها ما لا يمكن اعتباره إلا في جانب المحمول كالضرورة بحسب الوصف والدوام كذلك كما مرّ في المشروطتين والعرفيتين . فإنه لا بد من ذكر وصف يكون شرطًا لضرورة المحمول نحو الكاتب في قولنا بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك ما دام كاتبًا وليس مع وصف الموضوع وصف آخر يكون شرطًا للضرورة أو الدوام، فلا يمكن اعتبار هذه الأربع إذن إلا في جانب المحمول.
Third: Among the modalities, some can be considered in both extremes, as when you say 'Every ج is ب' and you mean by it that everything that has the property of being ج, however it is, has the property of being ب similarly: then the proposition is absolute general with respect to subject and predicate. And if you say 'Every ج is necessarily ب' or you say 'by necessity', then it is necessary of subject, absolute of predicate, and vice versa upon conversion, as when you say 'Every ج is necessarily ب', then it is absolute of subject, necessary of predicate. And if you say 'Every ج is necessarily ب necessarily', then it is necessary of subject and predicate, so it is necessary of both extremes. And likewise if you say 'Every ج is impossibly ب' or 'possibly ب'. And among them are those that cannot be considered except on the side of the predicate, like necessity according to description and permanence, as previously in the conditioned and conventional [propositions]. For it is necessary to mention a description that is a condition for the necessity of the predicate, like 'writer' in our saying 'necessarily every writer is moving as long as he is a writer', and there is not with the description of the subject another description that is a condition for necessity or permanence. So it is not possible to consider these four except on the side of the predicate.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage defines how modalities (necessity, impossibility, possibility) can attach to the subject term, predicate term, or both in a categorical proposition. The author begins by explaining that when a proposition is absolute (مطلقة عامة), no explicit modality is stated. If we say 'Every ج is necessarily ب', the modality (necessity) is attached to the subject term, making the proposition necessary of subject and absolute of predicate. If we say 'Every ج is ب necessarily', the modality is attached to the predicate term. If both terms carry the same modality, the proposition is necessary of both extremes. However, there are modalities like 'necessity by description' (الضرورة بحسب الوصف) that can only be considered in the predicate, because they require a description in the predicate that conditions the necessity, such as 'writer' being a condition for 'moving' in 'every writer is moving while a writer'. The subject lacks such a condition. Thus, these four modalities (necessity by description, by permanence, conditioned, conventional) apply only to the predicate.
