Modality-Negation Order: Scope Matters
Author distinguishes between modality preceding negation vs. negation preceding modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given Arabic modal proposition has the modality preceding the negation or the negation preceding the modality, and correctly interpret its logical meaning.

Core Idea
In modal logic, word order determines whether the modality qualifies the negation or the negation denies the modality.

This lesson tackles a subtle but crucial point in modal logic: the interaction between negation and modality. In Arabic, the placement of the negation particle (ليس) relative to the modal qualifier (e.g., بالضرورة) changes the entire meaning. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting modal propositions in kalam and usul al-fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the modal qualifier
Find the word expressing modality, such as بالضرورة (by necessity), بالامتناع (by impossibility), or بالإمكان (by possibility). This word will indicate the type of modality.
2
Locate the negation particle
Find the negation word (usually ليس or لا). Note its position relative to the modal qualifier: does it come before or after?
3
Determine precedence
If the modal qualifier comes first (e.g., بالضرورة ليس), then the modality applies to the negation: it is necessary that something is not the case. If the negation comes first (e.g., ليس بالضرورة), then the negation applies to the modality: it is not necessary that something is the case.
The original passage uses full sentences like بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان ليس بفرس (by necessity, nothing of man is not a horse) vs ليس بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس (not by necessity, nothing of man is a horse).
4
Translate the meaning accordingly
For مثال: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس = 'It is necessary that nothing of man is a horse' (necessity of negation). ليس بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس = 'It is not necessary that nothing of man is a horse' (denial of necessity).
Note the first example has an extra ليس in the Arabic: بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان ليس بفرس. This double negation is handled by the author.
5
Handle apparent word order mismatches
Be aware that in some constructions the modal qualifier may appear after the negation in wording but logically precede it. For example: لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان — here بالإمكان comes last, but the author says the modality precedes negation in reality. Interpret based on logical scope, not just surface order.
The passage says: 'the modality precedes the negation in reality even if it is postponed in wording.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع هو ما يتعلق بالسلب عند الإيجاب وبالإيجاب عند السلب، كما إذا قلت بالوجوب ليس مثلا فإنه مما يفارق قولنا ليس بالوجوب؛ إذ الأول مما يتحقق السلب عند تحقق الإيجاب، والثاني مما ينافي الإيجاب عند تحقق السلب والفرق بينهما بين وعلى هذا في الامتناع والإمكان فإن قولنا بالامتناع ليس مما يفارق قولنا ليس بالامتناع . وكذلك قولنا بالإمكان ليس مما يفارق قولنا ليس بالإمكان. وذلك بتقدم حرف السلب على الجهة وتأخره عنها. فلو قلت بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان ليس بفرس مثلا كانت الجهة متقدمة على السلب؛ والقضية هي السالبة الضرورية. ولو قلت ليس بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس كان السلب متقدمًا على الجهة والقضية سالبة بالضرورة. ففي الأولى الضرورة للسلب، وفي الثانية السلب للضرورة؛ فكانت الأولى تلازم الممتنعة والثانية تلازم العامة الموجبة إن سلبتَ ضرورة سلبية والسالبة ضرورة إيجابية، والممكنة الخاصة إن سلبتهما معا. وكذلك إذا قلت لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان فإن الجهة متقدمة على السلب في الحقيقة وإن كانت متأخرة عنه في اللفظ والقضية هي السالبة الممكنة، ولو قلت ليس بالإمكان لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب كان السلب متقدمًا على الجهة؛ والقضية سالبة الإمكان ففي الأولى الإمكان للسلب، وفي الثانية السلب للإمكان. فالأولى إن كانت عامة تلازم الممتنعة والممكنة الخاصة ملازمة غير منعكسة، وإن كانت خاصة تلازم الممكنة الخاصة الموجبة ملازمة منعكسة. والثانية تلازم الضرورية المقابلة للعامة إن سُلب العام والخاصة إن سُلب الخاص؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت في الغير من الصور فإن الأولى غير الثانية في جميع الصور.
Fourth: That which pertains to negation in the case of affirmation and to affirmation in the case of negation, as when you say 'by necessity not' for example, for it differs from our saying 'not by necessity'; because the first is such that negation is realized when affirmation is realized, and the second is such that it contradicts affirmation when negation is realized, and the difference between them is clear. And likewise concerning impossibility and possibility: for our saying 'by impossibility not' differs from our saying 'not by impossibility', and similarly our saying 'by possibility not' differs from our saying 'not by possibility'. That is due to the precedence and postponement of the negation particle relative to the modality. So if you say 'by necessity, nothing of man is not a horse' for example, the modality precedes the negation; and the proposition is the necessary negative. And if you say 'not by necessity, nothing of man is a horse', the negation precedes the modality, and the proposition is negative by necessity. So in the first, necessity belongs to the negation; in the second, negation belongs to necessity. Thus the first entails the impossible [proposition], and the second entails the general affirmative if you negate a negative necessity, and the negative of a positive necessity, and the special possible if you negate both together. And likewise if you say 'nothing of man is a writer by possibility', the modality precedes the negation in reality even if it is postponed in wording, and the proposition is the possible negative; and if you say 'not by possibility, nothing of man is a writer', the negation precedes the modality, and the proposition is negative of possibility. So in the first, possibility belongs to the negation; in the second, negation belongs to possibility. So the first, if general, entails the impossible and the special possible in a non-conversive entailment; and if special, it entails the affirmative special possible in a conversive entailment. And the second entails the necessity opposite to the general if the general is negated, and the special if the special is negated. So consider from what you know in other forms, for the first is not the second in all forms.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage explains the interaction between the negation operator and modal qualifiers (necessity, impossibility, possibility). The key distinction is whether the modality precedes the negation or the negation precedes the modality. Examples: 'بالوجوب ليس' (by necessity, not) means the necessity applies to the negation (i.e., it is necessary that something is not the case). 'ليس بالوجوب' (not by necessity) negates the necessity (i.e., it is not necessary). The author illustrates with more complex examples involving multiple negations and modalities, such as 'nothing of man is not a horse by necessity' versus 'not by necessity, nothing of man is a horse'. The first entails the impossible proposition (since if it is necessary that nothing is not a horse, then everything is necessarily a horse? Actually careful: 'لا شيء من الإنسان ليس بفرس' means 'nothing of man is not a horse' i.e., every man is a horse. With 'بالضرورة' it becomes 'it is necessary that every man is a horse'. This entails the impossible? The text says 'تلازم الممتنعة' (entails the impossible). Actually, if it is necessary that every man is a horse, that is false but not impossible? In modal logic, a necessary falsehood is impossible. But the author's reasoning is technical. The second entails the general affirmative if you negate a negative necessity, etc. The author also considers examples with 'possibility' and notes that in such propositions the modality may be considered to precede the negation in reality even if the wording places it after. The lesson emphasizes the importance of word order in determining the logical force of modalized negative propositions.

The Conceptual Reality of Modalities
Discussion on modality ontology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why modalities are considered conceptual (i'tibari) rather than real entities, and analyze the argument about general possibility not being a genus for necessity.

Core Idea
Modalities are mental constructs, not properties of objects.

In Islamic logic, propositions are often qualified by modalities like necessity, impossibility, and possibility. But what are these modalities? Are they real features of the world, or merely mental concepts? This passage from the Mantiq Kabir tackles exactly that question, clarifying their ontological status and setting the stage for how we use modal propositions in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modalities
The passage begins with 'these three' — necessity (wujub), impossibility (imtina'), and possibility (imkan). Notice they are listed together as a group whose nature is under debate.
These are the core modalities in Aristotelian and Islamic logic.
2
Understand the debate: existential vs. non-existential
The author mentions a discussion whether they are 'non-existential matters' (umur 'adamiyya) or not. Some argued that modalities are mere negations; others denied that. The author then gives his own position.
'Adamiyya' refers to things that are privative, lacking positive existence.
3
Grasp the author's verdict: conceptual entities
The author says 'the truth is that they are conceptual matters' (amr i'tibari), meaning they have no independent existence in the external world; they exist only in the mind.
This aligns with the Ash'ari and later philosophical tradition that attributes are mental constructs.
4
Trace the argument about general possibility
General possibility is defined as 'the negation of impossibility'. Since impossibility is pure negation, its negation becomes a positive notion (amr thubuti). Yet this positive notion cannot serve as a genus (jins) for necessity, because necessity is essentially different.
The passage cuts off, but the implication is that if possibility were a genus, necessity would be a species, which is problematic because necessity is more fundamental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس أن هذه الثلاثة وهي الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان ففيها من الكلام بأنها من الأمور العدمية كلا وجملة، أو لا من الأمور العدمية والحق أنها من الأمور الاعتبارية فلا وجود لها بنفسها في الأعيان. وقد قيل في الإمكان إن العام منه عبارة عن سلب الامتناع والامتناع سلب محض؛ فيكون الإمكان العام أمرًا ثبوتيا لكنه لا يمكن أن يكون جنسًا للوجوب بالذات. والإمكان الخاص كذلك وإن كان مشتركًا بينهما؛ إذ لو كان جنسًا لكان امتياز الوجوب
Fifth: These three, namely necessity, impossibility, and possibility, contain discussion concerning whether they are non-existential matters, entirely, or not non-existential. The truth is that they are conceptual matters, so they have no existence in themselves in external entities. And it has been said concerning possibility that the general [possibility] is an expression of the negation of impossibility, and impossibility is pure negation; thus general possibility is a positive matter, but it cannot be a genus for necessity essentially. And special possibility is likewise, even though it is shared between them; for if it were a genus, then the distinction of necessity would be [incomplete].
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage addresses the ontological status of the three modalities: necessity (وجوب), impossibility (امتناع), and possibility (إمكان). The author presents a debate: some claim they are non-existential (عدميه), others deny this. The author affirms that they are conceptual (اعتبارية), meaning they have no independent existence in external reality; they are mental constructs. The discussion then focuses on possibility: general possibility (الإمكان العام) is defined as the negation of impossibility, and since impossibility is pure negation, general possibility becomes a positive notion (أمر ثبوتي). However, this positive notion cannot serve as a genus for necessity, because necessity is essentially different. Special possibility (الإمكان الخاص) shares a similar issue. The passage is cut off before fully explaining why being a genus would affect the distinction of necessity. This is a common topic in Islamic logic and philosophy concerning the reality of modal concepts, often linked to the debate between the Peripatetics and the theologians.

Key Takeaway

Distinguishing Modal Scope: Negation Before or After Modality
Author analyzes how order of negation and modality changes proposition type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a 'necessary negative' (where modality qualifies negation) and a 'negative of necessity' (where negation qualifies modality), and identify their different logical entailments.

Core Idea
The order of negation and modality changes the proposition's logical force and entailments.

In modal logic, the placement of the negation particle relative to the modal word is crucial. This lesson unpacks how 'by necessity, no human is not a horse' differs from 'not by necessity, no human is a horse'. Understanding this distinction is essential for accurate logical analysis in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the order
Locate the negation particle (ليس or لا) and the modal word (بالضرورة, بالإمكان). Determine which comes first in the logical structure, not necessarily in the word order.
In Arabic, the modal word may appear first in the sentence but be logically after the negation in some cases.
2
Classify the proposition
If the modality precedes the negation (e.g., بالضرورة لا شيء...), the proposition is a 'necessary negative' (سالبة ضرورية): the necessity qualifies the negation itself. If the negation precedes the modality (e.g., ليس بالضرورة لا شيء...), it is a 'negative of necessity' (سالبة بالضرورة): the negation applies to the necessity.
3
Translate the meaning
For modality-before: the negation is itself necessary (it is necessarily the case that no S is P). For negation-before: the necessity is negated (it is not necessarily the case that no S is P).
4
Determine entailments
Using the author's rules: a necessary negative (universal) entails the impossible; a negative of necessity entails the universal affirmative after certain negations. Apply the same for possibility modality.
The passage provides specific entailments for each case.
5
Apply to possibility
Repeat the analysis with 'by possibility' (بالإمكان). Note that the first universal possible negative entails the impossible and the special possible, while the second entails the necessary opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس مما يفارق قولنا ليس بالامتناع . وكذلك قولنا بالإمكان ليس مما يفارق قولنا ليس بالإمكان. وذلك بتقدم حرف السلب على الجهة وتأخره عنها. فلو قلت بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان ليس بفرس مثلا كانت الجهة متقدمة على السلب؛ والقضية هي السالبة الضرورية. ولو قلت ليس بالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس كان السلب متقدمًا على الجهة والقضية سالبة بالضرورة. ففي الأولى الضرورة للسلب، وفي الثانية السلب للضرورة؛ فكانت الأولى تلازم الممتنعة والثانية تلازم العامة الموجبة إن سلبتَ ضرورة سلبية والسالبة ضرورة إيجابية، والممكنة الخاصة إن سلبتهما معا. وكذلك إذا قلت لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان فإن الجهة متقدمة على السلب في الحقيقة وإن كانت متأخرة عنه في اللفظ والقضية هي السالبة الممكنة، ولو قلت ليس بالإمكان لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب كان السلب متقدمًا على الجهة؛ والقضية سالبة الإمكان ففي الأولى الإمكان للسلب، وفي الثانية السلب للإمكان. فالأولى إن كانت عامة تلازم الممتنعة والممكنة الخاصة ملازمة غير منعكسة، وإن كانت خاصة تلازم الممكنة الخاصة الموجبة ملازمة منعكسة. والثانية تلازم الضرورية المقابلة للعامة إن سُلب العام والخاصة إن سُلب الخاص؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت في الغير من الصور فإن الأولى غير الثانية في جميع الصور.
It is not the case that our saying 'not by impossibility' differs from our saying 'not impossible'. Similarly, our saying 'by possibility' does not differ from our saying 'not by possibility'. That is due to the negation particle preceding or following the modality. For if you say 'By necessity, no human is not a horse' for example, the modality precedes the negation, and the proposition is the necessary negative. But if you say 'Not by necessity, no human is a horse', the negation precedes the modality, and the proposition is negative by necessity. In the first, necessity is for the negation; in the second, negation is for the necessity. Thus the first entails the impossible, and the second entails the universal affirmative if you negate negative necessity and the negative as necessary affirmation, and the special possible if you negate both together. Similarly, if you say 'No human is a writer by possibility', the modality precedes the negation in reality, even if it is after it in wording, and the proposition is the possible negative. If you say 'Not by possibility, no human is a writer', the negation precedes the modality, and the proposition is negative of possibility. In the first, possibility is for the negation; in the second, negation is for the possibility. The first, if universal, entails the impossible and the special possible in a non-reciprocal entailment; if it is special, it entails the affirmative special possible in a reciprocal entailment. The second entails the necessary that is opposite of the universal if the universal is negated, and the special if the special is negated. So consider what you know from other forms, for the first is not the second in all forms.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of how the order of the negation particle and the modality (jiha) affects the logical force of a proposition. The author begins by stating that 'not by impossibility' and 'not impossible' are equivalent—this is a baseline. Then he explains that the placement of the negation before or after the modality changes the proposition. He gives an example with necessity: 'By necessity, no human is not a horse' (the modality comes first, so it is a necessary negative; the necessity qualifies the negation). In contrast, 'Not by necessity, no human is a horse' has negation first, so the negation applies to the necessity, making it a negative of necessity. The author then derives entailments: the first (necessary negative) entails the impossible (since it denies possibility), while the second (negation of necessity) entails universal affirmative if certain negations are applied, etc. He then applies the same reasoning to possibility. For example, 'No human is a writer by possibility' (modality first) is a possible negative, whereas 'Not by possibility, no human is a writer' (negation first) is a negation of possibility. These have different entailments: the first universal possible negative entails the impossible and the special possible (non-reciprocally), while the special case entails the affirmative special possible reciprocally. The second case (negation of possibility) entails the necessary opposite. The key point is that the first and second forms are distinct in all cases, and one must carefully track the order to determine the correct logical relationships.

سالبة ضرورية
سالبة بالضرورة

C) بالضرورة لا شيء من الخمر ليس بحرام (By necessity, no wine is not forbidden)
D) ليس بالضرورة لا شيء من الخمر بحرام (Not by necessity, no wine is forbidden)

Key Takeaway
The scope of negation relative to modality determines whether the necessity applies to the negation or the negation applies to the necessity, and this changes the entire logical character of the proposition.

Conditional Propositions: Muttasila and Munfasila
Author defines conditional propositions and distinguishes muttasil and munfasil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a conjunctive conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive conditional (munfasila) based on the relation of implication (luzūm) or opposition (ʿinād).

Core Idea
Every conditional is either implication (if-then) or opposition (either-or) – no third possibility.

In this passage, the author defines the conditional proposition (al-sharṭiyya) and explains its two fundamental types. The key is understanding the relation (taʿalluq) between the two parts: either necessary implication (luzūm) or mutual opposition (ʿinād). This classification is the foundation for all further analysis of conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of a conditional proposition
A conditional is composed of two parts related by a condition. The author reminds you it can be true/false and reducible to categoricals.
2
Identify the two possible relations
The relation is either luzūm (implication) or ʿinād (opposition). No other relation is possible.
3
Map relation to proposition type
If luzūm, it is muttasila (conjunctive). If ʿinād, it is munfasila (disjunctive).
4
Understand the logical basis
The related part is either necessary in existence, necessary in non-existence, or neither. The third is incompatible, leaving only the first two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشرطية فقد مرَّ ذكرها من قبل بأنها ما هي؛ وكيف هي وإنها مشاركة مع الحمليات في كونها محتملة للتصديق والتكذيب ومنحلة إليها كذلك وإن كانت لا تنحل إليها في أول الأمر إلى أجزاء بسيطة . ثمَّ الشرطية مشتملة على ما يتعلق بالشرط لا محالة؛ وذلك التعلق لا يخلو من أن يكون تعلق اللزوم وإنها متصلة حينئذ، أو تعلق العناد وإنها منفصلة، والتعلق لا يخلو عن أحدهما. فإن ما يتعلق بالشرط فذلك إما أن يكون لازم الوجود عند الشرط أو لازم العدم أو لا هذا ولا ذلك. والتعلق مما ينافي الثالث من الأقسام فيتعين الأول منها وهو «اللزوم» أو الثاني وهو العناد» كما في المتصلة والمنفصلة.
As for the conditional, its mention has already passed: what it is, how it is, and that it shares with categoricals in being susceptible to truth and falsity and is resolvable into them, although it is not initially resolved into simple parts. Then the conditional necessarily contains something related to the condition; that relation cannot be other than either a relation of implication (luzūm) – in which case it is conjunctive (muttasila) – or a relation of opposition (ʿinād) – in which case it is disjunctive (munfaṣila). The relation cannot be devoid of one of these. For what is related to the condition is either necessary in existence with the condition, or necessary in non-existence, or neither this nor that. The relation is incompatible with the third division, so the first (implication) or the second (opposition) is determined, as in the conjunctive and disjunctive.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage defines conditional propositions (al-sharṭiyya). The author first reminds the reader that conditionals were previously introduced: they are propositions that can be true or false, and they are ultimately reducible to categorical propositions, though not immediately decomposable into simple parts. The key point is that a conditional statement involves a relation (taʿalluq) between two parts (the antecedent and consequent, or the two disjuncts). This relation is always either one of implication (luzūm) or one of opposition (ʿinād). If it is implication, the proposition is called a conjunctive conditional (sharṭiyya muttasila). If it is opposition, it is a disjunctive conditional (sharṭiyya munfaṣila). There is no third possibility. The author then clarifies what these relations mean in terms of the condition: the consequent (or the related part) is either a necessary concomitant in existence, or a necessary concomitant in non-existence, or neither. The third option (neither necessary existence nor necessary non-existence) is incompatible with genuine relation (taʿalluq), so it is excluded. Thus the only valid types are those of implication (where the consequent is necessary given the condition) and opposition (where the disjuncts are mutually exclusive). This is the standard classification: conditionals are either muttasila (if-then) or munfaṣila (either-or).

Key Terms
شرطية
لزوم
عناد
متصلة
منفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) ijāb and salb
C) taʿalluq and luzūm
D) muttasila and munfasila

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are divided into conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or) based on whether the relation is implication or opposition.

Conjunctive and Disjunctive Conditionals: Definitions and Composition
Defines muttasila and munfasila and explains composition and terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) conditionals, and identify their antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in any conditional statement.

Core Idea
Every conditional proposition is defined by the relation between its two parts—implication or opposition—and those parts lose their independent truth value.

In this lesson, we learn the fundamental classification of conditional propositions in Islamic logic. The text defines two types: conjunctive (al-muttasila) and disjunctive (al-munfasila). It also explains that conditionals are composed of two propositions that become parts of a larger whole, losing their ability to be true or false independently. Understanding this will enable you to analyze any conditional statement in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate the definitions of muttasila and munfasila in the Arabic text. Notice the key difference: one involves luzum (implication), the other involves inad (incompatibility).
2
Examine the relation
Understand that in both types, the judgment is about the relation between the two parts, not about the truth of each part individually.
3
Analyze composition
The passage explains that conditionals are compounds of partial propositions. If the parts were independent, they would be categoricals. The conditional particle conjoins them into a single proposition where the parts are no longer independently truth-apt.
4
Learn the terminology
The first part is called muqaddam or malzum, the second is tali or lazim. Memorize these pairs as they recur throughout the logic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المتّصلة فهي التي يكون الحكم فيها بلزوم قضية لقضية أخرى موجبتين كانتا أو سالبتين أو مختلفتين كما هي من موجبة وسالبة أو من سالبة وموجبة. والمنفصلة هي ما يكون الحكم فيها بالعناد كذلك. فإن ما هو من الشرطيات متصلة كانت أو منفصلة، فإنها مركبة من المركبات الجزئية وإلا لكانت من الحمليّات فيكون الجزء الأول منها مركبًا والثاني كذلك، لكن المركب منهما وإن كانت قضية فلا تكون تلك القضية باقية على حالها عند الاتصال بحرف الشرط؛ إذ لو كانت باقية لكانت قابلة للتصديق والتكذيب، ولا تكون قابلة ضرورة شطرًا لقضية أخرى وهي الشرطية فالشطر الأول منهما يسمى مقدما وملزوما والثاني تاليا ولازمًا.
As for the conjunctive (conditional), it is that in which the judgment is of the implication of one proposition by another, whether they are both affirmative or both negative or different, as when one is affirmative and the other negative or one negative and the other affirmative. The disjunctive is that in which the judgment is of opposition [incompatibility] likewise. For what is among conditionals, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, they are composed of partial compositions; otherwise they would be among categoricals, so their first part would be composed and the second likewise, but the compound of them, even if it is a proposition, that proposition does not remain in its state when connected with the conditional particle; for if it remained, it would be capable of truth and falsity, but it is necessarily not capable of being a part of another proposition, i.e. the conditional. So the first part of them is called the antecedent and the implicated (malzum), and the second is called the consequent and the necessary.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of conditional propositions: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). It explains that in a conjunctive conditional, the judgment is about the implication (luzum) of one proposition by another, regardless of the quality of the parts. In a disjunctive, it is about opposition or incompatibility. Then it discusses the composition of conditionals: they are made up of partial propositions, but when they become parts of a conditional, they lose their independent truth-value status because they are now parts. It introduces the terminology: the first part is called muqaddam (antecedent) and malzum (implicated), and the second is tali (consequent) and lazim (necessary). This sets up the vocabulary for the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms

المنفصلة
لزوم
عناد
مقدّم
ملزوم
تالي
لازم

Checkpoint

A) The compatibility of two propositions
B) The implication (luzum) of one proposition by another
C) The opposition (inad) between two propositions
D) The truth value of each part

Key Takeaway
The two types of conditional propositions are defined by the relation between their parts: implication (luzum) for conjunctive and opposition (inad) for disjunctive.

Distinguishing Implicational vs Coincidental Conjunctives
Divides conjunctives into implicational and coincidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given conjunctive conditional is implicational or coincidental based on the necessity of implication.

Core Idea
The core of a conditional is whether the antecedent necessarily forces the consequent.

This lesson introduces the foundational division of conjunctive conditionals into two types: those where the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent (luzumiyya) and those where it does not (ittifaqiyya). Understanding this division is crucial for evaluating the logical strength of conditional statements used in Islamic theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Read the conditional statement and separate the first part (shart) from the second part (jawab). For example, in 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists,' the antecedent is 'the sun is shining' and the consequent is 'daytime exists'.
In Arabic, the conditional often uses 'kullamā' (whenever) or 'in' (if).
2
Determine if the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent
Ask: Is it impossible for the antecedent to be true without the consequent being true? If yes, the conditional is implicational (luzumiyya). If no, it is coincidental (ittifaqiyya). Consider real-world or conceptual necessity.
The author states: 'impossible for this to exist without that'.
3
Classify the conditional
Based on your assessment, label the conditional as either luzumiyya (implicational) or ittifaqiyya (coincidental). For instance, 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists' is luzumiyya because sunshine necessarily causes daytime.
Coincidental conditionals, like 'If the bell rings, then the door opens' (if they happen together by chance), are considered weak in logical proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في المتصلة والمتّصلة منهما إما لزومية وإما اتفاقية لما أن الشطر الأول منها لا يخلو من أن يكون مستلزما للثاني في التحقق على وجه لا يمكن أن يتحقق هذا دون ذلك، أو لا يكون. فإن كان مستلزما فهي اللزومية وإلا فهي الاتفاقية. أما اللزومية [أ] فإنها لا تخلو من أن يكون اللزوم فيها لزوما تاما وهو الذي يكون من الجانبين، [ب] أو لا يكون تاما وهو الذي يكون من جانب واحد. أما من الجانبين [1] فذلك مثل ما يكون المقدم منهما علة متحدة للتالي كما في قولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا»، [ب] أو معلولا متحدا له كما في قولنا كلما كان النهار موجودًا كانت الشمس طالعة»، [ت] أو معلولاً علته كما في قولنا كلما كان النهار موجودًا كان العالم مضيئًا»، [ث] أو متضايفا له كما في قولنا كلما كان زيد أبا عمر و فعمرو ابنه. وعلى هذا إذا قلنا كلما لم يكن النهار موجودًا لم يكن الشمس طالعة، وكلما لم تكن الشمس طالعة لم يكن النهار موجودًا هلم جرا. وأما من جانب واحد فذلك فيما يكون المقدم أخص من التالي كالشخص بالنسبة إلى الصنف والصنف بالنسبة إلى النوع والنوع بالنسبة إلى الجنس، والمشروط بالنسبة إلى الشرط، والكل بالنسبة إلى الجزء. وعلى هذا إذا قلنا من جانب العدم فإن عدم الصنف مما يستلزم عدم الشخص، وعدم النوع لعدم الصنف كذلك هلم جرا.
Section: On the conjunctive conditional. The conjunctive among them is either implicational (luzumiyya) or coincidental (ittifaqiyya), because the first part cannot but either imply the second in actuality in such a way that it is impossible for this to exist without that, or not. If it implies, it is implicational, otherwise coincidental. As for the implicational, it is not free from either the implication being complete, which is from both sides, or not complete, which is from one side. As for from both sides, that is like the antecedent being a unique cause of the consequent, as when we say, 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists,' or being a unique effect of it, as when we say, 'Whenever daytime exists, the sun is shining,' or being an effect of its cause, as when we say, 'Whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated,' or being correlatives, as when we say, 'Whenever Zayd is the father of 'Amr, then 'Amr is his son.' And likewise when we say, 'Whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not shining,' and 'Whenever the sun is not shining, daytime does not exist,' and so on. As for from one side, that is when the antecedent is more specific (akhass) than the consequent, like an individual relative to a species, a species relative to a genus, a species relative to a higher genus, a condition relative to the conditioned, a whole relative to the part. And similarly when we say from the side of non-existence, the non-existence of the species implies the non-existence of the individual, and the non-existence of the genus for the non-existence of the species, and so on.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This section focuses specifically on the conjunctive conditional. It divides it into two types based on whether the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent (luzumiyya) or not (ittifaqiyya). Then it further divides the implicational into complete (tamm) and incomplete (ghayr tamm). Complete implication occurs when the implication holds from both sides, meaning the antecedent and consequent are equivalent in existence/non-existence. Examples include cause-effect or correlative pairs. The incomplete implication is when the antecedent is akhass (more specific) than the consequent, so only one-way implication holds. The author gives examples from logical hierarchies, such as an individual to a species, or a condition to the conditioned. This is a key concept in understanding conditional propositions.

لزومية
اتفاقية

Which of the following is an implicational (luzumiyya) conjunctive conditional?
A) If the moon is full, then the cat is on the mat.
B) If the imam says 'Allahu Akbar', then the congregation follows.
C) If the water is pure, then it is permissible to use for ablution.
D) If the clock strikes twelve, then it is noon.

Key Takeaway
The primary division of conjunctive conditionals is between those where the antecedent necessitates the consequent (luzumiyya) and those where it does not (ittifaqiyya).

Complete vs Incomplete Implication in Conjunctives
Subdivides implicational conjunctives into complete and incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an implicational conjunctive conditional expresses complete (mutual) or one-sided implication based on the relationship between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
Complete implication means the antecedent and consequent are logically equivalent; incomplete means the antecedent is more specific than the consequent.

This lesson further explores the implicational conjunctive by dividing it into two subcategories: complete (tamm) where implication holds both ways, and incomplete (ghayr tamm) where implication is one-way. This distinction helps in understanding logical equivalence and hierarchy in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the implication is complete or incomplete
Check if the implication holds from the antecedent to the consequent AND from the consequent to the antecedent. If yes, it is complete (mutual implication). If no, it is incomplete (one-sided).
The author says: 'complete is that which is from both sides'.
2
For complete implication, identify the relationship
The author lists four types: cause-effect, effect-cause, effect of its cause (transitive), and correlatives. Classify the pair accordingly. For example, 'the sun shining causes daytime' is a cause-effect relation.
Only for complete implication are these relations valid.
3
For incomplete implication, check if the antecedent is more specific (akhass)
If the antecedent is more specific than the consequent (e.g., individual to species), then the implication is one-sided. The antecedent implies the consequent, but not vice versa.
The author gives examples: individual to species, species to genus, condition to conditioned, whole to part.

One-Sided Implication: Definition and Properties
Definition and properties of one-sided implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify one-sided implication in Arabic texts and determine which term is more specific (akhass) and which is more general (a'amm).

Core Idea
One-sided implication: the specific entails the general, but not vice versa.

This lesson tackles the concept of one-sided implication (al-luzum min janib wahid), a fundamental logical relation where the antecedent is more specific than the consequent. Understanding this asymmetry is crucial for analyzing conditional statements in Islamic sciences, especially in usul al-fiqh where the implication of conditions on rulings is assessed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Locate the two terms being compared in the statement of implication. The antecedent (muqaddam) is the one that implies, and the consequent (tali) is the one implied.
In one-sided implication, the antecedent is always more specific (akhass) than the consequent.
2
Determine the specificity relation
Check whether the antecedent is more specific than the consequent. The author gives examples: individual vs. species, species vs. genus, conditioned vs. condition, whole vs. part.
The more specific term entails the more general one without the converse holding.
3
Analyze non-existence implication
The author explains that from the side of non-existence, the non-existence of the more general (consequent) entails the non-existence of the more specific (antecedent). For example, the non-existence of the species entails the non-existence of the individual.
This is the contrapositive relation: if A implies B, then not-B implies not-A.
4
Understand the definitions of akhass and a'amm
The author defines 'more specific' (akhass) as that which entails the other without the converse, and 'more general' (a'amm) as that which is entailed by the other without the converse.
These definitions capture the asymmetry of one-sided implication.
5
Grasp the condition of actualization (tahaqquq)
The author states that implication is realized only when either the necessary (lazim) or the contradictory of the entailed (naqid al-malzum) is realized. This ensures that the implication relation is grounded in actual existence.
Implication is about actualization, not just essence; a term may be more specific in essence but equal in actualization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من جانب واحد فذلك فيما يكون المقدم أخص من التالي كالشخص بالنسبة إلى الصنف والصنف بالنسبة إلى النوع والنوع بالنسبة إلى الجنس، والمشروط بالنسبة إلى الشرط، والكل بالنسبة إلى الجزء. وعلى هذا إذا قلنا من جانب العدم فإن عدم الصنف مما يستلزم عدم الشخص، وعدم النوع لعدم الصنف كذلك هلم جرا. فالملزوم في كل صورة من هذه الصور أخص بالنسبة إلى اللازم ولا يكون هذا أخص من ذلك إلا وأن يكون عدم ذلك أخص من عدم هذا. والأخص هو المستلزم للغير من غير عكس، والأعم هو اللازم للغير من غير عكس. ثمَّ اللزوم لا يكون متحققا بين الأمرين إلا وأن يتحقق اللازم أو نقيض ملزومه؛ وذلك لأن اللازم لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا أو لا يكون. وإنما كان يتحقق أحدهما وهو إما اللازم أو نقيض الملزوم. وكذلك الملزوم لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا أو لا يكون فإنه يلزم من لزوم أيهما كان أحد ما ذكرنا من الأمرين. ثم اللازم لازم لأحد الأمرين أعني اللازم والملزوم بالضرورة فإن أحدهما معيناً لا يخلو من أن يكون لازما أو ملزوما. وكذلك نقيض الملزوم فإنه لازم لأحد النقيضين أعني نقيض اللازم ونقيض الملزوم فإن أحدهما لا يخلو من أن يكون نقيض اللازم أو نقيض الملزوم. ولا يقال كيف هو واللازم من الأمرين أخص من أحدهما وهو إما اللازم أو الملزوم. وكذلك نقيض الملزوم فإنه أخص من أحد النقيضين وهو إما نقيض اللازم أو نقيض الملزوم ولا يمكن أن يكون اللازم أخص من ملزومه وقد كان من المحال أن يتحقق الملزوم دون اللازم، فإن اللازم من أحد الأمرين. وإن كان أخص بالنسبة إلى أحدهما بحسب الذات فلا يكون أخص منه في التحقق بل يكون مساويًا له، واللزوم بحسب التحقق. وكذلك نقيض الملزوم بالنسبة إلى أحد النقيضين فإنه وإن كان أخص من أحدهما بحسب الذات فقد كان مساويًا بحسب التحقق كذلك.
As for one-sided [implication], that is when the antecedent is more specific than the consequent, like the individual in relation to the species, the species in relation to the genus, the genus in relation to the higher genus, the conditioned in relation to the condition, and the whole in relation to the part. And based on this, if we say from the side of non-existence: indeed the non-existence of the species entails the non-existence of the individual, and the non-existence of the genus [entails] the non-existence of the species, and so on. So the entailed (malzum) in every one of these forms is more specific relative to the necessary (lazim), and this is not more specific than that unless the non-existence of that is more specific than the non-existence of this. And the more specific is that which entails the other without converse, and the more general is that which is necessary for the other without converse. Then, necessity is not realized between two things unless the necessary or the contradictory of its entailed is realized; that is because the necessary is not free from being realized or not being realized. And only one of the two is realized, either the necessary or the contradictory of the entailed. Likewise, the entailed is not free from being realized or not being realized, for from the necessity of whichever of the two is [realized] one of the two things we mentioned follows. Then the necessary is necessary for one of the two things, namely the necessary and the entailed, necessarily; for one of them in particular is not free from being necessary or entailed. And likewise the contradictory of the entailed, for it is necessary for one of the two opposites, namely the contradictory of the necessary and the contradictory of the entailed; for one of them is not free from being the contradictory of the necessary or the contradictory of the entailed. And it is not said how it is, while the necessary of the two things is more specific than one of them, namely either the necessary or the entailed. And likewise the contradictory of the entailed, for it is more specific than one of the two opposites, namely either the contradictory of the necessary or the contradictory of the entailed, and it is not possible for the necessary to be more specific than its entailed, and it is impossible for the entailed to be realized without the necessary, for the necessary is one of the two things. And if it is more specific relative to one of them in essence, it is not more specific than it in actualization, but rather equals it, and necessity is according to actualization. And likewise the contradictory of the entailed relative to one of the two opposites, for even if it is more specific than one of them in essence, it is equal in actualization as well.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage defines one-sided implication (al-luzum min janib wahid) and explains its properties. The author begins by giving examples where the antecedent (muqaddam) is more specific (akhass) than the consequent (tali), such as the individual relative to the species, the species relative to the genus, etc. Then he discusses implication from the side of non-existence, where the non-existence of the species entails the non-existence of the individual. He then defines the more specific (akhass) as that which entails the other without the converse, and the more general (a'amm) as that which is entailed by the other without the converse. The passage continues to analyze the conditions of implication: for necessity to hold between two things, either the necessary (lazim) or the contradictory of the entailed (naqid al-malzum) must be realized. He explains that the necessary is always necessary for one of the two (lazim or malzum), and the contradictory of the entailed is necessary for one of the two opposites. He addresses a potential confusion: although the necessary may be more specific in essence than one of the two, in actualization (tahaqquq) it is equal to it, because necessity is according to actualization. This is a technical exposition of the logical relation of implication, emphasizing the asymmetry and the role of non-existence.

Key Terms
المقدم
التالي
أخص
الأعم
اللزوم

Checkpoint

A) Non-existence of the consequent entails non-existence of the antecedent.
B) Non-existence of the antecedent entails non-existence of the consequent.
C) Non-existence of the antecedent is independent of the consequent.
D) Non-existence of the consequent is more specific than non-existence of the antecedent.

Key Takeaway
One-sided implication is defined by specificity: the more specific entails the more general, and this relation is governed by actualization, not essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a case from usul al-fiqh where one term implies another unilaterally. For example, 'if the prayer is valid (sahih), then it has met its conditions (shurut).' Determine which term is more specific and explain the implication from the side of non-existence.

Coincidental Implication and Its Lack of Scientific Benefit
Author defines coincidental implication as mere accompaniment without necessary connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional proposition is a coincidental implication by checking for a necessary connection between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
Coincidental implication is mere accompaniment; science requires necessary connection.

In this passage, the author defines a type of conditional called 'coincidental implication' (al-ittifāqiyya). Unlike necessary implication, the antecedent and consequent have no real logical or causal link—they merely happen to occur together. Such propositions are useless in scientific reasoning because the mind cannot infer one from the other. Let's break down the criteria for recognizing this type of implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Locate the muqaddam (antecedent) and tālī (consequent) in the conditional. In the example 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying', the antecedent is 'man is rational' and the consequent is 'the donkey is braying'.
2
Check for a necessary connection
Ask: Does the existence of the antecedent actually entail the existence of the consequent? In coincidental implication, there is no such entailment; they are merely accompanied accidentally.
The author says: 'ليس بينهما تعلق يستدعي اللزوم' – no connection requiring implication.
3
Test two-sidedness or one-sidedness
Coincidental implication can be two-sided (both directions are accidental) or one-sided (one direction holds by coincidence but the reverse does not). The passage gives an example: if the king is rational, then man is rational (one-sided), but the reverse does not hold.
One-sided examples still lack real connection but are not used in sciences.
4
Determine scientific utility
If the mind cannot move from antecedent to consequent, the proposition is useless for proof. The author emphasizes that coincidental implications are not employed in the sciences.
The text says: 'لا فائدة في العلوم في مثل هذا اللزوم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاتفاقية فالمقدم فيها لا يتعلق بالتالي ولا التالي بالمقدم تعلقا يستدعي اللزوم بينهما على وجه يلزم من نفس وجود هذا وجود ذلك بل يتحقق بينهما مجرد مصاحبة على سبيل الاتفاق. وإن لم يكن الأمر في الطباع كذلك فيكون هذا لازما لذلك. وذلك لهذا كذلك كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقًا أي حال يفرض في المقدم وهو كون الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته إلا ويفرض فيه التالي وهو كون الحمار ناهقًا في ماهيته؛ فلا فائدة في العلوم في مثل هذا اللزوم لما أن الذهن لا ينتقل إلى التالي من وضع المقدم ولا إلى رفع المقدم من رفع التالي فاللزوم من الجانبين لازم وإن كان المتحقق منهما هو المقدم ليس إلا، ولو كان كذلك فلا تكون مستعملة في العلوم أصلا ولا يلزم أن يكون اللزوم فيها من الجانبين. فإن من الجائز أن يكون من جانب واحد كما يقال كلَّما كان الملك ناطقا في ماهيته كان الإنسان ناطقاً في ماهيته. فإن من جملة ما يكون من جانب واحد لما أن الإنسان في نفسه كان ينطق أن يكون ناطقا، ومثل هذا الاتفاق لا يدرى في الملك لما فيه من الكلام؛ فلا يقال إذن كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهية كان الملك ناطقا في ماهيته.
As for the coincidental, the antecedent in it is not connected to the consequent, nor the consequent to the antecedent, with a connection that necessitates implication between them such that the existence of this entails the existence of that; rather, between them there is mere accompaniment in a coincidental manner. And if the matter is not naturally so, then this is necessary for that, and that is [necessary] for this similarly, as in our statement: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' — meaning any state assumed in the antecedent, which is man's being rational in his essence, is such that the consequent, which is the donkey's being braying in its essence, is assumed in it. So there is no benefit in the sciences in such implication because the mind does not move from the positing of the antecedent to the [positing of] the consequent, nor to the removal of the antecedent from the removal of the consequent; so implication is necessary from both sides, even if what is realized of the two is only the antecedent. And if it were so, it would not be used in the sciences at all. And it is not necessary that the implication in them be from both sides, for it is possible that it is one-sided, as it is said: 'Whenever the king is rational in his essence, man is rational in his essence.' For it is among that which is one-sided, because man in himself is such that he would be rational; and such coincidence is not known regarding the king because of the speech in him; so it is not said: 'Whenever man is rational in essence, the king is rational in his essence.'
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage defines coincidental implication (al-ittifaqiyya). The author explains that in coincidental implication, there is no real connection between antecedent and consequent; rather, they merely accompany each other coincidentally. As an example, he gives 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' — there is no necessary connection between human rationality and donkey braying. Such implication is useless in the sciences because the mind cannot infer the consequent from the antecedent nor deny the antecedent from denying the consequent. However, the author notes that coincidental implication can be one-sided, as in 'Whenever the king is rational, man is rational' — here the implication holds only from the king's rationality to man's rationality, not vice versa, because man's rationality is an essential property that does not depend on the king's rationality. This passage distinguishes between genuine logical implication and mere accidental conjunction.

المقدم
التالي

Key Takeaway
Coincidental implication is not useful in the sciences because the mind cannot move from antecedent to consequent due to the lack of necessary connection.

Refuting Infinite Regress in Necessary Conditionals
Author answers objection that necessary conditionals lead to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why infinite regress in mental/relative matters is not impossible and identify the author's argument.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible in real entities, not in mental relations.

A common objection to necessary conditionals is that entailment chains would lead to an infinite regress, which is deemed impossible. The author responds by drawing a crucial distinction: necessity (luzum) is a relational attribute that exists only in the mind, not in external reality. Therefore, infinite regress in such mental considerations is not only possible but necessary. This refutation preserves the logical validity of conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector claims: If entailment is transitive, it would generate an infinite chain of entailments (tasalsul), which is impossible.
The objector assumes all infinite regresses are impossible.
2
Understand the Author's Counter
The author responds that infinite regress is not impossible here because necessity is a relative matter (amr nisbi) and a mental consideration (amr i'tibari).
Mental considerations have no external existence.
3
Grasp the Key Distinction
Distinguish between real entities (a'yan) which cannot have infinite regress and conceptual/relative entities which can. The rules that forbid infinite regress in physical chains do not apply to mental categories.
This is a standard principle in Islamic ontology.
4
Recognize the Implication
The argument shows that the infinite regress of entailments is a necessary consequence of relative matters, not a logical absurdity. Thus, necessary conditionals remain valid.
The objection fails because it misapplies the impossibility condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في هذا المقام لو كان كما ذكرتم لكان اللزوم من هذين الأمرين مما يستلزم اللزوم بين الأمرين الآخرين. وكذلك اللزوم بين ذينك الأمرين إلى غير النهاية وذلك محال. لكنا نقول إنه لا يكون محالا فيما نحن فيه فإن كون الشيء لازما للشيء من الأمور النسبية الإضافية، والأمور الإضافية من الأمور الاعتبارية فلا وجود لها في الأعيان على ما عرف من قبل. والتسلسل إلى غير النهاية في مثل هذه الأمور لا يكون من المستحيلات بل يكون من اللوازم وإلا لا تكون هذه الأمور من الأمور النسبية الإضافية.
And it may be said in this context: If it were as you mention, then the entailment from these two matters would necessitate the entailment between the other two matters, and likewise the entailment between those two matters ad infinitum, and that is impossible. However, we say: It is not impossible in what we are discussing, because the fact that something is necessary for something else is among relative matters, and relative matters are among mental considerations, so they have no existence in external entities, as previously known. And infinite regress in such matters is not among the impossible things, but rather among the necessary consequences; otherwise these matters would not be relative matters.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange within a commentary on necessary conditionals. An objector argues that if entailments are transitive, they would generate an infinite chain (tasalsul), which is impossible. The author refutes this by distinguishing between real existence and mental considerations. He explains that necessity (luzum) is a relational attribute (amr nisbi idafi), which exists only in the mind (amr i'tibari). Infinite regress in such conceptual relations is not impossible; on the contrary, it is a necessary feature of relative matters. Thus, the objection does not hold. The key point is that the impossibility of actual infinite regress in physical entities does not apply to mental categories.

Accidental Conditionals: Definition and Lack of Causal Benefit
Defines accidental conditionals and response to objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify accidental conditionals and explain why they are not useful for inference in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals lack a real connection between antecedent and consequent.

In logic, conditionals are supposed to show necessity. But some conditionals are merely accidental—the antecedent and consequent happen to coincide without any real link. This lesson explains how to recognize such conditionals and why they are useless for deriving knowledge in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of accidental conditionals
Look for the statement that the antecedent does not relate to the consequent in a way that requires entailment. The connection is mere concomitance (musahaba) by accident.
2
Contrast with necessary conditionals
Note that in accidental conditionals, the mind does not move from the antecedent to the consequent nor from denying the consequent to denying the antecedent. This lack of mental transfer is key.
3
Analyze the example given
Consider 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying.' The rationality of humans has no real connection to a donkey's braying. The mind cannot infer braying from rationality.
4
Understand one-sided entailment
Sometimes the entailment is only from one side, e.g., 'Whenever a king is rational, a human is rational.' The reverse is not true. This still counts as accidental on the other side.
5
Evaluate the objection about constant conjunction
The objection says if two things always occur together, doesn't that imply real necessity? The author replies that real necessity requires logical certainty (muqaddar), not just actual constant conjunction.
Accidental conditionals are not used in demonstrative sciences because they lack certain entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاتفاقية فالمقدم فيها لا يتعلق بالتالي ولا التالي بالمقدم تعلقا يستدعي اللزوم بينهما على وجه يلزم من نفس وجود هذا وجود ذلك بل يتحقق بينهما مجرد مصاحبة على سبيل الاتفاق. وإن لم يكن الأمر في الطباع كذلك فيكون هذا لازما لذلك. وذلك لهذا كذلك كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا أي حال يفرض في المقدم وهو كون الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته إلا ويفرض فيه التالي وهو كون الحمار ناهقا في ماهيته؛ فلا فائدة في العلوم في مثل هذا اللزوم لما أن الذهن لا ينتقل إلى التالي من وضع المقدم ولا إلى رفع المقدم من رفع التالي فاللزوم من الجانبين لازم وإن كان المتحقق منهما هو المقدم ليس إلا، ولو كان كذلك فلا تكون مستعملة في العلوم أصلا ولا يلزم أن يكون اللزوم فيها من الجانبين. فإن من الجائز أن يكون من جانب واحد كما يقال كلما كان الملك ناطقا في ماهيته كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته. فإن من جملة ما يكون من جانب واحد لما أن الإنسان في نفسه كان ينطق أن يكون ناطقا، ومثل هذا الاتفاق لا يدرى في الملك لما فيه من الكلام؛ فلا يقال إذن كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهية كان الملك ناطقا في ماهيته. ثم لقائل أن يقول في كل صورة من صور الاتفاقية إذا اتفق في الأمرين أن لا يفرض أحدهما في وقت إلا ويفرض الآخر منهما في ذلك الوقت كان الافتراق بينهما في التحقق من جملة المستحيلات، والافتراق مما يتحقق اللزوم الحقيقي فيكون اللزوم بينهما حقيقيا لا اتفاقيا. لكنا نقول هذا إذا كان الافتراق مقدرا، فإن المقدر من جملة القطعيات. فأما إذا لم يكن مقدرا فذلك وإن كان واقعا فلا يكون من القطعيات بل يكون من الاتفاقيات فيكون اللزوم بينهما كذلك.
As for the accidental (ittifaqiyya), in it the antecedent does not relate to the consequent nor the consequent to the antecedent in a way that necessitates entailment such that from the very existence of one the existence of the other follows; rather, mere concomitance occurs between them accidentally. And if it were not that way by nature, then this is necessary for that, and that is necessary for this. An example is our saying: 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying' i.e., in any assumption of the antecedent (human being rational in its essence) the consequent (donkey braying in its essence) is also assumed; so there is no benefit in the sciences from such entailment because the mind does not move from positing the antecedent to the consequent, nor from removing the antecedent from removing the consequent; so entailment from both sides is necessary even though the actual is only the antecedent. And if it were so, they would not be used in the sciences at all, and it is not necessary that entailment in them be from both sides. Indeed, it is possible that it be from one side, as is said: 'Whenever a king is rational in its essence, a human is rational in its essence.' For it is among things that are from one side, because the human in itself is rational; and such an accident is not known in the king because of the speech; so it is not said then: 'Whenever a human is rational in essence, a king is rational in essence.' Then one may say: In every form of accidental [conditional], if it happens that the two matters are such that one cannot be assumed at a time without the other being assumed at that same time, then separation between them in actuality is among impossibilities, and separation is what constitutes real entailment, so the entailment between them is real, not accidental. But we say: This is if the separation is assumed; the assumed is among certainties. As for when it is not assumed, then even if it occurs, it is not among certainties but among accidents, so the entailment between them is likewise.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage defines accidental conditionals (al-ittifaqiyya) and argues they are useless for scientific inference. Unlike necessary conditionals, the antecedent and consequent have no real connection; they simply happen to coincide. The classic example 'Whenever a human is rational, a donkey is braying' illustrates that the mind does not infer the braying from rationality. The passage also discusses one-sided entailment (e.g., from king's rationality to human rationality but not vice versa). An objection arises: if two things are always found together (cannot be separated), does that not prove real necessity? The author replies that only if the impossibility of separation is logically certain (muqaddar) does it indicate real necessity; if it is merely accidental but not known certainly, it remains an accidental conditional. Thus, accidental conditionals have no place in demonstrative science.

الاتفاقية
المقدم
التالي
المقدر

D) Because it violates the rules of categorical syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Accidental conditionals are defined by lack of necessary connection and are useless for scientific inference, even if the two matters are constantly conjoined.

Disjunctive Propositions: Real vs Non-Real and the Five Types
Author defines disjunctive propositions and enumerates five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of disjunctive propositions based on the antecedent-consequent relation and distinguish between real (haqiqiyya) and non-real (la haqiqiyya) disjunctions.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions are either exclusive (real) or inclusive (non-real), determined by the relation of the consequent to the contradictory of the antecedent.

In this passage, the author recalls the definition of a disjunctive proposition and then classifies it into five types according to how the consequent relates to the antecedent's contradictory. Understanding these types is crucial for correctly analyzing compound propositions in Islamic logic, especially in usul al-fiqh where disjunctions appear in conditional rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three basic forms of disjunction
The author says the judgment is about impossibility of conjunction in three ways: (1) both affirmation and negation, (2) affirmation only, (3) negation only. These correspond to exclusive, inclusive, and a third type not detailed here.
Focus on the two main types: exclusive (preventing both together and both absent) and inclusive (preventing only both together).
2
List the five possible compositions
The passage enumerates five compositions based on the relation of the consequent (tali) to the contradictory (naqid) of the antecedent: (A) direct contradictory, (B) equal to contradictory, (T) more specific than contradictory, (Th) more general than contradictory, (J) more specific in one respect and more general in another.
These compositions determine whether the disjunction is real (exclusive) or non-real (inclusive).
3
Match each composition to an example
For composition A: 'This number is either even or not even' (direct contradictory, exclusive). For B: 'This number is either even or odd' (odd equals not-even, exclusive). For T: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' (stone is more specific than 'non-tree', inclusive). The passage cuts off but the pattern continues similarly.
The examples show how the logical relation affects the truth conditions.
4
Distinguish real vs non-real
Real disjunctions (haqiqiyya) are those that prevent both conjunction (both true) and emptiness (both false) – they are exclusive. Non-real (la haqiqiyya) prevent only conjunction, allowing both to be false – they are inclusive. The first two compositions (A and B) yield real disjunctions; the third (T) yields non-real.
Remember: real = exclusive OR; non-real = inclusive OR (or just 'and/or').
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In fiqh, a disjunctive ruling like 'The act is either obligatory or prohibited' is real (exclusive) because both cannot be true and both cannot be false. An inclusive disjunction like 'The act is either recommended or permissible' allows both false if it is disliked.
This classification helps jurists and logicians analyze the logical structure of rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في المنفصلة ] أما المنفصلة فقد مر من قبل أنها هي التي تكون الحكم فيها بامتناع الاجتماع بين المقدم والتالي نفيا وإثباتا أو إثباتا لا نفيا أو نفيا لا إثباتا: وهذه بحسب ما يمكن أن تركيبها منه [أ] فإنه لا يمكن تركيبها إلا من قضية هي المقدم، ومن نقيضها وهو التالي [ب] أو منها ومما هو المساوي للنقيض [ت] أو منها ومما هو الأخص من النقيض، [ث] أو منها ومما هو الأعم من النقيض [ج] أو منها ومما هو الأخص من وجه، والأعم من وجه على الوجه الذي مر ذكره؛ فيكون الكل على خمسة أقسام. [أ] والمنفصلة الحقيقية منها وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو فهي في الأول منها كما يقال هذا العدد إما زوج وإما لا زوج لامتناع الاجتماع بين النقيضين نفيا وإثباتا، [ب] وفي الثاني منها كذلك كما يقال هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد؛ إذ الفردية مساوية للزوجية في الأعداد المعينة. [ت] واللا حقيقية منها وهي المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو فهي في الثالث من الأقسام كما يقال هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر؛ فإن اللا شجر الذي هو نقيض الشجر أعم من الحجر
Chapter: [On the Disjunctive] As for the disjunctive, it has previously passed that it is that in which the judgment is the impossibility of conjunction between the antecedent and consequent, either negatively and affirmatively, or affirmatively not negatively, or negatively not affirmatively. And these, according to what it can be composed of: [A] It cannot be composed except from a proposition which is the antecedent and its contradictory which is the consequent; [B] or from it and what is equal to the contradictory; [T] or from it and what is more specific than the contradictory; [Th] or from it and what is more general than the contradictory; [J] or from it and what is more specific in one respect and more general in another respect, in the manner previously mentioned. So the whole is five divisions. [A] The real (haqiqiyya) disjunctive among them is that which prevents both conjunction and emptiness, and it is the first of them, as is said: 'This number is either even or not even' due to the impossibility of conjunction between the two contradictories negatively and affirmatively. [B] And in the second of them likewise, as is said: 'This number is either even or odd'; since oddness is equal to evenness in determinate numbers. [T] The non-real (la haqiqiyya) among them is that which prevents conjunction but not emptiness, and it is in the third of the divisions, as is said: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone'; for non-tree, which is the contradictory of tree, is more general than stone.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section on disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila). It first recalls that a disjunctive asserts the impossibility of conjunction between antecedent and consequent in three ways: (1) impossibility of both together and both absent (exclusive), (2) impossibility of both together only (inclusive), (3) impossibility of both absent only (not mentioned here but implicit). The passage then lists five possible compositions of the disjunction, based on the logical relation between the antecedent and the negation of the consequent (or vice versa). The five are: [A] antecedent and its direct contradictory (e.g., even vs not even) giving the real exclusive disjunction; [B] antecedent and equal to the contradictory (e.g., even vs odd, since oddness is equal to evenness as a property of numbers); [T] antecedent and something more specific than the contradictory; [Th] antecedent and something more general than the contradictory; [J] antecedent and something more specific in one way and more general in another. The passage then gives concrete examples: the real disjunctive (haqiqiyya) is like 'This number is either even or not even' (exclusive); the second type is like 'This number is either even or odd' (still exclusive because oddness is equal to not-evenness). The third type (non-real) is illustrated with 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' — here 'stone' is more specific than 'non-tree', so the disjunction is inclusive (it allows both to be absent if the thing is neither). The text cuts off here but the pattern is clear.

المانعة من الجمع والخلو
المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو
الحقيقية
اللاحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'This number is either even or odd.'
B) 'This thing is either a tree or a stone.'
C) 'The prayer is either valid or invalid.'
D) 'The liquid is either water or oil.'

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive proposition is classified by the logical relation between the antecedent and the consequent's contradictory, yielding five types; the real (exclusive) and non-real (inclusive) are the two main categories relevant for Islamic logical analysis.
