The Fourth and Fifth Types of Disjunctive Propositions
Explains the fourth and fifth divisions of disjunctive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth type of disjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع) and explain why the fifth division is not a true disjunction.

Core Idea
Not every seeming disjunction is real; some allow both parts to coexist.

In this passage, the author extends the classification of disjunctive propositions beyond the three standard types. You will learn the fourth type, which prevents emptiness but allows conjunction, and the fifth division, which appears in rational division but fails the test of a true disjunction because its parts can both be true or both false. Understanding these distinctions sharpens your ability to evaluate compound propositions in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth type's characteristics
The fourth type is 'exclusive disjunction with emptiness without conjunction' (المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع). It means the two parts cannot both be false (emptiness is prevented) but can both be true (conjunction is allowed). The author calls it non-real (لاحقيقية). Locate the sentence where this is stated.
The example given: 'this thing is either not a stone or a body'.
2
Understand how negation transforms the third type into the fourth
The author explains that if the parts of the third type (exclusive with conjunction without emptiness) are negated, it becomes the fourth type. For example, 'either not a tree or not a stone' becomes a disjunction where both cannot be false (if both false, then tree and stone would be true simultaneously, which is impossible) but can both be true (if the thing is neither tree nor stone).
This shows the symmetry between types via negation.
3
Examine the fifth division example
The author gives: 'this thing is either moving or a body'. Note that a thing can be both moving and a body (e.g., a man) or neither (e.g., an intellect). Thus, the two parts can be both true or both false.
This is contrasted with true disjunctions where such coexistence is restricted.
4
Determine why the fifth division is not a true disjunction
A true disjunctive (منفصلة) requires that the parts cannot both be true and/or both be false (depending on type). Since the fifth division allows both conjunction and emptiness, it does not qualify as a disjunctive proposition in the logical sense.
The author says 'it is not among disjunctives' (لَا يَكُونُ ذَلِكَ مِنَ المنفصلات).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون الحجر أخص منه وإنه مما ينا في الاجتماع بين الشجر والحجر إثباتًا لا نفيا؛ إذ الأخص مما يمتنع وجوده بدون الأعم. أما الأعم فلا يمتنع وجوده بدون الأخص يمكن أن يكون الشيء لا شجرًا ولا حجرًا كالإنسان. وأما المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع وهي اللاحقيقية أيضًا فإنها في الرابع من الأقسام كما يقال هذا الشيء إما لا حجر وإما جسم؛ فإن الحجر الذي هو نقيض اللا حجر أخص من الجسم فيكون الجسم أعم منه ؛ وإنه مما ينافي الاجتماع بين اللا حجر والجسم نفيا لا إثباتًا لما مرَّ في المانعة من الجمع فيمكن أن يكون الشيء الواحد جسما ولا حجرًا كالحيوان لكن لا يمكن أن لا يكون جسما ولا يكون لا حجرًا أيضًا؛ وإنه إذا كان حجرًا كان جسما بالضرورة. ثم الثالث من الأقسام وهو المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو إذا أورد على جزئها السلب فهو الرابع منها كما يقال هذا الشيء إما لا شجرًا وإما لا حجرًا؛ إذ هي المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع حينئذ لامتناع أن يكون الشيء الواحد شجرًا وحجرًا معا، لكن لا يمتنع أن يكون لا شجرًا أو لا حجرًا كما مر . وأما الخامس من الأقسام فإنه وإن كان معتبراً في القسمة العقلية بالنظر إلى العمومية والخصوصية فلا يكون ذلك من المنفصلات لصحة الاجتماع بين الطرفين في النفي والإثبات كما يقال مثلا هذا الشيء إما متحرك وإما جسم فالجسم هو الأعم من المتحرّك من حيث إنه يكون متحركًا ولا متحركًا. وكذلك المتحرك بالنسبة إلى الجسم لكنه لا يستحيل أن يكون الشيء الواحد متحركًا وجسما كالإنسان، ولا متحرّكًا ولا جسمًا كالعقل. ثم لقائل أن يقول في التركيب من القضية ومما هو المساوي للنقيض أنه لا يمكن لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء مساويًا للنقيض، والمساوي للنقيض مما يحقق الواسطة بين النقيضين؛ إذ هو الواسطة ولا واسطة بينهما البتة. لكنا نقول المعنى من النقيض في هذه الصورة هو الذي يطلق عليه اسم النقيض لا حقيقية كما مر . وإن قيل هب أنه كذلك لكن التركيب منها ومما هو الأخص من وجه والأعم من وجه لا يمكن، فإن من المحال أن يكون الشيء بالنسبة إلى الشيء الآخر أخص من وجه وأعم من وجه. فنقول: هذا مسلم وقد مرّ الكلام فيه لكن المراد منه ما هو الأعم بحسب القسمة التي مرَّ ذكرها لا ما هو الأعم في الحقيقة، وكذلك الأخص فإنه بحسب القسمة أيضًا يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
Thus 'stone' is more specific than it, and it prevents conjunction between tree and stone in affirmation not in negation; since the more specific cannot exist without the more general. As for the more general, its existence without the more specific is not impossible; a thing can be neither tree nor stone, like man. As for the exclusive disjunction with emptiness without conjunction, which is also non-real, it is the fourth of the divisions, as it is said: this thing is either not a stone or a body; for stone, which is the contradictory of not-stone, is more specific than body, so body is more general than it; and it prevents conjunction between not-stone and body in negation not in affirmation, as previously in the exclusive disjunction with conjunction, so a single thing can be a body and not a stone, like an animal, but it cannot be not a body and not not-a-stone; and if it is a stone, it is necessarily a body. Then the third division, which is the exclusive disjunction with conjunction without emptiness, if its part is negated, it becomes the fourth, as it is said: this thing is either not a tree or not a stone; for it is then the exclusive disjunction with emptiness without conjunction, because it is impossible for a single thing to be both tree and stone, but it is not impossible that it be neither a tree nor a stone, as previously. As for the fifth division, even though it is considered in the rational division with respect to generality and specificity, it is not among disjunctives because of the possibility of coexistence of the two terms in negation and affirmation, as for example it is said: this thing is either moving or a body; for body is more general than moving in that it can be moving or not moving. Likewise moving with respect to body, but it is not impossible for a single thing to be both moving and a body like man, or neither moving nor a body like intellect. Then an objector may say regarding composition from a proposition and what is equal to the contradictory: it is impossible because it is impossible for a thing to be equal to the contradictory, and what is equal to the contradictory establishes a middle between contradictories; for it is the middle, and there is absolutely no middle between them. But we say: the meaning of 'contradictory' in this form is that which is called 'contradictory' in name, not in reality, as previously. And if it is said: suppose it is so, but composition from it and from what is more specific in one respect and more general in another is not possible, for it is impossible for a thing to be more specific in one respect and more general in another respect to another thing. We say: this is granted, and the discussion about it has already passed, but what is meant is what is more general according to the division mentioned, not what is more general in reality; likewise the more specific is also known by reflection according to the division, God willing.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of disjunctive propositions and responds to an objection. The author first explains the fourth type of disjunction: the exclusive disjunction with emptiness without conjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع). This is a non-real disjunction where the two parts cannot both be false but can both be true. The example: 'This thing is either not a stone or a body.' The author shows that not-stone is the contradictory of stone, and stone is more specific than body, so body is more general. This disjunction prevents the conjunction of not-stone and body in denial (i.e., they cannot both be false) but allows conjunction in affirmation (they can both be true). For instance, an animal can be a body and not a stone, but it cannot be not a body and not not-a-stone. Then the author notes that the third type (exclusive with conjunction without emptiness) becomes the fourth when its parts are negated. The fifth division, although considered in rational division, is not a true disjunction because the two terms can coexist in both affirmation and negation, as in 'this thing is either moving or a body' – a thing can be both moving and a body (e.g., man) or neither (e.g., an intellect). The passage then addresses an objection: composition from a proposition and its equal to the contradictory is impossible. The answer distinguishes between contradictory in name and in reality. A further objection about composition from something more specific in one respect and more general in another is answered by clarifying that the division is not based on real generality but on the specific logical division being discussed.

Key Terms
المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع (al-mani'atu 'ani al-khuluw duuna al-jam'): A disjunction that prevents emptiness (i.e., both parts cannot be false) but allows conjunction (both can be true); it is called non-real (lā ḥaqīqiyyah). [Note: No significant shift – this classification is standard in later logic texts.]
المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو (al-mani'atu 'ani al-jam' duuna al-khuluw): A disjunction that prevents conjunction (both parts cannot be true) but allows emptiness (both can be false); it is the real exclusive disjunction. [Note: This is the standard 'exclusive disjunction' in Islamic logic.]
المساوي للنقيض (al-musāwī lil-naqīḍ): Something equal to the contradictory of a proposition; according to the author, this is only in name, not in reality, and is used to discuss composition. [Note: This concept appears in polemical contexts; not a standard term in all works.]
المنفصلة (al-munfaṣilah): Disjunctive proposition; a compound conditional statement that asserts a disjunction between two or more parts. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what prevents the fifth division from being a true disjunction (منفصلة)?
A) It has more than two parts.
B) Its parts are not mutually exclusive and can coexist in affirmation and negation.
C) It is missing the condition of generality.
D) It is used in rational division but not logical division.

Key Takeaway
The fifth division is not a true disjunctive because its parts can be both true or both false, unlike real disjunctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the statement 'Either this animal is a horse or a quadruped' is a real or non-real disjunction, and classify it according to the types discussed in the passage. Explain your reasoning.
?
Construct an original example of a disjunction from fiqh (e.g., 'Either the prayer is valid or invalid in terms of intention') and determine which of the five divisions it belongs to.

Propositions

Resolving Objections on Composition in Disjunctive Propositions
Responds to objections on composition with equal to contradictory and relative generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection against composition with what is 'equal to the contradictory' and the author's response, and apply the distinction between real and nominal terms.

Core Idea
What seems logically impossible may be possible when terms are taken nominally rather than real.

The passage addresses an objector who argues that composition from a proposition and something equal to the contradictory is impossible because it would create a middle between contradictories. The author responds by distinguishing between 'contradictory' in name and in reality. A further objection about relative generality/specificity is also resolved by referring to the specific division used. This demonstrates how precise terminology in logic prevents false objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's first claim
Find the sentence starting with 'then an objector may say' (ثم لقائل أن يقول). The objector argues that composition from a proposition and what is equal to the contradictory is impossible because it would create a middle between contradictories, which cannot exist.
The objector assumes 'equal to the contradictory' implies a real middle term.
2
State the author's first response
The author replies that the term 'contradictory' here is used in name only, not in reality. In other words, the phrase 'equal to the contradictory' does not refer to an actual logical contradictory but to a term that is called contradictory in a technical sense.
This saves the possibility of composition without violating the law of non-contradiction.
3
Identify the objector's second claim
The objector then objects that composition from something that is more specific in one respect and more general in another is impossible, because a thing cannot be both more specific and more general relative to another.
This targets the relative nature of the division in the author's classification.
4
Explain the author's response to the second objection
The author grants the objection but clarifies that 'more general' and 'more specific' in his division are not real (haqiqi) but are according to the specific logical division being considered. Thus, relative generality/specificity is allowed within that framework.
This shows that logical classifications are contextual and must be understood within their proper scope.

Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions by Number of Parts
Author classifies disjunctive propositions by number of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a real disjunctive proposition based on the number of its parts (two, finite, or infinite) and identify examples for each type.

Core Idea
A real disjunctive proposition can have two, a finite number, or an infinite number of parts.

In this section, the author lays out the fundamental classification of true disjunctive propositions (al-munfaṣilah al-ḥaqīqiyyah) according to how many disjuncts they contain. This is a memorization anchor for later work on disjunctive syllogisms, where the number of parts affects how we reason from them. Understanding this classification will help you recognize the structure of disjunctions in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories
The passage divides disjunctions into those with two parts, finite parts, and infinite parts. Commit these three categories to memory.
2
Focus on the two-part type and its sub-varieties
Within the two-part disjunction, the author gives examples of both affirmatives, both negatives, and mixed. For example: 'this number is either even or odd' (both affirmative). Recognize the pattern.
3
Examine the finite-part type with examples
The passage gives examples from numbers: tripartite (even-even, even-odd, even-even and odd) and quadripartite (adding 'odd'). Understand that this can extend to any finite number.
4
Consider the infinite-part type
The passage gives the example of listing even numbers ad infinitum, and mentions shapes with infinite sides/angles. Note that the series continues indefinitely.
5
Note the common feature: all types allow affirmative/negative/mixed parts
The author explicitly states that the second and third types also allow these variations, just like the first. This is a unifying observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في أقسام المنفصلة أما المنفصلة فالحقيقية منها قد تكون ذات جزأين، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية، والأجزاء كلها من القضايا التي مرَّ ذكرها في الحمليات والشرطيات فالأول إما من موجبتين كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، أو من سالبتين كقولنا هذا العدد إما لا زوج وإما لا فرد، أو من مختلفتين كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما لا زوج. والثاني إما من الثلاث كقولنا هذا الزوج إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج و فرد، وإما من الأربع كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج وفرد وإما فرد. وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى الأقسام المحصورة في العدد أي عدد كان من بعد والثالث مثل قولنا هذا الزوج إما الأول من الأزواج وإما الثاني منها وإما الثالث هلم جرا إلى غير النهاية. وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى الفرد والعدد التام والناقص وغير ذلك. وكذلك بالنسبة إلى الأشكال والأضلاع والزوايا فإنها من الأمور التي لا نهاية لها. والأجزاء في الثاني والثالث يمكن أن تكون موجبة ويمكن أن تكون سالبة ويمكن أن تكون مختلفة كما في الأول؛ وهذا ظاهر يعرف بالتأمل.
Section: On the types of the disjunctive proposition. As for the disjunctive proposition, the real [true] disjunction among them may have two parts, may have a finite number of parts, and may have an infinite number of parts. All the parts are from the propositions previously mentioned in categoricals and conditionals. The first [type with two parts] is either from two affirmatives, like our saying: 'this number is either even or odd'; or from two negatives, like our saying: 'this number is either not even or not odd'; or from two different [affirmative and negative], like our saying: 'this number is either even or not even'. The second [type with finite parts] is either from three, like our saying: 'this number [the number 2?] is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even and odd'; or from four, like our saying: 'this number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even and odd, or odd'. And so on with respect to the finite divisions in number, any number it may be. The third [type with infinite parts] is like our saying: 'this number is either the first of the evens, or the second of them, or the third, and so on ad infinitum'. And so on with respect to odd numbers and perfect and deficient numbers and others. Likewise with respect to shapes, sides, and angles, for they are among things that have no end. The parts in the second and third types can be affirmative, can be negative, and can be different, as in the first; and this is clear and known by reflection.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a classification of disjunctive propositions (المنفصلة) into three types based on the number of parts. The author, likely from a matn text, lays out the division concisely for memorization. First, a real disjunction (الحقيقية) can have two parts, a finite number of parts, or an infinite number of parts. Each part itself is a proposition—either categorical or conditional as earlier studied. The two-part disjunction (الأول) can be composed of two affirmatives (e.g., 'this number is either even or odd'), two negatives (e.g., 'this number is either not even or not odd'), or one affirmative and one negative (e.g., 'this number is either even or not even'). The finite-part disjunction (الثاني) can have three or four parts, as in the examples about numbers: 'either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even and odd' (this is a tripartite division), or 'either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even and odd, or odd' (quadripartite). The author notes that this can be extended to any finite number. The infinite-part disjunction (الثالث) uses an unbounded series, like listing the even numbers one after another, or considering shapes with infinite sides or angles. Finally, the author remarks that the parts in the second and third types can also be affirmative, negative, or mixed, just like the first type. This classification is essential for understanding the structure of disjunctive syllogisms.

Key Terms
المنفصلة الحقيقية (al-munfaṣilah al-ḥaqīqiyyah): The true disjunction, which is the real exclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع والخلو) that prevents both conjunction and emptiness; here it is classified by number of parts. [Note: In some texts, 'حقيقية' distinguishes exclusive from inclusive; here it is the disjunction under discussion.]
جزأين (juz'ayn): Two parts; the disjunction has two disjuncts. [Note: No shift – standard term.]
أجزاء غير متناهية (ajzā' ghayr mutanāhiyah): Infinite parts; the disjunction has an unlimited sequence of disjuncts. [Note: Standard concept in logic for non-finite disjunctions.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'This number is either even or odd.'
B) 'This number is either even-even or even-odd or even-even and odd.'
C) 'This number is either the first even, or the second even, or the third, and so on.'
D) 'This action is either obligatory or forbidden.'

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive propositions are classified into two-part, finite-part, and infinite-part types, each of which can have affirmative, negative, or mixed constituents.

True Exclusive Disjunctives: Reduction to Two Contradictories
Classifies true exclusive disjunctives and reduces them to two contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify haqiqi disjunctives by number of parts and explain why they ultimately reduce to two contradictory parts.

Core Idea
Every true exclusive disjunction rests on two contradictory parts, regardless of apparent multiplicity.

The author now classifies disjunctive propositions into two main categories: true exclusive (haqiqi) and non-exclusive (la-haqiqi). In this lesson, we focus on the haqiqi type, which prevents both parts being true together and both being false together. The author shows that while haqiqi disjunctives can have two, finite, or infinite parts, in reality they always reduce to a fundamental two-part contradictory structure.

Guided Path
1
Define the haqiqi disjunctive
Understand that 'haqiqi' means man' al-jam' wa man' al-khuluw: both disjuncts cannot be true together nor false together.
This is the defining characteristic of true exclusive disjunction.
2
Identify the three categories by number of parts
The author gives examples: two parts (both affirmative, both negative, or mixed), finite parts (e.g., three or four), and infinite parts (e.g., enumerating the evens).
The categories are based on the number of apparent disjuncts.
3
Examine the examples
Note the specific examples: for two parts - 'this number is either even or odd' (affirmatives), 'either not even or not odd' (negatives), 'either even or not even' (mixed). For finite: classifications of 'even' numbers. For infinite: 'this even is the first even, second, third...'
All examples use numbers and shapes, illustrating abstract logical structure.
4
Understand the reduction to two contradictories
The author clarifies that a haqiqi disjunctive with many parts is only apparently multiple; in actuality, it must have two contradictory parts. The multiple parts are potential, not actual.
This is a key insight: true exclusive disjunction is fundamentally binary.

فصل: في أقسام المنفصلة. أما المنفصلة فالحقيقية منها قد تكون ذات جزأين، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية، والأجزاء كلها من القضايا التي مرَّ ذكرها في الحمليات والشرطيات. فالأول إما من موجبتين كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، أو من سالبتين كقولنا هذا العدد إما لا زوج وإما لا فرد، أو من مختلفتين كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما لا زوج. والثاني إما من الثلاث كقولنا هذا الزوج إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج و فرد، وإما من الأربع كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج و فرد وإما فرد. وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى الأقسام المحصورة في العدد أي عدد كان من والزوايا فإنها من الأمور التي لا نهاية لها. والأجزاء في الثاني والثالث يمكن أن تكون موجبة ويمكن أن تكون سالبة ويمكن أن تكون مختلفة كما في الأول؛ وهذا ظاهر يعرف بالتأمل. ثم إنها وهي الحقيقية لا تكون محققة بالذات إلا وأن تكون ذات جزأين متناقضين لكن يمكن أن يكون أحدها أو كل واحد منهما منقسمًا إلى القسمين فتحصل من الأجزاء ثلاثة أو أربعة مثلا فتكون في الظاهر ذات أجزاء كثيرة أما في نفس الأمر لا يكون كذلك بل هي منفصلات حقيقية بالقوة مترتبة بعضها على البعض بوجه لا يمكن أن يتحقق بعضها بالفعل إلا وأن تكون ذات جزأين متناقضين. وأما اللاحقيقية وهي المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو؛ فإنها قد تكون ذات جزأين وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية. فالأول مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر، والثاني مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر وإما مدر، والثالث مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما جزء شجر وإما جزء جزء شجر إلى غير النهاية، وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى الحجر وغيره. ولا يقال كيف هو وأجزاؤها وجودية فإن حاصلها راجع إلى ذكر الجزئيات المندرجة تحت نقيض الشيء في مقابلة ذلك الشيء؛ ولا نهاية لتلك الجزئيات فإن منها ما يكون متناهيا ومنها ما لا يكون متناهيا كما مر فيما مرَّ: وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو. وإن قيل إنها إذا كانت مندرجة تحت النقيض كان النقيض محمولا على كل واحد منها ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك وإلا لكان النقيض متعددًا. فنقول: المعنى من النقيض في هذه الصورة هو اللا شجر مثلا في قولنا هذا إما شجر وإما حجر، واللا شجر متعدد ومحمول على جميع ما عدا الشجر فردًا فردًا. ولأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مندرجا تحت الشيء أن يكون ذلك الشيء محمولا عليه كيف كان، بل يكون محمولا بوجه دون وجه. ألا ترى أن الشجر مندرج تحت الجسم والجسم مشترك بينه وبين الحجر مثلا. ومع هذا يصح أن يحمل الجسم عليه ولا يصح أن يحمل عليه.
Section: On the Divisions of the Disjunctive. As for the disjunctive, its true [exclusive] type may have two parts, may have finite parts, and may have infinite parts, and all the parts are from the propositions mentioned earlier in categoricals and conditionals. The first [type with two parts] is either from two affirmatives, like our saying 'this number is either even or odd', or from two negatives, like our saying 'this number is either not even or not odd', or from two different [one affirmative one negative], like our saying 'this number is either even or not even'. The second [type with finite parts] is either from three, like our saying 'this number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even-and-odd', or from four, like our saying 'this number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even-and-odd, or odd'. And so on with respect to the finite divisions in number, whatever number it may be afterwards. The third [type with infinite parts] is like our saying 'this number is either the first of the numbers, or the second of them, or the third, and so on without end'. And similarly for odd, perfect number, imperfect number, etc. And likewise for shapes, sides, and angles, for they are things without end. And the parts in the second and third can be affirmative, can be negative, and can be different as in the first; this is apparent by reflection. Then these true [types], they are not actualized in themselves unless they have two contradictory parts, but it is possible that one of them or each of them is divisible into two divisions, so three or four parts result, for example, so they appear to have many parts, but in actuality it is not so, rather they are potential true disjunctives arranged one after another in a way that some cannot be actualized except by having two contradictory parts. As for the non-true [type], which is the exclusive without inclusive [i.e., man' al-jam' without man' al-khuluw], it may have two parts, may have finite parts, and may have infinite parts. The first is like our saying 'this thing is either a tree or a stone'; the second is like our saying 'this thing is either a tree or a stone or clay'; the third is like our saying 'this thing is either a tree or a part of a tree or a part of a part of a tree without end', and similarly for stone and other things. And one should not say: how is that? For its parts are existential, since their sum total reverts to mentioning the particulars subsumed under the contradictory of the thing in contrast to that thing; and there is no end to those particulars, for among them are finite and infinite, as has passed earlier: it is the exclusive without inclusive. And if it is said: if they are subsumed under the contradictory, then the contradictory is predicated of each one of them, and it cannot be so, otherwise the contradictory would be multiple. We say: the meaning of 'contradictory' in this form is 'not-tree', for example, in our saying 'this is either a tree or a stone', and 'not-tree' is multiple and predicated of everything besides tree, individually. And because it does not follow from something being subsumed under something else that that thing is predicated of it in any manner, rather it is predicated in one aspect and not another. Do you not see that 'tree' is subsumed under 'body', and 'body' is common between it and 'stone', for example. Yet body is correctly predicated of it but not correctly predicated of [the stone].
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila). The author first divides them into true exclusive (haqiqi) and non-exclusive (la-haqiqi), which is the type that prevents conjunction (man' al-jam') but not absence of both (man' al-khuluw). For each type, he explains that they can have two, finite, or infinite parts.

He begins with the haqiqi type. With two parts, the parts can be both affirmative (e.g., 'this number is either even or odd'), both negative (e.g., 'this number is either not even or not odd'), or mixed (e.g., 'this number is either even or not even'). With finite parts, he gives examples with three or four parts involving number classifications (even-even, even-odd, etc.) and explains that it applies to any finite number of divisions. With infinite parts, he uses the example of 'this even number is either the first even, the second, the third...' and extends it to odd numbers, perfect numbers, shapes, etc.

He then clarifies that even when a haqiqi disjunctive appears to have many parts, in reality it must reduce to two contradictory parts because true exclusive disjunction only holds between two contradictories. The multiple parts are only potential; they are arranged such that only two contradictory disjuncts are actual at a time.

Then he turns to the non-haqiqi type (man' al-jam' only). It also has three possibilities: two parts (e.g., 'this is either a tree or a stone'), finite parts (adding 'clay'), or infinite parts (e.g., 'this is either a tree or a part of a tree or a part of a part...'). He addresses an objection: if all the parts other than the first are subsumed under the contradictory (e.g., 'not-tree'), then the contradictory would be predicated of each, making it multiple, which seems impossible. He responds by explaining that in such disjunctives, 'naqid' (contradictory) is understood in a broader sense, like 'lā shajar' (not-tree), which includes many things and can be predicated of each individually. He gives the analogy of 'tree' being subsumed under 'body' but 'body' correctly predicated of tree but not of stone—similarly, 'not-tree' is predicated of stone in a specific way. This resolves the confusion.

Key Terms
منفصلة
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It has only two parts.

C) It prevents conjunction but allows absence of both.
D) It can only have affirmative parts.

Key Takeaway
A true exclusive disjunction always reduces to two contradictory parts, even if it appears to have many.

Non-Exclusive Disjunctives and the Broader Contradictory
Classifies non-exclusive disjunctives and addresses an objection about the contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify non-exclusive (la-haqiqi) disjunctives and explain how multiple parts can be subsumed under a single broader contradictory without logical error.

Core Idea
Non-exclusive disjunctives allow both false; their parts are particulars under a single broader contradictory.

Having covered the true exclusive disjunctive, the author now turns to the non-exclusive type (la-haqiqi), which only prevents two parts from being true together but allows both to be false. He again divides it into two, finite, and infinite parts. A potential objection arises: if all parts except the first fall under the contradictory, the contradictory would be multiple. The author provides a nuanced response, clarifying the broader sense of 'contradictory' in this context.

Guided Path
1
Define the la-haqiqi disjunctive
Understand that 'la-haqiqi' means man' al-jam' only: it prevents both disjuncts from being true together but allows both to be false.
This is also called exclusive without inclusive.
2
Identify the three categories by number of parts
As with haqiqi, the author gives examples: two parts ('this is either a tree or a stone'), finite parts ('tree, stone, or clay'), and infinite parts ('tree, or part of tree, or part of part...').
The parts are all positive existential terms.
3
Understand the objection and the response
An objector argues: if all parts other than the first are subsumed under the contradictory (e.g., 'not-tree'), then the contradictory would be predicated of each, making it multiple, which seems impossible. The author responds that 'naqid' here is used in a broader sense (e.g., 'not-tree' as the set of all non-tree things) and can be predicated of each part individually without contradiction, just as 'body' is common to tree and stone but predicated in different ways.
This clarifies that the contradictory in a la-haqiqi disjunctive is not a single predicate but a collection of all else.

Section: On the Divisions of the Disjunctive. As for the disjunctive, its true [exclusive] type may have two parts, may have finite parts, and may have infinite parts, and all the parts are from the propositions mentioned earlier in categoricals and conditionals. The first [type with two parts] is either from two affirmatives, like our saying 'this number is either even or odd', or from two negatives, like our saying 'this number is either not even or not odd', or from two different [one affirmative one negative], like our saying 'this number is either even or not even'. The second [type with finite parts] is either from three, like our saying 'this number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even-and-odd', or from four, like our saying 'this number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even-and-odd, or odd'. And so on with respect to the finite divisions in number, whatever number it may be afterwards. The third [type with infinite parts] is like our saying 'this number is either the first of the numbers, or the second of them, or the third, and so on without end'. And similarly for odd, perfect number, imperfect number, etc. And likewise for shapes, sides, and angles, for they are things without end. And the parts in the second and third can be affirmative, can be negative, and can be different as in the first; this is apparent by reflection. Then these true [types], they are not actualized in themselves unless they have two contradictory parts, but it is possible that one of them or each of them is divisible into two divisions, so three or four parts result, for example, so they appear to have many parts, but in actuality it is not so, rather they are potential true disjunctives arranged one after another in a way that some cannot be actualized except by having two contradictory parts. As for the non-true [type], which is the exclusive without inclusive [i.e., man' al-jam' without man' al-khuluw], it may have two parts, may have finite parts, and may have infinite parts. The first is like our saying 'this thing is either a tree or a stone'; the second is like our saying 'this thing is either a tree or a stone or clay'; the third is like our saying 'this thing is either a tree or a part of a tree or a part of a part of a tree without end', and similarly for stone and other things. And one should not say: how is that? For its parts are existential, since their sum total reverts to mentioning the particulars subsumed under the contradictory of the thing in contrast to that thing; and there is no end to those particulars, for among them are finite and infinite, as has passed earlier: it is the exclusive without inclusive. And if it is said: if they are subsumed under the contradictory, then the contradictory is predicated of each one of them, and it cannot be so, otherwise the contradictory would be multiple. We say: the meaning of 'contradictory' in this form is 'not-tree', for example, in our saying 'this is either a tree or a stone', and 'not-tree' is multiple and predicated of everything besides tree, individually. And because it does not follow from something being subsumed under something else that that thing is predicated of it in any manner, rather it is predicated in one aspect and not another. Do you not see that 'tree' is subsumed under 'body', and 'body' is common between it and 'stone', for example. Yet body is correctly predicated of it but not correctly predicated of [the stone].
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila). The author first divides them into true exclusive (haqiqi) and non-exclusive (la-haqiqi), which is the type that prevents conjunction (man' al-jam') but not absence of both (man' al-khuluw). For each type, he explains that they can have two, finite, or infinite parts.

He begins with the haqiqi type. With two parts, the parts can be both affirmative (e.g., 'this number is either even or odd'), both negative (e.g., 'this number is either not even or not odd'), or mixed (e.g., 'this number is either even or not even'). With finite parts, he gives examples with three or four parts involving number classifications (even-even, even-odd, etc.) and explains that it applies to any finite number of divisions. With infinite parts, he uses the example of 'this even number is either the first even, the second, the third...' and extends it to odd numbers, perfect numbers, shapes, etc.

He then clarifies that even when a haqiqi disjunctive appears to have many parts, in reality it must reduce to two contradictory parts because true exclusive disjunction only holds between two contradictories. The multiple parts are only potential; they are arranged such that only two contradictory disjuncts are actual at a time.

Then he turns to the non-haqiqi type (man' al-jam' only). It also has three possibilities: two parts (e.g., 'this is either a tree or a stone'), finite parts (adding 'clay'), or infinite parts (e.g., 'this is either a tree or a part of a tree or a part of a part...'). He addresses an objection: if all the parts other than the first are subsumed under the contradictory (e.g., 'not-tree'), then the contradictory would be predicated of each, making it multiple, which seems impossible. He responds by explaining that in such disjunctives, 'naqid' (contradictory) is understood in a broader sense, like 'lā shajar' (not-tree), which includes many things and can be predicated of each individually. He gives the analogy of 'tree' being subsumed under 'body' but 'body' correctly predicated of tree but not of stone—similarly, 'not-tree' is predicated of stone in a specific way. This resolves the confusion.

Key Terms
لاحقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the existence of many parts in a la-haqiqi disjunctive not violate the principle that the contradictory is single?
A) Because the contradictory is not truly a single predicate but a broad category covering many particulars.
B) Because the la-haqiqi disjunctive actually has only two parts.
C) Because the author rejects the objection outright without justification.
D) Because the parts are not genuine predicates.

Key Takeaway
In non-exclusive disjunctives, the 'contradictory' is a broad category that includes many particulars, allowing multiple parts under a single negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
2. Respond to the objection: 'If the parts of a la-haqiqi disjunctive are all subsumed under the contradictory, the contradictory becomes multiple.' Use the author's argument from the passage to answer.

Real Disjunctive Propositions (al-Haqiqiyya)
Author explains real disjunctive propositions and their binary nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a real disjunctive proposition and explain why it is essentially binary even when it appears to have multiple parts.

Core Idea
Real disjunctions are always binary at heart, even when they seem to have many parts.

In this passage, the author clarifies the nature of real disjunctive propositions (al-haqiqiyya). Although they may appear to have three or four parts due to subdivision, they are fundamentally composed of two contradictory parts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly analyzing disjunctive arguments in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Find the two contradictory parts
Look for a pair of statements that are logical opposites. In the passage, the real disjunction is described as having two contradictory parts (جزأين متناقضين).
For example, 'tree' vs. 'not-tree' is a contradictory pair.
2
Check for apparent multiplicity
See if either part can be subdivided. The author says one or each can be divided, creating three or four apparent parts, but this is only superficial.
The parts are not external additions but nested divisions.
3
Verify the deep structure
Confirm that despite the appearance of many parts, the essential opposition remains binary. The disjunction is 'real' because it ultimately reduces to a contradiction.
Even if we have 'tree, part of tree, part of part of tree,' the core opposition is still tree vs. not-tree.
4
Apply to examples
Test on the passage's example: 'This thing is either a tree or a part of a tree' reduces to 'tree or not-tree'.
This reduction reveals the binary essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنها وهي الحقيقية لا تكون محققة بالذات إلا وأن تكون ذات جزأين متناقضين لكن يمكن أن يكون أحدها أو كل واحد منهما منقسمًا إلى القسمين فتحصل من الأجزاء ثلاثة أو أربعة مثلا فتكون في الظاهر ذات أجزاء كثيرة أما في نفس الأمر لا يكون كذلك بل هي منفصلات حقيقية بالقوة مترتبة بعضها على البعض بوجه لا يمكن أن يتحقق بعضها بالفعل إلا وأن تكون ذات جزأين متناقضين، وأما اللاحقيقية وهي المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو؛ فإنها قد تكون ذات جزأين وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية فالأول مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر، والثاني مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر وإما مدر، والثالث مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما جزء شجر وإما جزء جزء شجر إلى غير النهاية، وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى الحجر وغيره. ولا يقال كيف هو وأجزاؤها وجودية فإن حاصلها راجع إلى ذكر الجزئيات المندرجة تحت نقيض الشيء في مقابلة ذلك الشيء؛ ولا نهاية لتلك الجزئيات فإن منها ما يكون متناهيا ومنها ما لا يكون متناهيا كما مر فيما مرَّ: وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو. وإن قيل إنها إذا كانت مندرجة تحت النقيض كان النقيض محمولا على كل واحد منها ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك وإلا لكان النقيض متعددًا . فنقول : المعنى من النقيض في هذه الصورة هو اللا شجر مثلا في قولنا هذا إما شجر وإما حجر، واللا شجر متعدد ومحمول على جميع ما عدا الشجر فردًا فردًا. ولأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مندرجا تحت الشيء أن يكون ذلك الشيء محمولا عليه كيف كان، بل يكون محمولاً بوجه دون وجه. ألا ترى أن الشجر مندرج تحت الجسم والجسم مشترك بينه وبين الحجر مثلا. ومع هذا يصح أن يحمل الجسم عليه ولا يصح أن يحمل عليه المشترك، فلا يقال الشجر مشترك بين الشجر والحجر. وأما المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع فإنها قد تكون ذات جزأين، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية؛ فالأول مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما لا شجر وإما جسم، والثاني مثل قولنا هذا إما لا شجر وإما لا حجر وإما جسم، والثالث مثل قولنا هذا إما لا شجر / وإما لا حجر وإما لا مدر وإما لا وإما لا وإما لا إلى غير النهاية : فإنه يمكن تعددها حسب تعدد الأجزاء في المانعة من الجمع، ولأن حاصلها راجع إلى ذكر لوازم نقيض الشيء في مقابلة ذلك الشيء ولا نهاية لتلك اللوازم.
Then indeed it—meaning the real (disjunction)—is not actualized in essence unless it has two contradictory parts, but it is possible that one of them or each of them is divided into two parts, so there result three or four parts, for example, so it appears to have many parts, but in reality it is not so; rather they are real disjunctions in potentiality, arranged one after another in a way that some of them cannot be actualized unless they have two contradictory parts. As for the non-real, which is the exclusive (man'at al-jam') without inclusive, it may have two parts, or finite parts, or infinite parts. The first is like our statement: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone'; the second like 'This thing is either a tree, a stone, or clay'; the third like 'This thing is either a tree, or a part of a tree, or a part of a part of a tree, ad infinitum', and similarly for stone and others. And it is not said how is it that its parts are existential? Its outcome is that it refers to mentioning the particulars subsumed under the contradictory of the thing in opposition to that thing; and these particulars are infinite, for among them are finite and infinite, as has passed earlier. And it is the exclusive and inclusive (man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw). And if it is said that when they are subsumed under the contradictory, the contradictory is predicated of each one, but that cannot be, otherwise the contradictory would be multiple. We say: the meaning of 'contradictory' in this form is 'not-tree', for example, in our statement 'This is either a tree or a stone', and 'not-tree' is multiple and predicated of everything other than tree individually. And because the fact that something is subsumed under something does not necessitate that that thing be predicated of it in any manner, but rather it is predicated in one manner and not another. Do you not see that tree is subsumed under body, and body is common between it and stone, for example? Yet body is correctly predicated of it, but it is not correct to predicate the common term of it; one does not say 'tree is common between tree and stone'. As for the inclusive (man'at al-khuluw) without exclusive, it may have two parts, or finite parts, or infinite parts. The first is like our statement: 'This thing is either not-tree or body'; the second like 'This is either not-tree, or not-stone, or body'; the third like 'This is either not-tree, or not-stone, or not-clay, or not, or not, or not, ad infinitum'; for it can multiply according to the multiplication of parts in the exclusive disjunction, and because its outcome refers to mentioning the necessary concomitants of the contradictory of the thing in opposition to that thing, and these concomitants are infinite.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage explains two types of disjunctive propositions: real (haqiqiyya) and non-real (la-haqiqiyya). Real disjunctions are those where the parts are contradictory opposites, and they are always essentially binary even if they appear to have more than two parts. The author clarifies that when one or both parts themselves are divisible into two, the disjunction seems to have three or four parts, but in actuality it is still a binary opposition at the core. Non-real disjunctions are further divided into two subtypes: the exclusive (man'at al-jam') which prevents combination but allows both to be false, and the inclusive (man'at al-khuluw) which prevents both being false but allows conjunction. For the exclusive type, the author gives examples with two, finite, or infinite parts. For instance, 'this is either a tree or a stone' has two parts; 'this is either a tree, a stone, or clay' has three; and the infinite example involves an endless division of a tree into parts. The author addresses a potential objection: if the parts are subsumed under the contradictory (e.g., 'not-tree' includes stone, clay, etc.), then 'not-tree' would be multiple. The response is that 'not-tree' is predicated of each individual thing that is not a tree, but this multiplicity does not entail that the contradictory is one unified predicate; rather, the universal 'not-tree' applies to each particular. The author illustrates with the example of 'tree' being subsumed under 'body' but 'body' is a common term shared with stone, yet 'tree' is not called 'common' because commonality requires a different relation. Then the inclusive disjunction is similarly explained with two, finite, or infinite parts, where the parts are necessary concomitants of the contradictory. The underlying principle is that these disjunctions ultimately refer to the particulars or necessary qualities of the opposite of the original term.

Key Terms
الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It has many parts that are not contradictory.
B) It essentially has two contradictory parts.
C) It always has infinite parts.
D) It prevents both parts from being false.

Key Takeaway
Real disjunctions are always binary in essence, even when they appear to have multiple parts.

Exclusive Disjunctive Propositions (al-Mani'at al-Jam' Duna al-Khuluw)
Author explains exclusive disjunctive propositions and their possible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an exclusive disjunctive proposition and determine whether it has finite or infinite parts.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunctions prevent combination but allow both false, and can have any number of parts.

The author now turns to non-real disjunctions, starting with the exclusive type (al-mani'at al-jam' duna al-khuluw). This type prohibits the conjunction of its parts but permits both to be false. It can be used with two, finite, or infinite parts, depending on whether the list of possibilities is complete or open-ended.

Guided Path
1
Determine if it prevents conjunction
Check if the disjunction forbids both parts being true at the same time. For exclusive, it does (المانعة من الجمع).
Example: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' — it cannot be both.
2
Check if it allows both false
Confirm that both parts can be false simultaneously (دون الخلو). In the example, the thing could be clay, which is neither tree nor stone.
This distinguishes exclusive from inclusive.
3
Count the parts
Identify if the disjunction has two, finite, or infinite parts. The author gives examples: two ('tree or stone'), three ('tree, stone, clay'), or infinite (endless division of a tree).
Infinite parts arise when listing all particulars under the contradictory.
4
Understand the infinite case
Recognize that infinite parts refer to enumerating all particulars subsumed under the contradictory (e.g., all not-tree items). The author addresses an objection about the multiplicity of the contradictory.
The contradictory 'not-tree' is predicated of each particular individually.

Propositions

Inclusive Disjunctive Propositions (al-Mani'at al-Khuluw Duna al-Jam')
Author explains inclusive disjunctive propositions and their parts as necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an inclusive disjunctive proposition and explain how its parts are necessary concomitants of the contradictory.

Core Idea
Inclusive disjunctions prevent both false but allow conjunction, and their parts are necessary concomitants of the contradictory.

The final type discussed is the inclusive disjunction (al-mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'), which ensures that at least one part is true while allowing both to be true. The author explains that its parts are not arbitrary but are necessary concomitants (lawaazim) of the contradictory of the original term. Like the exclusive type, it can have two, finite, or infinite parts.

Guided Path
1
Determine if it prevents both false
Check if the disjunction requires that at least one part is true (مانعة من الخلو). For inclusive, it does.
Example: 'This thing is either not-tree or body' — at least one must be true (but both can be).
2
Check if it allows conjunction
Confirm that both parts can be true simultaneously (دون الجمع). In the example, 'body' can be true with 'not-tree' if the object is a stone (stone is body and not-tree).
This distinguishes inclusive from exclusive.
3
Identify the nature of parts
Note that the parts are necessary concomitants (لوازم) of the contradictory, not just any particulars. The author says the outcome refers to mentioning the necessary concomitants of the contradictory.
For instance, 'body' is a necessary concomitant of anything that is not-tree (since everything not-tree is a body? Not exactly, but in the example it works).
4
Count the parts
Like exclusive, it can have two, finite, or infinite parts. The author gives examples: two ('not-tree or body'), three ('not-tree, not-stone, body'), or infinite (endless list of not-somethings).
The infinite case arises from the infinite number of necessary concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنها وهي الحقيقية لا تكون محققة بالذات إلا وأن تكون ذات جزأين متناقضين لكن يمكن أن يكون أحدها أو كل واحد منهما منقسمًا إلى القسمين فتحصل من الأجزاء ثلاثة أو أربعة مثلا فتكون في الظاهر ذات أجزاء كثيرة أما في نفس الأمر لا يكون كذلك بل هي منفصلات حقيقية بالقوة مترتبة بعضها على البعض بوجه لا يمكن أن يتحقق بعضها بالفعل إلا وأن تكون ذات جزأين متناقضين، وأما اللاحقيقية وهي المانعة من الجمع دون الخلو؛ فإنها قد تكون ذات جزأين وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية فالأول مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر، والثاني مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر وإما مدر، والثالث مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما جزء شجر وإما جزء جزء شجر إلى غير النهاية، وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى الحجر وغيره. ولا يقال كيف هو وأجزاؤها وجودية فإن حاصلها راجع إلى ذكر الجزئيات المندرجة تحت نقيض الشيء في مقابلة ذلك الشيء؛ ولا نهاية لتلك الجزئيات فإن منها ما يكون متناهيا ومنها ما لا يكون متناهيا كما مر فيما مرَّ: وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو. وإن قيل إنها إذا كانت مندرجة تحت النقيض كان النقيض محمولا على كل واحد منها ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك وإلا لكان النقيض متعددًا . فنقول : المعنى من النقيض في هذه الصورة هو اللا شجر مثلا في قولنا هذا إما شجر وإما حجر، واللا شجر متعدد ومحمول على جميع ما عدا الشجر فردًا فردًا. ولأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مندرجا تحت الشيء أن يكون ذلك الشيء محمولا عليه كيف كان، بل يكون محمولاً بوجه دون وجه. ألا ترى أن الشجر مندرج تحت الجسم والجسم مشترك بينه وبين الحجر مثلا. ومع هذا يصح أن يحمل الجسم عليه ولا يصح أن يحمل عليه المشترك، فلا يقال الشجر مشترك بين الشجر والحجر. وأما المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع فإنها قد تكون ذات جزأين، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية؛ فالأول مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما لا شجر وإما جسم، والثاني مثل قولنا هذا إما لا شجر وإما لا حجر وإما جسم، والثالث مثل قولنا هذا إما لا شجر / وإما لا حجر وإما لا مدر وإما لا وإما لا وإما لا إلى غير النهاية : فإنه يمكن تعددها حسب تعدد الأجزاء في المانعة من الجمع، ولأن حاصلها راجع إلى ذكر لوازم نقيض الشيء في مقابلة ذلك الشيء ولا نهاية لتلك اللوازم.
Then indeed it—meaning the real (disjunction)—is not actualized in essence unless it has two contradictory parts, but it is possible that one of them or each of them is divided into two parts, so there result three or four parts, for example, so it appears to have many parts, but in reality it is not so; rather they are real disjunctions in potentiality, arranged one after another in a way that some of them cannot be actualized unless they have two contradictory parts. As for the non-real, which is the exclusive (man'at al-jam') without inclusive, it may have two parts, or finite parts, or infinite parts. The first is like our statement: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone'; the second like 'This thing is either a tree, a stone, or clay'; the third like 'This thing is either a tree, or a part of a tree, or a part of a part of a tree, ad infinitum', and similarly for stone and others. And it is not said how is it that its parts are existential? Its outcome is that it refers to mentioning the particulars subsumed under the contradictory of the thing in opposition to that thing; and these particulars are infinite, for among them are finite and infinite, as has passed earlier. And it is the exclusive and inclusive (man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw). And if it is said that when they are subsumed under the contradictory, the contradictory is predicated of each one, but that cannot be, otherwise the contradictory would be multiple. We say: the meaning of 'contradictory' in this form is 'not-tree', for example, in our statement 'This is either a tree or a stone', and 'not-tree' is multiple and predicated of everything other than tree individually. And because the fact that something is subsumed under something does not necessitate that that thing be predicated of it in any manner, but rather it is predicated in one manner and not another. Do you not see that tree is subsumed under body, and body is common between it and stone, for example? Yet body is correctly predicated of it, but it is not correct to predicate the common term of it; one does not say 'tree is common between tree and stone'. As for the inclusive (man'at al-khuluw) without exclusive, it may have two parts, or finite parts, or infinite parts. The first is like our statement: 'This thing is either not-tree or body'; the second like 'This is either not-tree, or not-stone, or body'; the third like 'This is either not-tree, or not-stone, or not-clay, or not, or not, or not, ad infinitum'; for it can multiply according to the multiplication of parts in the exclusive disjunction, and because its outcome refers to mentioning the necessary concomitants of the contradictory of the thing in opposition to that thing, and these concomitants are infinite.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage explains two types of disjunctive propositions: real (haqiqiyya) and non-real (la-haqiqiyya). Real disjunctions are those where the parts are contradictory opposites, and they are always essentially binary even if they appear to have more than two parts. The author clarifies that when one or both parts themselves are divisible into two, the disjunction seems to have three or four parts, but in actuality it is still a binary opposition at the core. Non-real disjunctions are further divided into two subtypes: the exclusive (man'at al-jam') which prevents combination but allows both to be false, and the inclusive (man'at al-khuluw) which prevents both being false but allows conjunction. For the exclusive type, the author gives examples with two, finite, or infinite parts. For instance, 'this is either a tree or a stone' has two parts; 'this is either a tree, a stone, or clay' has three; and the infinite example involves an endless division of a tree into parts. The author addresses a potential objection: if the parts are subsumed under the contradictory (e.g., 'not-tree' includes stone, clay, etc.), then 'not-tree' would be multiple. The response is that 'not-tree' is predicated of each individual thing that is not a tree, but this multiplicity does not entail that the contradictory is one unified predicate; rather, the universal 'not-tree' applies to each particular. The author illustrates with the example of 'tree' being subsumed under 'body' but 'body' is a common term shared with stone, yet 'tree' is not called 'common' because commonality requires a different relation. Then the inclusive disjunction is similarly explained with two, finite, or infinite parts, where the parts are necessary concomitants of the contradictory. The underlying principle is that these disjunctions ultimately refer to the particulars or necessary qualities of the opposite of the original term.

Key Terms
المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are contradictory.

C) They are always finite.
D) They are mutually exclusive.

Key Takeaway
Inclusive disjunctions prevent both false but allow conjunction, with parts being necessary concomitants of the contradictory.

The Combinatorial Principle for Compound Propositions
Author calculates compound propositions via multiplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of possible compound propositions given the types of component propositions and the number of components.

Core Idea
Compound propositions multiply, not add: types times components.

In this passage, the author teaches us how to count the possible forms of compound propositions. This is not mere arithmetic — it reflects the logical principle that when you combine propositions, every variation of each part yields a distinct compound. Understanding this multiplication is crucial for systematically enumerating all possible syllogistic forms and conditional types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of component propositions
Recognize the distinct types that each part of a compound proposition can take. In the passage, the types are al-muhassala (affirmative), al-basita (simple), and al-mu'addala (modified).
These types may vary in different logical systems.
2
Determine the number of components
Count how many propositions are being combined. For a conditional proposition, there are two components: antecedent and consequent. The passage gives examples with two and three components.
3
Apply the multiplication rule
Multiply the number of types for each component. If each component can be one of t types, and there are n components, the total number of compounds is t^n (when all components have the same number of types).
The passage illustrates: for two propositions with two types (affirmative and modified), 2×2=4; for two propositions with three types, 3×3=9.
4
Extend to more components
The rule generalizes to any number of components. For three propositions each of three types, the total would be 3×3×3=27, and so on.
The author says: 'consider based on what you know in the increase above three'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن ما يتركب من قضيتين أو أكثر منهما فذلك متعدد حسب تعدد الحاصل من ضرب البعض منها في البعض فالمتركب من القضيتين نحو المحصلة والمعدولة مثلا على أربعة أقسام، ومن القضايا الثلاث على تسعة أقسام لما أن المقدم في الشرطية قد يكون من المحصلة وحينئذ يكون التالي إما من المحصلة وإما من البسيطة وإما من المعدولة، وقد يكون من البسيطة كذلك. وكذلك قد يكون من المعدولة : فاعتبر بما عرفت في الزيادة على الثلاث موجبة كانت القضايا أو سالبة.
And know that what is composed of two propositions or more is multiplied according to the multiplicity resulting from multiplying some of them by others. So the compound of two propositions, such as the affirmative (al-muhassala) and the modified (al-mu'addala), for example, has four divisions. And of three propositions, there are nine divisions. Because the antecedent in the conditional may be from the affirmative, and then the consequent is either from the affirmative, or from the simple (al-basita), or from the modified; and it may be from the simple likewise; and likewise it may be from the modified. So consider based on what you know in the increase above three, whether the propositions are affirmative or negative.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents a combinatorial rule for compound propositions. When two propositions are combined (e.g., antecedent and consequent of a conditional), the number of possible compounds equals the product of the number of types for each part. The author gives an example: if the component propositions can be of three types—affirmative (muhassala), simple (basita), or modified (mu'addala)—then two propositions yield 3 × 3 = 9 combinations, but the text says for two it gives four? Actually the text says for two propositions like affirmative and modified, there are four divisions. This seems to imply only two types (affirmative and modified) then 2×2=4. For three propositions, if there are three types, then 3×3=9. The author then explains the enumeration for conditional propositions: the antecedent can be affirmative, then the consequent can be one of three types; similarly when antecedent is simple, and when antecedent is modified, hence 3×3=9. The rule extends to more than three components by multiplication. This is a precise mathematical treatment of the number of possible compound propositions based on the types of their constituent parts.

Key Terms
المحصلة
البسيطة
المعدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

The number of compound propositions is the product of the number of types of each component part.

The Nine Kinds of Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates the nine possible compositions of conditional propositions from categoricals and conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine kinds of conditional propositions based on their components and explain how the antecedent/consequent distinction differs between conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions can be composed from categoricals and other conditionals in nine possible ways.

The author now turns to a systematic classification of conditional propositions. He shows that conditionals are not limited to simple 'if-then' from two categoricals; they can also be formed from other conditionals. Understanding this taxonomy is key to correctly analyzing compound propositions in Islamic logic, especially in usul al-fiqh where conditional clauses often appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic component types
Recognize the three proposition types mentioned: hamliyya (categorical, subject-predicate), muttasila (conjunctive conditional, if-then), and munfasila (disjunctive conditional, either-or).
These are the building blocks for all conditionals.
2
Enumerate the six raw combinations
List the three pure types (two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives) and three mixed types (categorical + conjunctive, categorical + disjunctive, conjunctive + disjunctive).
The author explicitly states these six kinds.
3
Account for precedence in mixed types
Each mixed type yields two sub-kinds because either component can be the antecedent (muqaddam) and the other the consequent (tali). For example, categorical + conjunctive gives (categorical antecedent, conjunctive consequent) and (conjunctive antecedent, categorical consequent). This applies similarly to the other two mixed types, giving 2 × 3 = 6 sub-kinds.
The source says 'each of these last three has two divisions according to precedence'.
4
Arrive at the total of nine
Add the three pure types (one each) to the six mixed sub-kinds to obtain nine total divisions. The author calls this a 'rational classification' (qismah 'aqliyyah).
The classification applies to both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, but the antecedent-consequent relationship is opposite: in conjunctives, the antecedent is the condition and consequent the result; in disjunctives, the relation is mutual exclusion or exhaustion, not a conditional implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في تقسيم الشرطية الأول] أما الشرطية فإنه يمكن تركيبها من الحمليتين والمتصلتين والمنفصليتين والحملية والمتّصلة والحملية والمنفصلة والمتّصلة والمنفصلة. وكل واحد من هذه الثلاثة الأخيرة على قسمين بحسب التقدم والتقدم. فلا جرم يكون الكل على تسعة أقسام قسمة عقلية في المتّصلات والمنفصلات غير أن المقدم يتميز عن التالي في المتصلات على خلاف ما يكون في المنفصلات.
Section: [On the First Division of the Conditional] As for the conditional, it can be composed of two categoricals, two conditionals (conjunctive and disjunctive), a categorical and a conjunctive conditional, a categorical and a disjunctive conditional, and a conjunctive and a disjunctive conditional. And each of these last three has two divisions according to precedence and precedence. Thus there are in total nine divisions, a rational classification for conjunctives and disjunctives, except that the antecedent is distinguished from the consequent in conjunctives contrary to the case in disjunctives.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the division of conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author lists the possible combinations of component propositions that form a conditional. The first is two categoricals (hamliyya). Then two conjunctive conditionals (muttasila) or two disjunctive conditionals (munfasila). Then mixed types: categorical with conjunctive, categorical with disjunctive, and conjunctive with disjunctive. Each of the mixed types has two variations depending on which part is antecedent and which is consequent (taqaddum), giving 2 each. That makes: 1 (two categoricals) + 1 (two conjunctives) + 1 (two disjunctives) + 2 (categorical & conjunctive) + 2 (categorical & disjunctive) + 2 (conjunctive & disjunctive) = 9 total. The author notes that this is a rational classification valid for both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals, but the distinction between antecedent and consequent is opposite in the two types: in conjunctives, the antecedent is the condition and consequent is conditioned; in disjunctives, the relation is different (the parts are mutually exclusive or exhaustive, not conditional in the same sense).

Key Terms
الحملية
المتصلات
المنفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Seven

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are classified into nine kinds based on whether their components are categoricals or other conditionals and the order of antecedence.

Types of Inclusive Disjunction
Author enumerates three types of inclusive disjunction: two-part, finite, and infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of inclusive disjunction (al-māni'a min al-khuluw dūna al-jam') and explain why infinite parts are possible.

Core Idea
Inclusive OR can have two, finite, or infinite parts because its truth reduces to listing all entailments of the negation.

In the previous lesson, we learned about exclusive disjunction (al-māni'a min al-jam'). Now we turn to inclusive disjunction (al-māni'a min al-khuluw dūna al-jam'), which allows both parts to be true. The author not only defines it but classifies it into three types based on the number of parts. This classification is not arbitrary: the possibility of infinite parts follows from a deep logical insight about entailments. Let us explore each type and the reasoning behind the infinite case.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the inclusive disjunction form
Identify that 'al-māni'a min al-khuluw dūna al-jam'' means at least one part is true (prevents emptiness) but both can be true (does not prevent conjunction). This is inclusive OR, often expressed as 'either A or B (or both)'.
Contrast with exclusive OR which prevents both being true. In Arabic logic, the term 'al-māni'a' alone sometimes meant exclusive; this text is careful to specify the subtype.
2
Distinguish two-part from finite multi-part
The author gives examples: two-part 'either not a tree or a body' has two disjuncts. Finite multi-part 'either not a tree, or not a stone, or a body' has three. Recognize that adding more disjuncts is possible as long as the number is finite.
The examples assume a physical universe. The logical form is valid for any domain where the disjuncts cover the possibilities.
3
Understand the infinite case
The author says it may have infinite parts, giving an example with a series ending in 'ad infinitum'. This is possible because the disjunction reduces to listing all the entailments (lawāzim) of the negation of the original thing.
The entailments are potentially unlimited, so the parts can be infinite in principle. This shows the author's deep logical analysis.
4
Grasp the logical reduction
The author explains that the content of such a disjunction is effectively: 'Either the thing itself, or any one of the entailments of its negation.' Since there is no end to those entailments, infinite parts are possible.
This is a key insight: the inclusive disjunction is not just a list of terms but a structured expression of logical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع فإنها قد تكون ذات جزأين، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء متناهية، وقد تكون ذات أجزاء غير متناهية؛ فالأول مثل قولنا هذا الشيء إما لا شجر وإما جسم، والثاني مثل قولنا هذا إما لا شجر وإما لا حجر وإما جسم، والثالث مثل قولنا هذا إما لا شجر وإما لا حجر وإما لا مدر وإما لا وإما لا وإما لا إلى غير النهاية : فإنه يمكن تعددها حسب تعدد الأجزاء في المانعة من الجمع، ولأن حاصلها راجع إلى ذكر لوازم نقيض الشيء في مقابلة ذلك الشيء ولا نهاية لتلك اللوازم.
As for the exclusive disjunction that prevents emptiness without preventing conjunction, it may have two parts, and it may have finite parts, and it may have infinite parts. The first is like our saying 'This thing is either not a tree or a body.' The second is like our saying 'This is either not a tree, or not a stone, or a body.' The third is like our saying 'This is either not a tree, or not a stone, or not mud, or not ... and not ... and not ... ad infinitum.' For it is possible for them to multiply according to the multiplicity of parts in the exclusive disjunction that prevents conjunction, and because their content reduces to mentioning the entailments of the negation of the thing in contrast to that thing, and there is no end to those entailments.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage defines the types of a particular kind of disjunctive proposition called 'al-mani'a min al-khuluw duna al-jam'' – the disjunction that prevents emptiness (i.e., at least one part must be true) but does not prevent conjunction (i.e., both parts may be true). This is inclusive OR. The author says this kind of disjunction can have two parts, or a finite number of parts, or an infinite number of parts. He gives an example for each: for two parts, 'This thing is either not a tree or a body.' For a finite number, he gives three parts: 'This is either not a tree, or not a stone, or a body.' For an infinite number, he gives a series that tails off: '... or not mud, or not ... and not ... ad infinitum.' The reason for the possibility of infinite parts is that the content of such a disjunction reduces to listing all the entailments (lawāzim) of the negation of the thing in contrast to the thing itself. Since those entailments are potentially infinite, the parts can be infinite. This is a careful logical observation: when we say 'this is either A or B' under inclusive OR, if we take A as the original thing and B as something else, the disjunction essentially lists what follows from not-A, and those consequences are unlimited. Thus the classification is logically grounded.

Key Terms
المانعة من الخلو دون الجمع (al-māni'a min al-khuluw dūna al-jam'): A disjunctive proposition that requires at least one part to be true (prevents emptiness) but allows both to be true (does not prevent conjunction). This is inclusive OR. [Note: In earlier logic, al-māni'a often refers to exclusive OR; this text distinguishes the two types clearly.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) This animal is either a horse or a donkey.
B) This object is either not a plant or it is a living thing.
C) This food is either halal or haram.
D) This number is either even or odd.

Key Takeaway
Inclusive disjunction (al-māni'a min al-khuluw dūna al-jam') is classified by number of parts, and the infinite possibility stems from the unbounded entailments of the negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Combinatorial Principle for Compound Propositions
Author explains combinatorial counting of compound propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of possible compound conditional propositions given a set of proposition types using the combinatorial principle.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals multiply: n types give n² possibilities.

This lesson introduces a simple yet powerful combinatorial principle that classical logicians used to enumerate all possible forms of conditional propositions. Understanding this principle is essential for systematically classifying hypothetical arguments in Islamic logic, especially in discussions of conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the component types
Note the three proposition types mentioned: muḥaṣṣala (affirmative), muʿaddala (negative predicate), and basīṭa (simple). These are the building blocks.
Basīṭa is tentatively defined as a simple categorical, possibly universal affirmative.
2
Understand the conditional structure
A conditional proposition has an antecedent and a consequent. Each can be any of the available types.
The author uses this structure to illustrate the combinations.
3
Apply the multiplication principle
If there are 2 types, the number of compounds is 2×2 = 4. For 3 types, 3×3 = 9. Generalize: for n types, n×n = n².
The passage explicitly shows this for 2 and 3 types.
4
Extend to more types
The principle holds for any number of types, whether propositions are affirmative or negative. The author says 'consider for increases beyond three'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن ما يتركب من قضيتين أو أكثر منهما فذلك متعدد حسب تعدد الحاصل من ضرب البعض منها في البعض فالمتركب من القضيتين نحو المحصلة والمعدولة مثلا على أربعة أقسام، ومن القضايا الثلاث على تسعة أقسام لما أن المقدم في الشرطية قد يكون من المحصلة وحينئذ يكون التالي إما من المحصلة وإما من البسيطة وإما من المعدولة، وقد يكون من البسيطة كذلك. وكذلك قد يكون من المعدولة : فاعتبر بما عرفت في الزيادة على الثلاث موجبة كانت القضايا أو سالبة.
And know that what is composed of two or more propositions is multiplied according to the product of some of them with others. Thus the composite of two propositions, such as affirmative (muḥaṣṣala) and negative (muʿaddala), for example, yields four divisions. And from three propositions, there are nine divisions, because the antecedent in a conditional may be affirmative, and then the consequent may be affirmative, or simple (basīṭa), or negative; and it may also be simple likewise. And it may likewise be negative. So consider by what you have learned when increasing beyond three, whether the propositions are affirmative or negative.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage sets out a combinatorial principle for forming compound propositions from simpler ones. The author states that when you combine two or more propositions, the number of resulting compounds is obtained by multiplying the number of options for each component. For example, if you have two types of propositions—say, affirmative (muḥaṣṣala) and negative (muʿaddala)—then the possible combinations of two propositions (taking one as antecedent and one as consequent) are 2 × 2 = 4. For three propositions (e.g., affirmative, simple, negative), there are 3 × 3 = 9 combinations. The author explains using the conditional: the antecedent could be affirmative, then the consequent could be affirmative, simple, or negative; similarly if the antecedent is simple or negative. This yields 3 × 3 = 9. He notes that this holds whether the propositions are affirmative or negative. The principle is generalizable: the total number of compounds from n types of propositions is n² (if order matters). This is a useful rule for classifying all possible conditional propositions.
