Three Types of Categorical Propositions in Compound Conditionals
Author lists three proposition types used in combinatorial examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muḥaṣṣala, muʿaddala, and basīṭa propositions and identify each type in examples.

Core Idea
Three propositional types—affirmative, negated-predicate, and simple—form the building blocks of conditionals.

This lesson explores the three types of categorical propositions that classical logicians used as components in compound conditionals. Understanding their differences is crucial for correctly applying the combinatorial principle and for analyzing hypothetical arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Define muḥaṣṣala (affirmative)
A muḥaṣṣala proposition is a standard affirmative categorical, e.g., 'Zayd is just'. It has no negation.
It is the basic positive statement.
2
Define muʿaddala (negated predicate)
A muʿaddala proposition has a negated predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing'. This is distinct from a negated copula (salba).
In later logic, muʿaddala is sometimes conflated with salba; here it is a separate type.
3
Define basīṭa (simple)
Basīṭa is a simple proposition that is neither muḥaṣṣala nor muʿaddala. It may be a universal affirmative like 'Every human is animal'.
The exact definition is ambiguous; context suggests it is a third basic type.
4
Apply the distinctions
Given a proposition, classify it as one of the three. For example, 'The world is created' is muḥaṣṣala; 'The world is not-eternal' is muʿaddala; 'Every effect has a cause' is basīṭa (if universal affirmative).

Nine Types of Conditional Propositions
Author lists nine varieties of conditional propositions based on composition of categoricals, conditionals, and disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition into one of the nine types based on the logical forms of its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions come in nine types: determined by whether each part is categorical, conditional, or disjunctive.

The classical logicians meticulously catalogued how conditionals can be formed. This classification is not mere taxonomy—it equips you to parse complex arguments in kalam and fiqh. In this lesson, we explore the nine types of muttasilat (connected conditionals) and learn to recognize their structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three basic proposition types
Recall that every proposition is either categorical (ḥamliyya: subject-predicate), conditional (sharṭiyya muttaṣila: if-then), or disjunctive (sharṭiyya munfaṣila: either-or). These are the building blocks.
Categorical: 'Zayd is standing.' Conditional: 'If Zayd is standing, then Amr is sitting.' Disjunctive: 'Either Zayd is standing or Amr is sitting.'
2
Recognize the pure compositions (types 1–3)
In the first three types, both antecedent and consequent are the same kind: both categorical (type 1), both conditional (type 2), or both disjunctive (type 3). The passage gives clear examples for each.
Type 1: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' – both parts are categorical.
3
Master the mixed compositions and order (types 4–9)
The remaining six types mix two different kinds. The order matters: the kind of the antecedent and consequent can be swapped, giving two types for each pair (e.g., categorical-conditional vs. conditional-categorical). The author enumerates all six.
Type 4: categorical antecedent + conditional consequent. Type 5: conditional antecedent + categorical consequent. Similarly for categorical-disjunctive (types 6–7) and conditional-disjunctive (types 8–9).
4
Check each example against the pattern
Go through the nine Arabic examples one by one. For each, identify the antecedent and consequent types. Verify that they match the described composition and order.
Use the provided translations to help. For instance, type 6: 'If this number is odd (categorical), then it is either prime or composite (disjunctive).'
5
Construct your own example from an Islamic science
Now apply this taxonomy. Choose a topic from fiqh, kalam, or usul al-fiqh and generate a conditional of a specific type (e.g., type 8). This solidifies your understanding.
Example: 'If whenever a sale is valid (conditional antecedent) it is not void, then either the sale is valid or it is void (disjunctive consequent).'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في تقسيم الشرطية الأول] أما الشرطية فإنه يمكن تركيبها من الحمليتين والمتصلتين والمنفصليتين والحملية والمتّصلة والحملية والمنفصلة والمتّصلة والمنفصلة. وكل واحد من هذه الثلاثة الأخيرة على قسمين بحسب التقدم والتقدم. فلا جرم يكون الكل على تسعة أقسام قسمة عقلية في المتّصلات والمنفصلات غير أن المقدم يتميز عن التالي في المتصلات على خلاف ما يكون في المنفصلات. أما الأول من المتصلات وهو الذي يكون من حمليتين، فذلك مثل ما يقال كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا . وأما الثاني منها وهو ما يكون من متصلتين، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فكلما لم يكن النهار موجودًا لم يكن الشمس طالعة . وأما الثالث وهو ما يكون من منفصلتين، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان الجسم إما متحركًا وإما ساكنا، فبعض الجوهر إما ساكن وإما متحرّك. وأما الرابع وهو ما يكون من حملية ومتصلة، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وأما الخامس وهو ما يكون من متصلة وحملية، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فوجود النهار لازم لطلوع الشمس. وأما السادس وهو ما يكون من حملية ومنفصلة، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان هذا العدد فردًا فهو إما أولي وإما مركب. وأما السابع وهو ما يكون من منفصلة وحملية، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان هذا العدد إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد فهو مركب. وأما الثامن وهو ما يكون من متصلة ومنفصلة، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلَّما كان العدد زوجًا فليس بفرد فإما أن يكون العدد زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا . وأما التاسع وهو ما يكون من منفصلة ومتصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان العدد إما زوجًا وإما فردًا فكلما كان زوجًا فليس بفرد.
Section: On the First Division of the Conditional. As for the conditional, it can be composed of two categoricals, two conditionals, two disjunctives, a categorical and a conditional, a categorical and a disjunctive, and a conditional and a disjunctive. And each of these last three has two divisions according to precedence. So necessarily the total is nine logical divisions in conditionals and disjunctives, except that the antecedent is distinguished from the consequent in conditionals contrary to what is the case in disjunctives. As for the first of the conditionals, which is composed of two categoricals, it is like the statement 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' As for the second, which is composed of two conditionals, it is like our saying 'If whenever the sun is rising daytime exists, then whenever daytime does not exist the sun is not rising.' As for the third, which is composed of two disjunctives, it is like our saying 'If the body is either moving or at rest, then some substance is either at rest or moving.' As for the fourth, which is composed of a categorical and a conditional, it is like our saying 'If the rising of the sun is a cause for the existence of daytime, then whenever the sun is rising daytime exists.' As for the fifth, which is composed of a conditional and a categorical, it is like our saying 'If whenever the sun is rising daytime exists, then the existence of daytime is necessary for the rising of the sun.' As for the sixth, which is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive, it is like our saying 'If this number is odd, then it is either prime or composite.' As for the seventh, which is composed of a disjunctive and a categorical, it is like our saying 'If this number is either even-even or even-odd, then it is composite.' As for the eighth, which is composed of a conditional and a disjunctive, it is like our saying 'If whenever the number is even it is not odd, then the number is either even or odd.' As for the ninth, which is composed of a disjunctive and a conditional, it is like our saying 'If the number is either even or odd, then whenever it is even it is not odd.'
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of conditional propositions (al-sharṭiyya) into nine types based on the logical types of their antecedent and consequent. The author first enumerates the possible compositions: both categorical, both conditional, both disjunctive, and then mixed pairs (categorical+conditional, categorical+disjunctive, conditional+disjunctive). Since each mixed pair can be in two orders (first type as antecedent, second as consequent, or vice versa), we get 2×3 = 6 from the mixed, plus 3 from the pure = 9 total. This is a rational division (qisma 'aqliyya) applicable to both connected conditionals (muttasilāt) and disjunctives (munfaṣilāt), though the author notes that in conditionals the antecedent is naturally distinct from the consequent, unlike disjunctives. He then provides one example for each of the nine types, using the familiar pattern of sun rising and daytime, and number properties. For example, type 1 (two categoricals): 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' Type 2 (two conditionals): 'If whenever the sun is rising daytime exists, then whenever daytime does not exist the sun is not rising.' Type 3 (two disjunctives): 'If the body is either moving or at rest, then some substance is either at rest or moving.' These examples illustrate the logical forms. The student should memorize the nine types and recognize them in logical analysis.

Real vs. Verbal Types of Disjunctive Propositions
Refinement of disjunctive classification into real vs verbal types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the six real types and nine verbal types of disjunctive propositions and explain why the reduction occurs.

Core Idea
Symmetry in disjunctives reduces real types from nine to six.

We learned earlier that disjunctive propositions can be classified into nine types verbally. But the author now refines this: in reality, only six types are logically distinct because the antecedent and consequent in a disjunctive are not ordered by nature. This lesson explains the reason for this reduction and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Find where the author states that disjunctives have only six divisions according to reality (bi-ḥasabi al-ḥaqīqa) and nine verbally.
The claim is at the beginning of the passage.
2
Understand the Reason for Reduction
The author says the antecedent and consequent are not distinguished by nature (yatimayyazu bi al-ṭabʿ). This means they are symmetrical: swapping them does not create a different logical form. The three mixed types (e.g., categorical+disjunctive) each collapse into one when order is not considered.
The last three of the nine are not further divisible because of this symmetry.
3
Analyze the First Example
Consider the example 'This number is either even or odd.' Whether we say 'even or odd' or 'odd or even,' the meaning is identical. This shows the disjunction is symmetric.
This is a simple two-part disjunctive.
4
Analyze the Second Example
The author gives a compound example: 'Either whenever the sun is rising daytime exists, or if the sun is rising daytime does not exist.' Here each disjunct is a conditional, but the overall disjunction is symmetric because swapping the two conditionals yields the same logical situation.
The passage cuts off, but the point is that even compound disjunctives follow the same principle.
5
Apply the Distinction
From the six real types, we can generate the nine verbal types by considering order, but we must not mistake verbal multiplicity for real logical diversity. Practice identifying when a disjunction is symmetric.
This distinction prevents overcomplicating logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يكون من المنفصلات فذلك لا يكون إلا على ستة أقسام بحسب الحقيقة؛ إذ المقدم فيها لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع فلا تكون الثلاثة الأخيرة منها منقسمة إلى القسمين كذلك. فأما بحسب اللفظ فإنها منقسمة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الكل على تسعة أقسام كما مر أما الأول منها فذلك مثل قولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد وأما الثاني فذلك إما أن يكون كلّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود.
As for what is composed of disjunctives, that is only in six divisions according to reality, because the antecedent in them is not distinguished from the consequent by nature, so the last three of them are not divided into two divisions likewise. But verbally they are divided; and thus it follows that the total is nine divisions as before. As for the first of them, it is like our saying 'This number is either even or odd.' As for the second, it is like 'Either whenever the sun is rising daytime exists, or if the sun is rising daytime does not exist.'
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage refines the previous classification. The author points out that while the nine-fold division holds verbally for disjunctives, in reality there are only six distinct types. The reason is that in a disjunctive proposition (munfaṣila), the antecedent and consequent are not naturally ordered; they are symmetrical. Therefore, the three mixed types (categorical+disjunctive, disjunctive+categorical, etc.) each collapse into one, because swapping the order does not produce a genuinely different logical form. For example, 'This number is either even or odd' is the same whether we take 'even' as antecedent or 'odd' as antecedent; the disjunction is symmetric. So the three pairs reduce to three single types, giving 3 (pure) + 3 (mixed) = 6 real types. Verbally, however, we can still enumerate nine by considering order, which is useful for analysis. The author gives two examples: the first is a simple two-part disjunction: 'This number is either even or odd.' The second appears to be a compound disjunction where each part is a conditional, but the text is cut off. This distinction between 'real' and 'verbal' divisions is a sophisticated logical point: it warns that not all combinatorial possibilities are logically distinct — some are merely notational variants.

Identifying the Nine Types of Compound Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates nine types of compound conditionals by combining hamli, muttasil, munfasil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the composition (حمليّة, متّصلة, منفصلة) of the antecedent and consequent in any compound conditional proposition and classify it into one of the nine types.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals are built from three building blocks: categorical, conditional, disjunctive — yielding nine types.

In this passage, the author systematically classifies compound conditional propositions (الشرطية المركبة) based on the types of their antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي). Just as simple propositions are either categorical (حمليّة), conditional (متّصلة), or disjunctive (منفصلة), compound conditionals combine these in nine ways. Understanding this classification helps you parse complex logical statements used in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three basic proposition types
Recall the three types: حمليّة (categorical: subject-predicate), متّصلة (conditional: if...then), منفصلة (disjunctive: either...or). Every compound conditional's antecedent and consequent are one of these.
These are the building blocks.
2
Locate the antecedent and consequent
In a compound conditional like 'If [A] then [B]', A is the antecedent (مقدم) and B is the consequent (تالي). Identify them clearly.
The antecedent comes after 'إن' or 'كلما'.
3
Determine the type of antecedent and consequent
Decide whether each part is categorical, conditional, or disjunctive. For example, in the author's first type, both parts are categorical: 'إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود' – 'الشمس طالعة' is categorical, 'النهار موجود' is categorical.
Use the examples given in the passage.
4
Match the pair to the author's numbering
The author lists nine combinations: (1) حمليّة + حمليّة, (2) متّصلة + متّصلة, (3) منفصلة + منفصلة, (4) حمليّة + متّصلة, (5) متّصلة + حمليّة, (6) حمليّة + منفصلة, (7) منفصلة + حمليّة, (8) متّصلة + منفصلة, (9) منفصلة + متّصلة. Memorize this order.
The order matters for classification.
5
Handle the special case of two disjunctives
When both antecedent and consequent are disjunctive, the author notes that only six distinct types exist 'according to reality' (بحسب الحقيقة) but nine 'according to expression' (بحسب اللفظ). Understand that the last three (7-9) are not naturally distinct but are treated as such for linguistic consistency.
This is a nuanced point; the examples given clarify the distinction.

Source Text

وأما الأول من المتصلات وهو الذي يكون من حمليتين، فذلك مثل ما يقال كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا . وأما الثاني منها وهو ما يكون من متصلتين، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فكلما لم يكن النهار موجودًا لم يكن الشمس طالعة . وأما الثالث وهو ما يكون من منفصلتين، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان الجسم إما متحركًا وإما ساكنا، فبعض الجوهر إما ساكن وإما متحرّك. وأما الرابع وهو ما يكون من حملية ومتصلة، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وأما الخامس وهو ما يكون من متصلة وحملية، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فوجود النهار لازم لطلوع الشمس. وأما السادس وهو ما يكون من حملية ومنفصلة، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان هذا العدد فردًا فهو إما أولي وإما مركب. وأما السابع وهو ما يكون من منفصلة وحملية، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان هذا العدد إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد فهو مركب. وأما الثامن وهو ما يكون من متصلة ومنفصلة، فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلَّما كان العدد زوجًا فليس بفرد فإما أن يكون العدد زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا . وأما التاسع وهو ما يكون من منفصلة ومتصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان العدد إما زوجًا وإما فردًا فكلما كان زوجًا فليس بفرد. وأما ما يكون من المنفصلات فذلك لا يكون إلا على ستة أقسام بحسب الحقيقة؛ إذ المقدم فيها لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع فلا تكون الثلاثة الأخيرة منها منقسمة إلى القسمين كذلك. فأما بحسب اللفظ فإنها منقسمة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الكل على تسعة أقسام كما مر أما الأول منها فذلك مثل قولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد وأما الثاني فذلك إما أن يكون كلّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار ليس بموجود. وأما الثالث فذلك إما أن تكون الشمس إما طالعة وإما غاربة وإما أن تكون الشمس إما لا طالعة وإما لا غاربة. وأما الرابع فذلك إما أن لا يكون طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار وإما أن يكون كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا. وأما الخامس فذلك إما أن يكون كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وإما أن لا يكون طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار. وأما السادس فذلك إما أن لا يكون هذا الشيء عددًا وإما أن يكون زوجًا أو فردًا. وأما السابع فذلك إما أن يكون هذا الشيء زوجا أو فردًا وإما أن لا يكون عددًا. وأما الثامن فذلك إما أن يكون هذا الشيء عددًا فهو فرد وإما أن يكون هذا الشيء عددًا وإما أن يكون فردا. وأما التاسع إما أن يكون هذا الشيء عددًا وإما أن يكون إن كان عددًا فهو فرد، فاعتبر بما عرفت. واعلم بأن المقدم فيها وإن لم يكن متميزا عن التالي بالطبع فلا يلزم أن يكون حمليا في المركب من الحملي والمتصل أو من الحملي والمنفصل، ولا يلزم أن يكون متصلا أيضًا في المركب من المتصل والمنفصل بل يمكن أن يكون حمليّا، ويمكن أن لا يكون، ويمكن أن يكون متصلا كذلك وأن لا يكون.
As for the first of the conditionals, which is composed of two categoricals, that is like saying 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' And as for the second of them, which is composed of two conditionals, that is like our saying 'If whenever the sun is rising daytime exists, then whenever daytime does not exist the sun is not rising.' And as for the third, which is composed of two disjunctives, that is like our saying 'If the body is either moving or at rest, then some substance is either at rest or moving.' And as for the fourth, which is composed of a categorical and a conditional, that is like our saying 'If the rising of the sun is a cause for the existence of daytime, then whenever the sun is rising daytime exists.' And as for the fifth, which is composed of a conditional and a categorical, that is like our saying 'If whenever the sun is rising daytime exists, then the existence of daytime is necessary for the rising of the sun.' And as for the sixth, which is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive, that is like our saying 'If this number is odd, then it is either prime or composite.' And as for the seventh, which is composed of a disjunctive and a categorical, that is like our saying 'If this number is either even-even or even-odd, then it is composite.' And as for the eighth, which is composed of a conditional and a disjunctive, that is like our saying 'If whenever the number is even it is not odd, then the number is either even or odd.' And as for the ninth, which is composed of a disjunctive and a conditional, that is like our saying 'If the number is either even or odd, then whenever it is even it is not odd.' As for what is composed of disjunctives, that only occurs in six divisions according to reality; because the antecedent in them is not distinct from the consequent by nature, so the last three of them are not divided into two parts likewise. But according to expression, they are divided; and then it follows that the total is nine divisions as passed. As for the first of them, that is like our saying 'This number is either even or odd.' And as for the second, that is either 'Whenever the sun is rising daytime exists' or 'If the sun is rising then daytime does not exist.' And as for the third, that is either 'The sun is either rising or setting' or 'The sun is either not rising or not setting.' And as for the fourth, that is either 'The rising of the sun is not a cause for the existence of daytime' or 'Whenever the sun is rising daytime exists.' And as for the fifth, that is either 'Whenever the sun is rising daytime exists' or 'The rising of the sun is not a cause for the existence of daytime.' And as for the sixth, that is either 'This thing is not a number' or 'It is either even or odd.' And as for the seventh, that is either 'This thing is even or odd' or 'It is not a number.' And as for the eighth, that is either 'If this thing is a number then it is odd' or 'This thing is a number and it is odd.' And as for the ninth, that is either 'This thing is a number' or 'If it is a number then it is odd.' So consider what you have known. And know that the antecedent in them, even if it is not distinct from the consequent by nature, it is not necessary that it be categorical in the composite of categorical and conditional or of categorical and disjunctive, nor is it necessary that it be conditional also in the composite of conditional and disjunctive; rather it is possible that it be categorical, and possible that it not be, and possible that it be conditional similarly and that it not be.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of compound conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author enumerates nine types based on the composition of the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) from three kinds of propositions: categorical (hamliyya), conditional (muttasila), and disjunctive (munfasila). He provides clear examples for each, using themes like sunrise/daytime, body motion/rest, and number parity. After listing the nine, he notes that in reality, compositions using only disjunctives yield only six distinct types because the antecedent and consequent are not naturally distinct, but linguistically they can be considered nine. He then illustrates each of these six with examples. Finally, he adds a note that the antecedent in such compounds is not necessarily of the same type as the component propositions; it can be categorical or conditional regardless of the components. The passage is a parsing exercise: the student must learn to identify the type of compound conditional based on its structure.

Second Division of Conditionals: Specificity of Muqaddam
Author divides conditionals based on whether muqaddam is specific or general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish determinate and general antecedents, and calculate the nine divisions of conditional propositions.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions split into nine types by multiplying antecedent and consequent specificity.

This passage introduces a new way to classify conditionals: not by connection (muttasila/munfasila) but by the nature of the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). The author explains that each can be determinate (specific to a number) or general, leading to nine possible forms. Understanding this helps you parse logical statements more precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes
Notice that the passage treats the antecedent and the consequent as independent axes. Each can be either determinate (mu'ayyan) or general (ʿāmm).
Determinate means it refers to a specific number of possibilities, e.g., 'one of two' or 'one of three'.
2
Determine the antecedent's specificity
Classify the antecedent as either determinate (مُعَيَّن) or general (عَام). Use the author's example: 'one of two things' is determinate; a general clause like 'if it rains' is general.
The passage says the antecedent is either determinate or not determinate, i.e., general.
3
Determine the consequent's specificity
Apply the same classification to the consequent. The author notes that the consequent also has three divisions (though the text is ambiguous; treat it as two: determinate or general).
The multiplication to nine suggests that both antecedent and consequent are considered to have three states each (determinate, something else, general?), but for basic understanding, treat both as binary: determinate or general.
4
Multiply the possibilities
Combine the states of antecedent and consequent. If each has 3 possibilities (as the text implies), then 3×3=9. Thus muttasilat and munfasilat each have nine types based on this division.
The exact three states per side may require clarification, but the multiplication principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في تقسيم الشرطية الثاني] أما الشرطية فإنها مشتملة على مقدم وتالى متّصلة كانت أو منفصلةً كما مرَّ. والمقدم لا يخلو من أن يكون معينًا معلوما، أو لا يكون؛ وذلك إما أن يكون له اختصاص بعدد معين كأحد الأمرين أو أحد الأمور الثلاثة مثلا، أو لا يكون بل يكون عاما لا اختصاص له بواحد من الأعداد. وكذلك التالي فإنه على ثلاثة أقسام بالضرورة أيضًا، والثلاثة في الثلاثة تسعة فتكون المتصلات على تسعة أقسام بهذا الاعتبار، والمنفصلات كذلك.
Chapter: [On the Second Division of the Conditional] As for the conditional, it consists of an antecedent and a consequent, whether it is conditional or disjunctive, as has passed. And the antecedent is not free from being determinate and known, or not; that is either that it has a specification with a certain number, such as one of two things or one of three things for example, or that it is not, rather it is general with no specification to any of the numbers. And similarly the consequent, for it is of three divisions necessarily as well, and three times three is nine, so the conditionals (muttasilat) are of nine divisions in this respect, and the disjunctives (munfasilat) likewise.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a new classification of conditional propositions (both muttasila and munfasila) based on the determinate or general nature of the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (talin). The author states that the antecedent is either definite (specific to a certain number, e.g., one of two things or one of three) or indefinite (general, not specific to any number). The same applies to the consequent, which necessarily has three possibilities. Multiplying the three possibilities of the antecedent (though the text says three for each, but earlier it says 'does not free from being determinate or not'—that's two? Actually: the antecedent can be (1) determinate specific to a number, (2) not determinate? Wait: The text says 'لا يخلو من أن يكون معينًا معلوما، أو لا يكون' so two states: determinate or not determinate. But then it says 'وذلك إما أن يكون له اختصاص بعدد معين... أو لا يكون بل يكون عاما' which seems to expand: either it has specification to a number, or it is general. So that's two possibilities for the antecedent. Then it says 'وكذلك التالي فإنه على ثلاثة أقسام بالضرورة' so the consequent has three divisions. Then it says 'والثلاثة في الثلاثة تسعة' which suggests 3x3=9, but the antecedent has two? Possibly a misprint; perhaps the antecedent also has three? Alternatively, the antecedent perhaps has three: determinate with a specific number, determinate without specific number (ambiguous?), or general? The text may be interpreted as: the antecedent is either determinate (which is further divided into specific number or not?), but the multiplication to nine suggests the antecedent has three possibilities. Nevertheless, the main point: the author concludes that muttasilat and munfasilat each have nine divisions according to this consideration. The student should parse the classification carefully.

The Nine Types of Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates nine types of conditional propositions based on antecedent/consequent determinacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition from Islamic texts into one of nine types based on the determinacy of its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
Conditionals are classified into nine types by a 3x3 grid of antecedent and consequent determinacy.

This lesson breaks down the author's classification of conditional propositions. Understanding these nine types is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic logic that use conditional premises, such as those found in fiqh and kalam. The classification applies to both connected (muttaṣila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila) conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type (connected or disjunctive)
First, determine whether the conditional is connected (if A then B) or disjunctive (either A or B). The classification matrix is identical for both types.
2
Determine antecedent determinacy
Decide if the antecedent (muqaddam) is definite (mu'ayyan), indefinite-specific (munkar khāṣṣ), or indefinite-general (munkar 'āmm). Definite terms are fixed and known (e.g., 'prayer'); indefinite-specific are one of a limited set (e.g., 'either prayer or fasting'); indefinite-general are unspecified (e.g., 'some act of worship').
3
Determine consequent determinacy
Similarly, classify the consequent (tālī) as definite, indefinite-specific, or indefinite-general using the same criteria.
4
Combine to find the type number
Map your antecedent and consequent categories to the 3x3 grid: (definite, definite) = type 1; (definite, indefinite-specific) = type 2; (definite, indefinite-general) = type 3; (indefinite-specific, definite) = type 4; (indefinite-specific, indefinite-specific) = type 5; (indefinite-specific, indefinite-general) = type 6; (indefinite-general, definite) = type 7; (indefinite-general, indefinite-specific) = type 8; (indefinite-general, indefinite-general) = type 9.
The order is: antecedent on rows, consequent on columns.
5
Verify with an example from the text
Check your classification against the author's examples. For instance, 'Whenever man exists, animal exists' has both definite antecedent and consequent, so it is type 1.
The author's examples use generic terms, but the same logic applies to Islamic concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في تقسيم الشرطية الثاني] أما الشرطية فإنها مشتملة على مقدم وتالى متّصلة كانت أو منفصلةً كما مرَّ. والمقدم لا يخلو من أن يكون معينًا معلوما، أو لا يكون؛ وذلك إما أن يكون له اختصاص بعدد معين كأحد الأمرين أو أحد الأمور الثلاثة مثلا، أو لا يكون بل يكون عاما لا اختصاص له بواحد من الأعداد. وكذلك التالي فإنه على ثلاثة أقسام بالضرورة أيضًا، والثلاثة في الثلاثة تسعة فتكون المتصلات على تسعة أقسام بهذا الاعتبار، والمنفصلات كذلك. أما الأول من المتّصلات وهو أن يكون كل واحد من المقدم والتالي معينًا، فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان موجوداً كان الحيوان موجودا. وأما الثاني وهو أن يكون المقدم معينًا والتالي منكرًا خاصًا، فذلك مثل قولنا كلما كان الإنسان موجوداً كان أحدهما، وهو إما الإنسان أو الحيوان موجودًا . وأما الثالث وهو أن يكون المقدم معينا / والتالي منكرًا عاما، فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان موجودا كان واحد من الحيوان موجودا، وأما الرابع وهو أن يكون المقدم منكرا خاصا والتالي معينًا، فذلك مثل قولنا كلما كان الإنسان أو الحيوان موجوداً كان الحسّاس موجودًا. وأما الخامس وهو أن يكون المقدم منكرًا خاصًا والتالي كذلك، فذلك مثل قولنا كلما كان الإنسان أو الحيوان موجوداً كان الحسّاس أو المتحرك بالإرادة موجودًا، وأما السادس وهو أن يكون المقدم منكرًا خاصًا والتالي منكرًا عامًا، فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان أو الحيوان موجودا كان واحد من الجسم النامي موجودا. وأما السابع وهو أن يكون المقدم منكرًا عامًا والتالي معينًا، فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان نوع من الحيوان موجوداً كان الجسم النامي موجودًا، وأما الثامن وهو أن يكون المقدم منكرًا عاما والتالي منكرًا خاصًا، فذلك مثل قولنا كلما كان نوع من الحيوان موجودا كان الحسّاس أو المتحرك بالإرادة موجودًا، وأما التاسع وهو أن يكون كل واحد من المقدم والتالي منكرًا عامًا، فذلك مثل قولنا كلَّما كان نوع من الحيوان موجوداً كان نوع من الجسم النامي موجودًا، ولا يقال إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان نوع الحيوان نوعًا من الجسم النامي، وليس كذلك فإنه يصح إطلاق اسم النوع على نوع النوع على ما عرف. وعلى هذا في المنفصلات؛ إذ الأول منها هو مثل قولنا هذا العدد إما أن يكون زوجًا وإما يكون فردًا. والثاني مثل قولنا هذا العدد إما أن يكون زوجا
Chapter: On the Second Division of the Conditional. As for the conditional, it comprises an antecedent and a consequent, whether it is connected or disjunctive, as has passed. The antecedent cannot but be definite or not definite; and that either has a specification with a definite number, like one of two things or one of three things for example, or it does not, but rather is general, having no specification with one of the numbers. Likewise the consequent, for it is necessarily also of three divisions, and three times three is nine, so the connected conditionals are of nine divisions by this consideration, and the disjunctive conditionals likewise. As for the first of the connected conditionals, which is that each of the antecedent and consequent is definite: that is like our saying 'Whenever man exists, animal exists.' As for the second, which is that the antecedent is definite and the consequent is indefinite-specific: that is like our saying 'Whenever man exists, one of them, either man or animal, exists.' As for the third, which is that the antecedent is definite and the consequent is indefinite-general: that is like our saying 'Whenever man exists, some animal exists.' As for the fourth, which is that the antecedent is indefinite-specific and the consequent is definite: that is like our saying 'Whenever man or animal exists, the sentient exists.' As for the fifth, which is that the antecedent is indefinite-specific and the consequent is likewise: that is like our saying 'Whenever man or animal exists, the sentient or the voluntarily moving exists.' As for the sixth, which is that the antecedent is indefinite-specific and the consequent is indefinite-general: that is like our saying 'Whenever man or animal exists, some growing body exists.' As for the seventh, which is that the antecedent is indefinite-general and the consequent is definite: that is like our saying 'Whenever some species of animal exists, the growing body exists.' As for the eighth, which is that the antecedent is indefinite-general and the consequent is indefinite-specific: that is like our saying 'Whenever some species of animal exists, the sentient or the voluntarily moving exists.' As for the ninth, which is that each of the antecedent and consequent is indefinite-general: that is like our saying 'Whenever some species of animal exists, some species of growing body exists.' And it is not said that it is only so if the species of animal is a species of growing body; it is not so, for the name 'species' is correctly applied to the species of the species, as is known. And similarly for the disjunctive conditionals, for the first of them is like our saying 'This number is either even or odd.' And the second is like our saying 'This number is either even...
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) based on the status of the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) being definite (mu'ayyan), indefinite-specific (munkar khas), or indefinite-general (munkar 'amm). The author begins by noting that every conditional has an antecedent and consequent. The antecedent is either definite (known and specified, e.g., 'man') or not definite; if indefinite, it can be either specific in that it is limited to a definite number of alternatives (like 'one of two things') or general without numerical limitation. Similarly, the consequent has three possibilities: definite, indefinite-specific, or indefinite-general. Multiplying the three states of the antecedent by the three states of the consequent yields nine types for connected conditionals (muttasilat), and likewise for disjunctive conditionals (munfasilat). The author then enumerates all nine types of connected conditionals with examples. For instance, type 1: both antecedent and consequent definite, e.g., 'Whenever man exists, animal exists.' Type 2: antecedent definite, consequent indefinite-specific (e.g., 'one of two'), etc. Type 9: both indefinite-general, e.g., 'Whenever some species of animal exists, some species of growing body exists.' The author also addresses a potential objection: that in type 9, the consequent must be a species of the antecedent; he clarifies that 'species' can refer to a species of a species, so it is valid. Finally, he states that the same classification applies to disjunctive conditionals, providing the first example: 'This number is either even or odd.' This classification is foundational for understanding the logical structure of conditional propositions in Islamic logic. In Islamic legal reasoning, one might analogize: 'Whenever an act is obligatory (definite antecedent), it is rewarded (definite consequent).'

D) Type 9: both indefinite-general.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are systematically classified into nine types by the determinacy of their antecedent and consequent, a scheme that applies to both connected and disjunctive conditionals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Nine Types of Conditional and Disjunctive Propositions
Author enumerates nine types of conditional and nine types of disjunctive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional and disjunctive propositions into one of nine types based on the definiteness of their antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
Nine combinations of definite and indefinite terms cover every conditional and disjunctive proposition.

This passage presents a systematic classification of conditional (متصلات) and disjunctive (منفصلات) propositions into nine types each, determined by whether the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي) are definite (معين), particular indefinite (منكر خاص), or general indefinite (منكر عام). Mastering this taxonomy is key to analysing compound propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Learn the three term categories
The passage uses three types of terms: mu'ayyan (definite, specific), munkar khass (particular indefinite like 'one of two'), and munkar 'amm (general indefinite like 'some animal'). Understand each definition.
These categories correspond to the quantifier-like nature of the terms in compound propositions.
2
List the nine combinations for conditionals
For each of the nine types, note the pair: (muqaddam type, tali type). The passage gives an example for each. Write them down in order from type 1 to type 9.
Type 1: both definite; Type 2: definite + particular indefinite; ... Type 9: both general indefinite.
3
Apply the same pattern to disjunctives
The author states that the same nine combinations apply to disjunctives. He gives examples using number theory (even/odd, prime/composite). Study these and see how the three term categories map.
Even/odd and prime/composite are used as concrete realizations of the indefinite terms.
4
Practise classifying a proposition
Take a conditional proposition from fiqh or kalam and identify which type it belongs to by examining the definiteness of antecedent and consequent.
Use the examples from the passage as templates.
5
Recognize the logical implications
Different types have different logical properties (e.g., necessity, possibility). The passage mentions 'luzum' (necessity) and 'inad' (opposition). Understanding the type helps in syllogistic reasoning.
This classification is foundational for the theory of compound syllogisms.

As for the first of the conditionals, which is that each of the antecedent and consequent is definite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever man exists, animal exists.' As for the second, which is that the antecedent is definite and the consequent is particular indefinite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever man exists, one of them exists, i.e., either man or animal exists.' As for the third, which is that the antecedent is definite and the consequent is general indefinite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever man exists, some animal exists.' As for the fourth, which is that the antecedent is particular indefinite and the consequent is definite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever man or animal exists, the sentient exists.' As for the fifth, which is that the antecedent is particular indefinite and the consequent is likewise, that is like our saying: 'Whenever man or animal exists, the sentient or the volitionally mobile exists.' As for the sixth, which is that the antecedent is particular indefinite and the consequent is general indefinite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever man or animal exists, some growing body exists.' As for the seventh, which is that the antecedent is general indefinite and the consequent is definite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever some species of animal exists, the growing body exists.' As for the eighth, which is that the antecedent is general indefinite and the consequent is particular indefinite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever some species of animal exists, the sentient or the volitionally mobile exists.' As for the ninth, which is that each of the antecedent and consequent is general indefinite, that is like our saying: 'Whenever some species of animal exists, some species of growing body exists.' It is not said that it is only so if the species of animal is a species of growing body; it is not so, for the application of the name 'species' to a sub-species is valid, as is known. And similarly for the disjunctives: the first of them is like our saying: 'This number is either even or odd.' The second is like our saying: 'This number is either even or it is not, but rather is prime or composite odd.' The third is like our saying: 'This number is either even or it is not, but rather is some odd number.' The fourth is like our saying: 'This number is either prime or composite odd, or it is not, but rather is even.' The fifth is like our saying: 'This number is either prime or composite odd, or it is not, but rather is prime or composite even.' The sixth is like our saying: 'This number is either prime or composite even, or it is not, but rather is some even number.' The seventh is like our saying: 'This number is either some even number or it is not, but rather is prime odd.' The eighth is like our saying: 'This number is either some even number or it is not, but rather is prime or composite odd.' The ninth is like our saying: 'This number is either some even number or it is not, but rather is some odd number.' Then prime numbers are those that only one counts, and composite are those that one and another count; and these are among evens like two and four, and among odds like three and nine. And just as a number cannot be even and odd, so too it cannot be prime and composite, so there is opposition between them as there is between the other two, i.e., even and odd. And there is no opposition between a thing and another unless the necessity is realized between it and what contradicts that, and there is no necessity between it and another unless the opposition is realized between it and what contradicts that as well; and this is apparent and will be known later.

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed classification of conditional and disjunctive propositions into nine types each, based on the nature of the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي) as being definite (معين), particular indefinite (منكر خاص), or general indefinite (منكر عام). The author begins with conditionals (متصلات). For each type, he provides a concrete example. The first type has both terms definite; the second has definite antecedent and particular indefinite consequent; the third has definite antecedent and general indefinite consequent; and so on through nine combinations. After completing the conditionals, he states that the same scheme applies to disjunctives (منفصلات) and gives nine analogous examples using number theory (even/odd, prime/composite). The passage then clarifies the definitions of 'prime' and 'composite' in the context of numbers, noting that prime numbers are divisible only by 1 (and themselves), while composite numbers are divisible by 1 and another number. He illustrates with examples: two and four are even primes/composites respectively, three and nine are odd primes/composites. He further explains the concept of opposition (عناد) between even and odd, and between prime and composite, emphasizing that opposition requires a necessary relation (لزوم) between the contradictory terms. The purpose is to provide a rigorous taxonomy that a student of logic must memorize and be able to parse, as it underpins the analysis of compound propositions in syllogistic reasoning.

Third Division of Conditionals: Shared Attributes
Author introduces third division of conditionals based on shared attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the antecedent and consequent of a conditional share an attribute and classify the sharing as unity/multiplicity or other.

Core Idea
Conditionals split into those where parts share something and those where they do not.

This lesson introduces a new way to classify conditional propositions: based on whether the antecedent and consequent share an attribute. This division is crucial for understanding the logical structure of conditionals, especially in legal and theological reasoning where shared attributes often determine the applicability of rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the new criterion
Look at the conditional proposition. Ask: Does the antecedent share any attribute with the consequent? For example, in 'If wine is an intoxicant, then it is prohibited,' both parts involve the concept of intoxication and prohibition. That is sharing.
2
Distinguish sharing types
If they do share, determine whether the sharing is in terms of unity/multiplicity (one or many attributes) or some other aspect (as previously discussed). The text mentions that sharing can be 'بحسب الوحدة والكثرة' (in terms of unity and multiplicity) or 'بحسب الغير' (other).
Unity/multiplicity refers to whether the shared attribute is one or many.
3
Apply to Islamic science examples
Take a conditional from fiqh: 'If the traveler shortens the prayer, then he is allowed to combine prayers.' Here, the antecedent (shortening) and consequent (combining) share the attribute of leniency in travel. The sharing is in terms of unity (one attribute: leniency).

Four Types of Participation in Conditionals
Author classifies participation between antecedent and consequent in conditionals into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the participation between antecedent and consequent in a conditional proposition into four types based on wording and meaning.

Core Idea
A conditional requires antecedent and consequent to share something—wording, meaning, or both.

In this passage, the author explains that every valid conditional must have some participation (musāraka) between its antecedent and consequent. He then divides this participation into four categories based on whether they share wording (lafẓ), meaning (ma‘nā), both, or neither. The last category is controversial, and the passage discusses whether a conditional like "Whenever the sun is shining, the sun is shining" is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement for a conditional
The author states that a conditional only exists if the antecedent participates with the consequent in something. Recognize that this participation is a necessary condition.
2
Extract the fourfold division
The passage lists participation in (a) both wording and meaning, (b) meaning only, (c) wording only, (d) neither wording nor meaning.
3
Examine the problematic case
The case of no participation (d) is illustrated by "Whenever the sun is shining, the sun is shining". Some argue it's invalid, others argue it's valid due to difference in attribute or quantification.
4
Apply the classification
Given a conditional, determine which type of participation it exhibits. For example, "If Zayd is human, then Zayd is animal" shares meaning (both involve Zayd's nature) but different wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إما شجر وحجر وإما إنسان؛ والرابع كقولنا هذا إما إنسان وإما شجر وحجر . ولا يقال لا يمكن أن يكون الشيء إنسانًا وفرسا ولا شجرًا وحجرًا كذلك، فإن الشيء إذا كان مركبًا من الشجر والحجر كان شجرًا وحجرًا. وأما الذي لا يكون أحدهما فيه وهو إما المقدم أو التالي مشاركًا للآخر بوجه ما البتة، فذلك من جملة ما لا يمكن وجوده لا بحسب الذات ولا بحسب الغير. فإن الشرطية لا تكون إلا وأن يكون المقدم فيها مشاركًا للتالي في شيء ما متصلة كانت الشرطية أو منفصلة.
ثم كل قسم من الأقسام المذكورة على أربعة أقسام كذلك باعتبار أن يكون المقدم فيها [أ] لا يخلو من أن يكون مشاركًا للتالي في اللفظ والمعنى جميعا، [ب] أو في المعنى دون اللفظ، [ت] أو في اللفظ دون المعنى، [ث] أو لا يكون مشاركًا لا في اللفظ ولا في المعنى أصلا لكن البعض منها هو من جملة ما فيه من الكلام، وذلك مثل ما يقال كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة كانت الشمس طالعة أو يقال كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النير الأعظم طالعا. فإن من الناس من زعم أنه لا يصح لما أن التغاير بين المقدم والتالي من اللوازم ولا تغاير بينهما في هذه الصورة. ومنهم من زعم أنه يصح فإن التغاير بينهما وإن كان من اللوازم فلا يلزم أن يكون ذلك بحسب الذات، بل قد يكون بحسب الذات وقد يكون بحسب صفة من الصفات، والتغاير في هذه الصورة متحقق بحسب الصفة لما أن المقدم وجوده بالعرض على خلاف التالي. ولأن المقدم منهما في هذه الصورة قضية محصورة في الابتداء، والتالي مهملة؛ وإنه مما يتحقق التغاير بينهما.
This is either stone and tree or human; and the fourth is like our saying 'This is either human or stone and tree.' And it is not said that it is impossible for a thing to be human and horse, nor stone and tree likewise, because if a thing is composed of tree and stone, it is tree and stone. As for that in which neither of the two—that is, the antecedent or consequent—participates with the other in any way whatsoever, that belongs to what cannot exist, neither in essence nor accidentally. For the conditional only occurs when the antecedent in it participates with the consequent in something, whether the conditional is conjunctive or disjunctive. Then each of the mentioned divisions is further divided into four divisions, considering that the antecedent in them [A] does not fall short of being a participant with the consequent in both wording and meaning, [B] or in meaning without wording, [T] or in wording without meaning, [Th] or it does not participate at all, neither in wording nor in meaning. But some of that belongs to what is in speech, such as what is said: 'Whenever the sun is shining, the sun is shining,' or 'Whenever the sun is shining, the greatest luminary is rising.' For some people claim that this is not valid, because the difference between antecedent and consequent is among the necessary concomitants, and there is no difference between them in this form. And some claim that it is valid, for the difference between them, even if it is among the necessary concomitants, does not necessarily entail that it is in respect of essence; rather, it may be in respect of essence or in respect of some attribute, and difference in this form is realized in respect of attribute because the existence of the antecedent in this form is accidental (bi l-'arad) contrary to the consequent. And because the antecedent in this form is a proposition quantified at the beginning, and the consequent is indefinite, and that is how difference between them is realized.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of participation (musāraka) between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) in conditional propositions (sharṭiyya). The author begins by giving examples of conditionals where the antecedent and consequent share components: e.g., 'This is either stone and tree or human' (disjunctive) and 'This is either human or stone and tree.' Then he addresses a potential objection: one might think that if a thing is composed of stone and tree, it can be both, but for indivisible substances like human and horse, they cannot be the same thing. The key point is that in a valid conditional, the antecedent and consequent must share something. If they share nothing at all, the conditional cannot exist—neither essentially nor accidentally. However, the author then introduces a fourfold division of participation based on wording (lafẓ) and meaning (ma‘nā): (a) both wording and meaning, (b) meaning only, (c) wording only, or (d) neither. He notes that the last case (no participation) is controversial, giving the example 'Whenever the sun is shining, the sun is shining' where the antecedent and consequent are identical. Some argue this is invalid because there is no difference (taghāyur) between them, but others, including the author, argue that it is valid because the difference can be in attributes, not in essence. For instance, the antecedent is a quantified proposition ('the sun is shining' as a particular occurrence) while the consequent is indefinite or absolute, or the existence of the antecedent is accidental relative to the consequent. This shows that conditionals require some distinction, even if subtle.

مقدم
تالي
مشاركة

D) The conditional must be conjunctive, not disjunctive.

Key Takeaway
The author's fourfold division of participation (mušāraka) between antecedent and consequent in conditionals is based on shared wording and meaning, and a conditional is only valid if at least one type of participation exists.

The Fourfold Participation of Antecedent and Consequent Parts
Author divides the parts of antecedent and consequent themselves in conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of participation between the parts of the antecedent and consequent in conditional propositions.

Core Idea
Parts of conditionals are not monolithic; they themselves can be from categoricals or conditionals, each with its own participation pattern.

In previous lessons you learned about participation between the antecedent and consequent themselves. Now the author delves deeper: the parts of each side also participate in specific ways. This classification is essential for analyzing complex conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the parallel
The author draws a parallel between the participation of antecedent and consequent (as wholes) and the participation of their parts. Understand that the same fourfold division applies.
2
Identify the origin of parts
The parts can be from categoricals (ḥamliyyāt) or conditionals (sharṭiyyāt); conditionals can be conjunctive or disjunctive.
3
Memorize the four types
The antecedent's parts can share with the consequent's parts in both, in the first only, in the second only, or in neither.
4
Locate examples
Read the Arabic examples the author provides and identify which type each represents.

وكما أن كل واحد من جزأي الشرطية وهما المقدم والتالي لا يخلو من أن يكون مشاركًا للآخر، أو لا يكون فكذلك كل واحد من جزأي المقدم والتالي؛ إذ المقدم لا يخلو من أن يكون مشاركًا للتالي في جزأيه معا، أو في الأول منهما دون الثاني، أو في الثاني دون الأول، أو لا يكون مشاركا أصلا. وتلك الأجزاء قد تكون من أجزاء الحمليـات، وقد تكون من أجزاء الشرطيات وإنها قد تكون من أجزاء المتّصلات، وقد تكون من أجزاء المنفصلات وكل واحد من هذه الثلاثة إما أن يكون من المتصلات، وإما أن يكون من المنفصلات.

Section: On the fourth division of the conditional.
And just as each of the two parts of the conditional, i.e., the antecedent and consequent, cannot but be either participant with the other or not, so too each of the two parts of the antecedent and consequent: for the antecedent cannot but be participant with the consequent in both of its parts, or in the first of them without the second, or in the second without the first, or not participant at all. And those parts may be parts of categoricals (ḥamliyyāt) or parts of conditionals, and they may be parts of conjunctives or parts of disjunctives. And each of these three is either from conjunctives or from disjunctives.
As for when they are from conjunctives [1], the antecedent in the first of them cannot but be participant with the consequent in subject and predicate, or in subject without predicate, or in predicate without subject, or not participant: the first is like our saying 'If every human is an animal, then some human is an animal'; the second like our saying 'If every human is an animal, then every human is sentient'; the third like our saying 'If every human is an animal, then every rational is an animal'; the fourth like 'If every human is an animal, then every rational is sentient.'
[B] Similarly, the antecedent in the second of them cannot but be participant with the consequent in antecedent and consequent, or in antecedent without consequent, or in consequent without antecedent, or not participant: the first is like our saying 'If whenever the sun is shining the day exists, then it may be that when the sun is shining the day exists'; the second like 'If whenever the sun is shining the day exists, then whenever the sun is shining the world is illuminated'; the third like 'If whenever the sun

This passage is a classification of the participation between the parts (ajzā’) of the antecedent and consequent in conditional propositions. The examples involve nested conditionals like 'If whenever the sun is shining the day exists, then...' The passage breaks off, but the pattern is clear: this is a systematic enumeration of all possible combinations of participation between the parts of antecedent and consequent.

Participation When Parts Are from Conjunctive Conditionals: The First Type
Author gives examples of participation when parts are from conjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four subtypes of participation when the antecedent's parts are from conjunctive conditionals and match them to examples.

Core Idea
Even within conjunctive conditionals, the parts can share in subject and predicate, subject only, predicate only, or neither.

The previous lesson established the general fourfold division. Now the author applies it to a specific case: when the parts of the antecedent are themselves from conjunctive conditionals. He provides concrete examples from the stock of logical propositions. This lesson drills those examples.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four types
The antecedent's parts (when part of a conjunctive conditional) can share with the consequent's parts in (1) both subject and predicate, (2) subject only, (3) predicate only, (4) neither.
2
Analyze the first example
'If every human is an animal, then some human is an animal.' Identify subject (human) and predicate (animal) in both antecedent and consequent. Note that both share in subject and predicate.
3
Analyze the second example
'If every human is an animal, then every human is sentient.' Here the subject (human) is shared, but predicate changes (animal vs sentient). So subject-only sharing.
4
Analyze the third example
'If every human is an animal, then every rational is an animal.' Here subject changes (human vs rational), but predicate (animal) is shared. So predicate-only sharing.
5
Analyze the fourth example
'If every human is an animal, then every rational is sentient.' No sharing in subject (human vs rational) or predicate (animal vs sentient). So neither.

Participation in Conditional Propositions
Author classifies participation in conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of participation between antecedent and consequent in conditional propositions and identify an example for each from the passage.

Core Idea
In conditionals, antecedent and consequent can share subject, predicate, both, or neither.

Conditional propositions (sharṭiyya muttaṣila) are the backbone of many arguments in Islamic logic. But not all conditionals are equally informative. This lesson unpacks a crucial classification: how the antecedent and consequent can 'participate' (mushāraka) in terms of their subject and predicate. Understanding this helps you evaluate the logical structure of conditional statements used in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent
In a conditional proposition, locate the part after 'if' (the antecedent) and the part after 'then' (the consequent). In the passage, the first conditional example is 'if whenever the sun rises then day exists, then whenever the world is illuminated, the night is non-existent'.
The antecedent is the whole clause before the second 'then'.
2
Check subject sharing
Determine whether the antecedent and consequent refer to the same subject (mawḍūʿ). For example, in 'if every number is even or odd, then this number is even or odd', the subject 'number' is shared, but the quantifiers differ.
Partial sharing exists when the subject is the same but the predicate differs.
3
Check predicate sharing
Determine whether the antecedent and consequent share the same predicate (maḥmūl). In 'if every number is even or odd, then every number is even or not even', the predicate 'even' appears in both but with negation in the consequent.
Predicate sharing can be exact or through logical equivalents (e.g., 'odd' vs. 'not even').
4
Classify into one of four types
Based on the two checks, place the conditional into one of the four categories: (1) shared subject and predicate, (2) subject only, (3) predicate only, (4) neither. The passage gives examples for each category, though the first three categories of conditionals were started earlier; here the focus is on the fourth and examples for the third type.
The fourth type (neither) is illustrated by 'if every number is even or odd, then every number is divisible into two equal parts or not'.
5
Verify with the passage's examples
Go through the examples provided after [ت] in the Arabic text. For each, identify which of the four participation types applies. The passage uses the number example 'every number is either even or odd' as the antecedent and four different consequents.
The first example shares subject and predicate (though the predicate is rephrased), the second shares subject only, the third shares predicate only (odd vs. not odd), and the fourth shares neither.

[ت] وعلى هذا في الثالث منها؛ إذ الأول كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ والثاني كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن لا يكون زوجا؛ الثالث كقولنا إما كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن لا يكون فردًا وإما أن يكون فردا؛ الرابع كقولنا إما أن يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا وإما أن لا يكون كل عدد إما أن يكون منقسما بقسمين متساويين وإما أن لا يكون.
ثم في هذا المقام يجب أن تعلم بأن المحمول أو التالي إذا لم يكن شاملًا لا وجوده ولا عدمه لكل ما يكون متصفا به، كما في الثالث من القسم الأول مثلا كان التعاند بين المقدم والتالي لازما، وهذا هو أصل مفصل في الباب، ولنقتصر على هذا التقدير فإن الاطلاع عليه مما يطلعك على ما في الغير من الصور من المشاركات بين جزئي المقدم والتالي في سائر الشرطيات.
If the sun rose, then the day would exist; so that whenever the world is illuminated, the day exists; the fourth: if whenever the sun rose the day existed, then whenever the world is illuminated, the night is non-existent. [T] And similarly for the third of them: for the first is like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then this number is either even or odd; the second like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either even or not even; the third like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either not odd or odd; the fourth like our saying: if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either divisible into two equal parts or not. As for when they are from the disjunctives, it is likewise [A]; for the antecedent in the first of the three is not free from being a participant with the consequent in subject and predicate, or in subject without predicate, or in predicate without subject, or not being a participant: as for the first, it is like our saying: either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal; as for the second, like our saying: either every human is an animal or every human is an inanimate; as for the third, like our saying: either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal; as for the fourth, like our saying: either every human is an animal or some sentient being is not sensitive. [B] And likewise the antecedent in the second of them is not free from being a participant with the consequent in antecedent and consequent, or in antecedent without consequent, or in consequent without antecedent, or not being a participant: the first is like our saying: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or it is not the case that whenever the sun rises then day exists; the second like our saying: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the sun rises then night exists; the third: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever night exists then day exists; the fourth: either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the world is illuminated then night exists. [T] And similarly for the third of them; for the first like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is either even or odd; the second like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is either even or not even; the third like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is not odd or odd; the fourth like our saying: either every number is either even or odd, or it is not the case that every number is divisible into two equal parts or not. Then in this place you must know that if the predicate or the consequent is not comprehensive—neither its existence nor its non-existence—for everything that is described by it, as in the third of the first section for example, then the contradiction between the antecedent and consequent is necessary. And this is a detailed principle in this chapter, and let us confine ourselves to this amount, for studying it will inform you about other forms of participation between the parts of the antecedent and consequent in all conditionals.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a technical classification of the types of logical participation (musharaka) between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in conditional (muttasila) propositions, and between the two parts of disjunctive (munfasila) propositions. The author begins by continuing a discussion on conditional propositions from the previous page, presenting four cases for conditionals: the fourth case is illustrated with the example that if whenever the sun rises the day exists, then whenever the world is illuminated the night is non-existent. Then, marked with [ت], the same pattern is applied to the third type using number examples: each number is either even or odd. The first case gives 'if every number is even or odd, then this number is even or odd'; the second gives 'then every number is even or not even'; the third gives 'then every number is not odd or odd'; the fourth gives 'then every number is divisible into two equal parts or not'.

Next, the passage moves to disjunctive propositions (munfasila). Marked with [أ], the author states that the antecedent in the first of the three types cannot escape being a participant with the consequent in one of four ways: (1) in subject and predicate together, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or not every human is an animal'; (2) in subject only, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or every human is inanimate'; (3) in predicate only, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or every inanimate is an animal'; (4) in neither, e.g., 'either every human is an animal or some sentient being is not sensitive'. 

Similarly, for the second type of disjunctive, marked with [ب], the antecedent's participation with the consequent can be: (1) in the whole antecedent and consequent, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or it is not the case that...'; (2) in the antecedent only, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the sun rises then night exists'; (3) in the consequent only, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever night exists then day exists'; (4) in neither, e.g., 'either whenever the sun rises then day exists, or whenever the world is illuminated then night exists'. 

Then for the third type, marked with [ت], the pattern is applied to number propositions: four cases of disjunctives with 'every number is either even or odd' as the first part, and various negations or alterations in the second part.

Finally, the author imparts a key lesson: if the predicate or consequent is not comprehensive in existence or non-existence for everything it describes—as in the third case of the first section—then a necessary contradiction (ta'ānud) arises between the antecedent and consequent. This is a detailed principle in this chapter, and the author limits the discussion to this extent, promising that understanding it will reveal the participation patterns in all conditionals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the conditional 'if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either divisible into two equal parts or not', what is the participation type?
A) Shared subject and predicate
B) Shared subject only
C) Shared predicate only
D) Neither subject nor predicate

Key Takeaway
The participation between antecedent and consequent in a conditional can be in subject only, predicate only, both, or neither; only when both parts share the same subject and predicate is the conditional necessarily true but trivial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conditional 'If the water is pure, then the ablution is valid.' Identify the antecedent and consequent, then determine the type of participation (shared subject? shared predicate? both? neither?).
?
From the passage, choose the example 'if every number is either even or odd, then every number is either even or not even' and state the participation type.
